Having completed for you, my children, this book, which is the last of the medical sciences with its completion, and having reached its end in its expositions and declarations, it seemed to me that I should complete it for you with this treatise, which is a part of the operation, that is, surgery by hand.
Since surgery by hand is languishing in our region and in our time, quite private, perhaps its knowledge has perished, and its trace has been severed; and nothing has remained of it except small descriptions in the books of the ancients, which changing hands have altered; and error and hesitation have befallen them, until its intentions have been closed and its power has been estranged: It seemed to me that I should revive it with the ordering of this treatise, in it, according to the way of exposition, declaration, and abbreviation; and that I should come with the forms of cautery instruments and the other instruments of the work: with the addition of declaration and the expedition of that which is necessary.
However, the reason why a good craftsman of it with his hand is not found in this time of ours is this: because the art of medicine is long, and its author needs to be exercised beforehand in the knowledge of anatomy, as Galen recounted, until he knows the helpings of the limbs, and their forms, and their conjunction, and separation, and has knowledge of the bones, and nerves, and muscles, and their number, and their emergence, and of the pulsating and still veins, and their places of exit.
And because of that Hippocrates said that name with name is much, and with work is little, and especially in the art of the hand.
We however have already spoken of that at the beginning of this book.
Because whoever is not knowledgeable in what we have said about anatomy is not prevented from falling into error, by which people are killed; just as I have seen many of those who are formed in this science, and boast of it without knowledge and without experience.
That is, because I saw a foolish physician cut an abscess of the throat on a woman's neck, and cut some arteries in her neck; therefore the woman's blood flowed, until she fell dead in his hands.
And I saw another physician, who presumed to extract a stone from a man who had advanced in age, and the stone was large. Therefore, he reached it and extracted it with a piece of bladder. Therefore the man died on the third day.
And I myself had already been called to extract it: and I saw from the size of the stone and the disposition of the sick man that he would die.
And I saw another physician, who was receiving payment from a certain Duke of our land for medicine: and it happened to a black eunuch, who was with him, a fracture in his leg near the heel: and the physician hurried with his ignorance, and bound the fracture over the wound with cushions and splints with strong constriction, and did not allow the wound an exit.
Then he discharged him according to his desires. Afterwards he dismissed him for some days, and commanded him not to loosen the bandage: and he stood like that until his leg and foot became ulcerated, and he reached ruin.
I was therefore called to him, and I hurried to loosen the bandage, and there followed tranquility, and he escaped from his pains.
However, the corruption had already become firm in the limb, and I could not restrain it.
Therefore, the corruption ceased to wander in the limb until it perished.
And I saw another physician, who perforated the cancerous ulcer: and it ulcerated after days, until its malice was magnified with its owner.
That is because when cancer is pure from melancholic humour, then it should not be reached with iron at all, unless it is in a limb that can tolerate the complete excision of it.
And for that, my sons, it is necessary for you to know that surgery with the hand is divided into two divisions: namely, surgery to which health is associated, and surgery with which there is danger in many dispositions.
And I have already roused in every place of this book, where surgery comes, in which there is terror and fear.
Therefore it is necessary for you to beware of that, and to diminish it, so that fools may not find a way to speak ill of it.
Therefore, receive the measure with care and protection, and your patients with ease and firmness, and use the better way leading through the grace of God to salvation and praiseworthy succession: and leave the terrible diseases of difficult healing; and remove your souls from that which makes you afraid, so that hesitation may enter upon your faith and your advantage.
For it is more remaining for your glory, and more sublime in this world, and finally to your rewards.
For Galen has already said in some of his admonitions. Do not treat a bad illness, lest you be called bad physicians.
Now I have divided this Treatise into three Chapters.
First Chapter.
Concerning the cautery with fire, and the cautery with sharp medicine, treated and arranged from head to foot, and the forms of instruments and the tools of the cautery, and everything that is necessary in the operation.
Second Chapter.
Concerning incision and perforation, phlebotomy and cupping, and wounds, and the extraction of arrows and similar matters: and everything treated and arranged with the forms of instruments.
Third Chapter.
Concerning restoration, and dislocation, and the treatment of twisting, and similar matters: treated and arranged from head to foot, and the forms of instruments.
But before I recount the operations using it, it is necessary to state the nature of its benefits and harms, and in which temperament it should be applied.
I say therefore, that the discourse on the nature of the cautery's benefit and harm is a lengthy and subtle discourse, requiring knowledge and a hidden secret.
Many wise men have spoken on it, and disagreed; but for me, a little from their words suffices, fearing to prolong the discussion.
I say therefore, that the cautery benefits universally every temperament, whether with or without material, except for the two temperaments which are hot without material, and dry without material.
However, in a hot and dry temperament with matter, they disagreed. For some said that the cautery is beneficial in it. And others said the opposite, namely that the cautery is not suitable for an illness arising from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat; and it is unsuitable for being healed from a hot and dry illness with heat and dryness.
And he who says the opposite stated that through the cautery benefit is achieved in every hot and dry illness that occurs in human bodies, because when you compare the human body and its humors to the temperament of fire, you find the human body to be cold.
And indeed I say, through his discourse, that experience has revealed this to me several times.
However, it should not be applied to it unless he is already proven and skilled in the final exercise of the cautery chapter, and knows the diversity of human temperaments and the disposition of illnesses within themselves, and their causes and their occurrences, and the duration of their time.
However, there is no fear concerning the remaining temperaments, and especially in illnesses that are cold and moist. For all physicians have now agreed upon them, and they have not disagreed in the benefit of cautery in them.
And know, children, that from the secret of treatment by cautery with fire, and its goodness upon cautery with burning medicine, is that fire is simple, whose action does not reach, unless to the limb that is cauterized; and does not harm another limb joined with it, except for small harm.
However, the action of burning medicine reaches that which is elongated from the limbs; and perhaps causes illness of difficult healing, and perhaps kills, in the limb.
But fire, because of its subtlety and the goodness of its substance, does not do that, unless it overflows.
Now, however, that has become known to us through experiment, because of the length of service and study with the art, and the standing upon the truth of things; and because of that, I am excused from the length of speech.
And if it were not so, because it is not permitted to this book of mine, I would present to you the secret hidden in fire, and the quality of its operation on the bodies of its expulsion, by which it expels illnesses with a demonstrative philosophical discourse, which would be sharpened by your minds.
And know, children, that they disagree on the time in which the cautery should be performed, and they set a better time in the spring.
And I say that cautery is appropriate at any time, because the harm that happens because of the time is submerged in the benefit that is drawn with the cautery. And especially if the cautery is for pains from necessary, strong, urgent pains, which do not tolerate delay, because it is feared from them that a greater harm may occur than the small harm entering because of the time.
And do not let it fall into your minds, O children, that the common people and foolish physicians fear that the illness cured by cautery does not always return, and they posit that as a consequence, and the matter is not as they think: for cautery is nothing but a place for medicine, which alters the complexion and dries the humours which are the cause why pains occur.
However, cautery excels medicine in the speed of its cure, and the power of its operation, and the vehemence of its dominion: and it is possible that the illness may return at some time, according to the patient's complexion, and the location of their illness, and its power, and what is prepared in their body from the aggregation of superfluities in it, and what arrives in them by their acquisition from foods, and things similar to these causes, unless it is an illness in which cautery is administered.
A subtle illness in a limb, of few superfluities and humours, like the cautery of a tooth, because of pain, and things similar to it, for it is possible that that pain will not return in it. But that happens in few.
But indeed the common people's saying, that cautery is the last resort in medicine, is a correct saying: not according to what they intend by it; because they believe that there is no cure that can compare to medicine, nor to anything else after the cautery has passed.
However, the reality is the opposite of that, and the intention that cautery is the last resort in medicine is not, except that when we administer methods of treatment for some illnesses, and those medicines do not help; then we administer cautery in the end, and it does help; then from this it follows that cautery is the last resort in medicine, not according to the intention that the common people and many simple-minded physicians understand.
However, the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron; and they did not say that unless because of the equal weight of gold, and the excellence of its substance; and they said that the area of the cautery does not form pus. But that is not absolutely so; because I have already tested it, and found that it does not do that in some bodies without others.
And cautery with it [gold] is good, and better than with iron, as they said. However, when you are heating cautery of gold in fire, it is not clear to you when it is heated according to the quantity you desire, due to the redness of the gold; and when it is quickly cooled, and if you add more heat to it, it melts in the fire, and turns into sheets.
Therefore, the craftsman falls into that occupation [or: predicament] due to that; and because of this, cautery with iron is quicker and closer to us, for the correctness of the procedure.
And I have indeed already arranged this chapter on cautery, according to chapters, and have arranged them from head to foot, so that they alleviate the one who seeks what they want from it.
Chapter II.
This cauterization contributes to the dominion of moisture and coldness over the brain; these two are the causes of headache, and of a multitude of flows of humors from the head to the eyes and ears, and of much sleep, and toothache, and throat pain; and universally every illness that arises from cold, such as paralysis, and headache, epilepsy, and apoplexy, and similar illnesses.
The method of cauterization is, that you instruct the sick person to be evacuated first with a purging medicine, cleansing the head for three or four nights, according to the strength of the sick person, and their age, and their custom.
Then instruct him to shave his head with a razor. Then make him sit between your hands square, so that he has already placed his hands on his chest.
Then place the root of your palm on the root of his nose between his eyes. Wherever therefore your middle finger arrives, mark that place with the cautery.
Then apply the olive-shaped cautery to the fire: whose shape is this.
Then place it upon the place marked with the cautery, with a descent, whereby your hand is gradually tightened, and you will turn it. Then lift your hand quickly, and look at the place.
If you see now exposed from the bone a quantity of alkiel, or a quantity of zeuherbi, then raise your hand. And if not, repeat your hand with the iron itself, or with another from it if it cools: until the bones are seen, which I told you.
Then take some salt, and dissolve it in water, and soak the cotum in it, and place it upon the place: and leave it for three days.
Then place the soaked cotum upon butter, and leave it upon the place, until the eschar of fire is destroyed. Then treat it with tetrapharmacon ointment, until it is healed.
They have now said that the more a wound remains open, the more it emits pus: wherefore it is better and more helpful.
And some of them said that the skin should be cauterized down to the bone: and the cautery should be held, until something of the thickness of bone is burned.
Then that which is burned from the bone should be shaved off, then treated.
And others say that it is necessary to reach the ultimate with the cautery, until it presses an impression on the bone, so that the bone falls, which is done as if shaping a kirate; or an instrument that women place on an infused small alphabet: and they think that vapors of the head are exhaled from this place, and that the wound be left open for a long time.
Then it should be treated until it is healed.
However, these two types of cautery do not seem to me to be suitable except in some people, according to the path of caution.
And to leave it, in my opinion, is better with the safety of him.
For the head is weakened by the disruption of its natural continuity, as we have witnessed in other limbs; and especially when the head of the sick person is naturally weak.  And indeed, the first type of cautery is more safe and better in my opinion, and I use it; therefore operate with it for safety.
Chapter III.
When chronic pain occurs in the whole head, and it is prolonged in the sick person, and he uses leeches, and cupping, and head purges, and oils, and poultices. And especially if it has already been cauterized with the single cautery that we have described, and none of those things helps him, then consider. For if the head of the sick person is of strong natural constitution, and not weak, and finds a violent cold, then cauterize with another cautery slightly beneath the previous one. Then cauterize it above every horn of his head with a cautery, so that the skin is not pierced, and a quantity of bone is uncovered, as we have described. And cauterize it with a single cautery on the back of his head, in the place called the *vas*, which is the place where the cupping glass is placed; and elevate your cautery there, and do not uncover the bone. For the sick person experiences a violent pain because of it, different from the pain of the other cauterizations of all parts of the head.
And I shall say this: The cautery in its place should be the cautery with which the horns of the head are cauterized, and the back of his head should be cauterized with a finer cautery than that used for the middle of the head.
Chapter IV.
When pain and throbbing occur in a part of the head, or the pain extends to the eye, and the head is purged with purifying medicines, and the remaining treatment, which I have described in the divisions of illnesses, is administered, and yet it does not benefit the entire condition: then cauterization is performed in it according to two methods: either by cauterization with a sharp burning medicine, or with iron.
The cautery with sharp burning medicine is to take one garlic clove, and peel it, and cut off its ends from both sides. Then, with a broad lancet, open the painful area for a time, until a space is made beneath the skin, into which the clove is placed.
Then insert it into the space beneath the skin, until it is hidden; then bind it above with a suitable constriction bandage, and leave it for fifteen hours; then remove it, and take out the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days; then apply to it a poultice soaked in butter, until the place produces pus; then treat it with ointment until it heals.
And if you wish, do it with certain burning medicines, which I have written about in the twelfth treatise, on Burning Medicines.
However, its cauterization with iron is according to this method. A cautery of fire, whose form is this.
And it is called a "clauilis," because the shape of its head is like that of a nail, in which there is something curved, and in its middle there is a small eminence.
Then place it upon the area, and hold your hand; and you revolve the iron slowly with speed; and let the amount that is burned be, from the thickness of the skin, like its half. And raise your hand, so that the artery, which is below, is not burned, and a flow of blood occurs.
Then soak the cloth in salt water, and place it over the area, and leave it for three days; then place the cloth with butter; afterwards, treat with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize this hemicrania with the second cautery's knife-edge. And beware of cutting the artery in this hemicrania, not properly an ancient one.
Concerning the method of cauterizing an ancient hemicrania.
When treating hemicrania, with what we have said from the preceding treatment, and what we have said in the divisions of illnesses, and the treatment does not help: and you see from the illness that what we have said of the first cautery with medicine, or with cautery with fire, is not sufficient for it, then it is necessary to heat the cautery knife until it whitens after having marked the place of pain with a line, whose length is half a finger, or something like that; and place your hand on one side, and squeeze it until you cut the artery, and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to beware of the continuation of the jaw, which moves during chewing, lest the side or the nerve which moves it be burned, whence spasm might result.
And be on guard, and cautious about the flow of blood from the artery which has been cut. For in its cutting is the danger, especially for him who does not know what he is doing, and is not studious, nor experienced: and he abandons the operation with it first of all.
And I will come with a reminder of the regimen of blood flow arising from the artery according to its manner, and in its place in this book.
If you see from the illness that this cautery is not sufficient for him, and you see the body of the sick person tolerating the cautery in the middle of the head, as we have narrated, treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery, which we have said in the chapter on the extraction of the artery with the cautery having two knives. For cauterization is better than this and more beneficial.
Concerning the method of cauterization in earache.
When earache occurs from cold, and is treated with remedies, and the other treatment which we have said in the division, and the pain is not removed, then apply a cautery to it: and that is what is called the Point, whose shape is this.
Then, after its ignition, touch the points along the entire circumference of the ear, as it revolves; or, along the circumference of each, if the pain is in both at the same time. And draw back the cautery a little from the root of the ear, after you have marked the places with the marking-ink; and let the amount of the cautery be ten points on each ear, and approximately that.
Then treat the places until they are healed.
Concerning the method of cauterization in torture.
Chapter VII.
Torture that is treated with cauterization is only of the kinds that arise from phlegm, according to what we said in the divisions of illnesses.
And avoid cauterization of the type that arises from desiccation and nerve spasm.
When you treat this type of torture with leeches, and head purgings, and gargles, and your treatment is unsuccessful, then you must cauterize the afflicted with three cauteries: one near the base of the ear, and a second a little lower on its temporal part, and a third at the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the afflicted part. Because softening does not occur unless in the part that appears healthy.
And the form of cauterization is that you cauterize it with a cautery directly at the tip of the upper ear, under the horn of the head a little: and another on the temporal part; whose length should be according to the length of the thumb. And hold your hand with the cautery until a quantity equal to half the thickness of the skin is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a kind of knife, whose form is already described: however, it is not much thinner than that, as you see: and it is necessary that the knife has a little excess thickness in it.
Then treat the places, with what preceded, until they are healed.
Concerning the method of cauterization in old apoplexy.
When apoplexy is longstanding, and you have treated it with what we said, and your treatment does not help, and the patient does not have a fever, then cauterize him with four cauteries on each horn of his head, and with a cautery in the middle of the head, as we said, and with a cautery on the back of the head, according to what preceded: and the method of cauterization should be as before.
Also, cauterization is performed with a cautery on the mouth of the stomach; and this is the more ultimate one.
Then let him be treated with what preceded.
Chapter IX.
It is necessary that large hyerae and pills that cleanse the brain be given first to the sick person in drink.
Then shave the entire head of the sick person, and place on his/her occiput a plaster of mustard, as described in the treatise on plasters; and apply it several times.
For this is the method of cautery; and do it according to the same order that I have said there.
If therefore he is healed by it, it is good. If not, then make three cauteries on the back part of his head, which shall descend from the upper part of the head down to the lower part of the eye; and place the thickness of a finger between each cautery.
Then treat the cautery, with what preceded it.
If however you wish an addition, and the sick person is tolerating it, then make a cautery in the middle. If you wish an addition, then cauterize him above the two horns.
Then treat him until he is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped according to the form that was stated previously.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in paralysis and softening.
Chapter X.
It is necessary that you proceed in the cleansing of the head with hyeres, and with what we have said.
Then shave the head of the sick person; afterwards cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with a cautery on each horn of the head, and with a cautery on its back, and with three on the vertebrae of the neck.
If, however, in the illness there is a need for the softening of the body with more of those, and the sick person is tolerant, and the illness is strong: then cauterize him with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and the last with a cautery, until most of it is burned from the skin, and raise your hand. Then care for him until he is healed; and thus the cautery of Oliuare.
Concerning the method of cauterization in Epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized, unless that one whose epilepsy is from phlegm.
Therefore, it is necessary that you first cleanse his brain with leeches, and with the rest of the treatment that we have described in the division: when the sick person is strong, or is tolerant in the taking of medicines.
However, if it is a child, who does not tolerate medicines, then a gargle and cleansing mastications for the brain are administered, many days before, with the improvement of foods.
Then shave his head, afterwards cauterize him with one cautery in the middle of the head, according to what preceded in the manner: and with another cautery in his occiput, and on every horn of his head with a cautery. If, however, the sick person is strong and tolerant, then cauterize him with the cauteries that I have mentioned for the paralytic, and for the softening of the body on the vertebrae of the neck and vertebrae of the back: and let the olive cautery be in accordance with the manner that preceded.
If, however, the sick person is a child, then apply a subtle cautery, according to this manner.
On the method of cauterization in Melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupted humors and thick phlegm, then cauterize him with the cauteries that we have mentioned for the paralytic.
And if the cause of melancholy is a superfluity flowing towards melancholy, and the sick person's body is moist, then give him a drink that will cleanse his brain, according to what has preceded in the division; then shave the head of the sick person, and afterwards make a neatly crafted bandage of linen, like a circle.
Then place it in the middle of the head, and the patient, sitting upright, must be held on all sides.
Then apply the liniment. And it is better made from old sheep's butter. And heat it over a fire with a moderate amount of heat, which the finger can tolerate when inserted into it. Then apply it to the middle of his head in a circle, and leave it until it cools.
Do this to the sick person once a week, with the rest of his good regimen, until he is healed.
Or if you wish, cauterize it by making many small punctures in it, apart from which you should hold the cautery: indeed, it should be smelling.
For this type of cautery moistens the brain with quality.
Then place over it a cloth soaked in butter, or hen's fat.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in descending water to the eye.
Chapter XIII.
When the beginning of water in the eye is declared to you by the signs which I have said in the division, then begin and treat the afflicted one, with that which will cleanse his head.
And keep him from all humidities, and make him sit in a bath, in fasting for some days.
Then order him to shave his head, and cauterize him in the middle of his head. Then cauterize him above the two temples with two cauteries, if it is the beginning of the descent of water in both eyes simultaneously; or from one side, if it is the beginning of water in one eye; and cut with the cautery all the veins and arteries that are under the skin; and let the cauteries be as long as the width of the temples; and beware of the flow of blood.
If you see anything from him, then cut it off immediately with whatever medicine is possible; and I will come with the skill that is in extracting arteries, and cutting them, and caution from the flow of blood.
And sometimes it is cauterized on the neck, under the two bones, with two cauteries finally applied.
On the method of cauterizing in ancient lachrymal cases.
When the tears of the eyes are chronic, persistent, and located above the veins and arteries that are on the visible exterior of the head, it is ascertained that it arises from cold, phlegmatic superfluities; then cauterize with the same cautery as I described at the beginning for descending water, using one cautery in the middle of the head, and two cauteries on each temple, and two cauteries on the neck beneath the two bones. And if it needs further treatment, then cauterize it on every side of the eye carefully, above the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
On the method of cauterizing in nasal foul odor.
When you treat it, according to what we said in the division, and the treatment is not effective, then act quickly and give the sick person decoctions in a drink for three nights. Afterwards, shave his head and cauterize it with a medium cautery and an olive cautery. Then cauterize it with a claw-shaped cautery, using two cauteries, above the two eyebrows under the hair a little.
And guard against the artery, lest you strike it.
And this is the form of the claw-shaped cautery.
Concerning the method of cauterization in the softening of the eyelid.
When the eyelid of the eye is softened by disease or moisture, then cauterize the eyelid with one cautery, using this crescent-shaped cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize it above the two eyebrows with a few cauteries on every side: and let them be distant from the two temples: and let the length of each cautery be according to the length of the eyebrow: and do not place your hand with the cautery at the very end, but according to the amount that is to be burned of the third eyelid.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the eyelid when the hairs are inverted, pricking the eye.
The cautery is in it according to two methods: either a cautery with fire, or a cautery with a burning medicine.
In the cautery with fire, beforehand, instruct the sick person to remove their own hairs, if they are among those who remove them, until they are elongated and made even. If, however, they prick him at their origin: then bind his eyes with a bandage, so that they do not move, until they are born.
When therefore they are born and made even, then place the head of the sick person in your bosom; then mark on the eyelid of his eyes with ink, a sign that is according to the shape of a myrtle leaf; and let the beginning of the sign be near the hairs.
Then place a cotton ball soaked in egg white, or in psyllium mucilage, on the eye.
Then apply the cautery, whose shape is this.
Then cauterize over the shape that you marked, gradually in many stages, until the surface of the skin, which is like the shape of a myrtle leaf, is completely burned away.
And the sign of the success of your work is that if you see the eyelid already contracting, and the hairs rising near the puncture of the eye.
Therefore raise your hand then, and leave it for three days.
Then place upon it a poultice with butter, until the scab is removed. Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
If any hairs return after an hour, and the eyelid softens, then again cauterize that spot, as you did at first.
If however the hairs are in the lower part, then cauterize it until it is raised to its natural place and is even so that the hairs do not prick the eye.
You should instruct the sick person to let the hairs fall out until they are elongated and even. Make a shape of a myrtle leaf from paper on cloth.
Then take from known soap, and from unsoaked lime, each one ounce in weight, or approximately that; then grind them together thoroughly, and do it quickly so that it does not cool down. Then take some of that from the paste and place it over the paper you made, in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place it over the eyelid of one eye, or two. And place under the eye a poultice soaked in egg white: and let the head of the sick person be in your bosom.
And place your index finger on the medicine, and press it for a short time. And you should move it whenever the sick person feels the medicine's bite: for its bite is found like fire.
As long as he finds that bite, then leave the medicine and move it with your finger. When the bite subsides, then remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And then consider, if you see the eyelid raised, as it is raised upon contraction with fire or with incision, it is well; and if not, then again put medicine upon the place where the medicine does not take hold and does not darken, until your operation is equal and the eye contracts.
Then apply a poultice with butter until the skin of the burn is removed. Then treat it with diaphanous ointment, or another, until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you in the operation, that you guard the final guard, lest something of the medicine fall into the eye.
If the eyelid softens after days, and something of the hair fights in the eye, then again put medicine on the place which softens from the eyelid properly, as you did at first. Then treat it until it is healed.
And this is the form of the myrtle leaf.
And know that the eyes of men are diversified in size and smallness. Therefore according to that let your consideration be: and the way of rectitude is not hidden from him who is diligent in this art.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a tear duct fistula.
When you treat a fistula, if what we have said in the divisions of illnesses does not help your treatment, then you should cauterize it according to this method.
Tell the sick person to place their head in your lap, and let the attendant hold their head in their hands in such a way that it does not move or shake with their head.
Then place a heated Cotum, soaked in the white of an egg or in psyllium mucilage, over their eye.  Then apply the cautery, whose shape is this.
Let it be concave, like the shape of a vulture's wing feather from one end, with which the cautery is made; and if you wish it to penetrate to the other end, and if you wish it to be held upwards in this way.
However, this concave shape is better for your work.
Then express the fistula, if it is open, and draw out the pus from it, and dry it; and if it is not open, then open it and draw out its pus.
Then place the cautery, made very hot, upon it.
And hold your hand with the cautery near the eye, towards the nose, so that your hand does not wander or the sick person is not disturbed; and you shall place the cautery on the fat of the eye, and corrupt it.
If therefore you arrive at the beginning of your cautery up to the opening, it will be good. And if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if you need it, and leave it for three days.
Then apply a poultice with butter, and treat it with a drying ointment until it is healed.
If forty days have passed and it is not healed, then apply to it a sharp corrosive medicine until the opening is exposed, and scrape it according to what comes in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing fistulas, which some of the first ones said. Aim at the location of the fistula, and split it open; then place in the fissure a fine camahan, that is, a fine cannula, whose form is this. And pour into it a drachma of melted lead, and hold your hand with a good retention cannula, and let the sick person not move at all, so that the melted lead does not run or reach his eye.
And it is necessary that you place over the sick person's eye a cloth soaked in egg white, or in water.
For lead burns the location of the fistula, and heals it with a marvelous cure.
If the fistula is cured with what we have said concerning the cautery and treatment, it is good. And if not, then it is necessary to administer a nasal opening, and to reduce the fistula to the course of the nose, according to where it will be in its proper place.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in lip fissures.
Often fissures occur in the lip, which are called hairs, and especially in the lips of children.
Therefore, when you treat these fissures, with what we have said concerning the division of illnesses, and if treatment does not help, then use a small, knife-like cautery with fire, according to this form.
And its concavity should be according to the sharpness of the knife.
Then quickly place the heated cautery on the fissures themselves until the cautery reaches the depth of the fissures.
Then treat it with a cautery, until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing an oral fistula.
When an abscess occurs at the root of the gum, or in the palate, or at the roots of the teeth; then it forms pus, and bursts; afterward it becomes chronic, and pus runs, and a fistula forms; then you treat it, and the treatment is of no avail, then it is necessary to cauterize the fistula with a cautery, according to its size. Then introduce the heated iron into the opening of the fistula, and hold your hand until the hot iron reaches its depth and its furthest point; do it once, or twice.
Then treat it afterward, with what we said about treatment until it is cured.
If therefore the pus is cut off and it heals, it will be good. And if not, then it is necessary to expose the place, and remove the corrupted bone according to what will come to its head.
Concerning the method of cauterization in softened gums and teeth.
When the gum softens because of moisture, and the teeth move, and you treat it with medicines, and it does not help, then place the head of the sick person in your bosom.
Then with fire, a cautery, whose form will come after this, so that you place the Cannula on the tooth: and quickly introduce the heated Cautery into it: and hold your hand for a little while, until the sick person feels the heat of the fire already having reached the root of the tooth.
And lift your hand; then repeat the cautery several times, according to your will.
Then let the sick person fill their mouth with salt water, and hold it for one hour: and let them spit it out.
For the moving tooth is strengthened, and the softened gum is tightened, and the corrupt moisture is dried.
Concerning the method of cauterization in toothache.
When toothache is due to cold, or there is cold or a worm within, and treatment with medicines is of no avail, then a cautery is applied to it in two ways: either a cautery with butter, or a cautery with fire.
However, its cautery with butter is that you should take cow's butter and boil it in an iron spoon, or in a pan. Then take a stick, and wrap it around the tip of the probe. Then immerse it in the boiled butter, and quickly place it on the aching tooth, and hold it until it cools down; then repeat it several times, until the power of fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, immerse wool or a stick in cold butter, and place it on the aching tooth; and place burning iron on top of it, until fire reaches the bottom of the tooth's cavity.
Its cauterization with fire is to apply a copper or iron tube. And let there be something of its thickness, so that the sharpness of the fire does not reach the sick person's bone.
Then apply a cautery, whose shape will come: and place it on that tooth: and hold your hand, until the cautery cools.
Do it several times, for the pain will be removed, either on the same day or on another day.
And it is necessary, in the application of that cautery, that the sick person fill his mouth with good butter, and hold it for an hour: then he should spit it out.
And this is the shape of the cautery.
Cauterize with whichever extremity of force as is possible: and this is the form of the Cannula.
Concerning the manner of cauterization in Strophuls.
When Strophuls are from phlegm and cold humors, and are not obedient to digestion by medicines, and force their digestion quickly: then with a concave Cautery of fire, whose form is this.
Having two open extremities, so that smoke may exit at the cautery from the other extremity; and place it, ignited, upon the abscess once, and a second time, if that is not enough, until it reaches the depth of the abscess.
If the abscess is small, then place the cautery according to the size of the abscess.
Then leave it for three days, and place upon it a poultice infused in butter, until that which the fire burned is removed.
Then treat it with tooth ointment until the form of the cannula is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterization in hoarseness of voice and constriction of breathing.
When humidities overcome the lung's passage, and especially when it is with a cold temperament, then it is necessary that the infirm be evacuated in the first place with loosening medicines.
Then cauterize it at the sore spot near the root of the throat in the depressed place: and be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery, nor the skin, unless something of it.
Then cauterize it with another cautery at the junction of the neck at the last vertebra of it, bringing it to a final end: and let the cautery be narrow according to the manner which preceded.
Then treat it with what preceded until it is healed.
On the method of cauterization in diseases of the Lung and Cough.
When cough and lung disease are from cold humors, and the sick person does not have fever, nor consumption, and the disease is old, then cauterize him with two cauteries above the fork, in low, smooth, empty places. And with another cautery in the middle of the chest between the two breasts: and let the cautery be of a cannula according to the form that preceded.
And if your cauterization wishes to be by puncturing with a cautery, called a Point, whose form preceded in the puncturing of ear pain: and let the points be from thirty points up to almost another.
Then treat him with that which preceded, until he is healed.
And now let the cautery have three branches: according to this form. Therefore, the cauterization is alleviated when you do with it in one turn three cauteries.
Chapter XXVII.
When the head of the humerus is dislocated due to moisture, and does not settle into its position of return from its dislocation until it is accustomed to returning, then, subsequently dislocated by a slight movement, as we have already witnessed, it is necessary to first reduce the dislocation. Then the patient is laid supine on his back, or on his healthy side. Then lift the skin that is within the Titillico towards the top with your left hand: and if the joint is dislocated inwards.
Then take a cautery with two prongs. Whose form is this.
Then cauterize the skin until it penetrates it to the other side, and the figure of the cauterization will come with four cauteries.
And sometimes a cauterization is made with a cautery having three prongs, therefore the figure of the cauterization then is of six cauteries: and the prongs are according to the fineness of the thread.
And this is the form of the cautery having three prongs.
And sometimes one is added above this way, therefore there are eight cauteries.
Then place crushed leeks with salt upon the cautery. Then cause the infirm person to adhere to tranquility, so that the limb is not moved at any time until it is strengthened.
And if the dislocation is upwards, and that rarely happens, then cauterize it above the shoulder with a clavicular cautery, or with many cauteries by pricking.
For the joint is strengthened, and moisture is removed: and the sick person is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the stomach.
When cold and much moisture occur in the stomach, until they draw it out of its complexion, and phlegms multiply within it, and incessant methods of treatment are of no avail, then it is necessary that the sick person be laid on their back, and extend their legs and hands. Then cauterize with three cauteries, one cautery under the breastbone the size of one finger with a [small?] cautery, and two cauteries below on either side of one cautery, until the shape of the cauteries becomes triangular. And lengthen [the cauteries] between each so that they do not join, when they will form pus.
And the depth of the cautery should be the quantity of two-thirds the thickness of the skin.
And let the shape of the cauteries be according to this form without addition; and if you wish to cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this form, then let the cautery with which this is done be one in the shape of a circle according to this form.
And the stomach is cauterized by puncturing it for the one who is terrified by this cautery; and it is as if you mark a point on the stomach according to the quantity that you wish with ink; then cauterize it with the point cautery; then treat it with the treatment which preceded until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing a cold liver.
When pain occurs in the liver from cold and moisture, or from thick wind, until it departs from its natural complexion by the exit of superfluities, and the patient is treated with what we have said, and it does not benefit him: then it is necessary that the patient be placed supine on his neck, and mark three cauteries with ink according to this figure, and this same quantity on the lower liver from the hypochondria, where the man's elbow reaches; and let the space between each cautery be according to the thickness of a finger; and let the cautery be straight according to the length of the body; and do not press your hand with the cautery too much.
And let the quantity of that which is burned be, in thickness of the skin, the quantity of half of it not added; and let the patient be standing on his feet.
If however he is not standing, then let him be lying, having already extended his legs and raised his arms.
This is the form of the cautery.
And it is possible for you, indeed, to cauterize these cauteries with a knife cautery, when there is subtlety and prudence in your skill.
And beware lest you proceed to the utmost in cauterization, and the thickness of the entire skin is burned, wherefore the whole body is burned and it reaches the intestines; for the skin there is delicate, so know that.
Concerning the method of cauterizing liver abscesses.
When an exit occurs, and you wish to know whether the abscess is in the flesh of the liver or in its membrane: then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient is afflicted with pain, and heaviness without sharpness; and if it is in the membrane of the liver, there is intense sharpness with the pain; and you see already that the treatment has wearied his physicians.
Therefore, the patient should lie on their back. Then, mark the location of the abscess with ink; then apply a cautery that resembles a ray. And this is its shape. And cauterize with it one cautery, until the entire skin is burned, and it reaches the siphac, until all the pus comes out; then treat it with the treatment for exits until it is healed.
And it is not fitting that one use this type of cautery, unless one whose study in the art of medicine is prolonged, and through whose hands these illnesses have come together with experience many times; then only should one dare on such an operation.
But truly, to leave it alone is better, in my opinion.
Concerning the method of cauterization in Pleurisy.
The ancients said that cautery with aristolochia roots is for cold pleurisy in this manner. And it is that you should take from dry, long aristolochia roots, one root longer than the others, which is found among them, and let it be as thick as a finger. Then immerse it in oil and light it in the fire. Then cauterize with one cautery in the area between the fork-like junction with the neck and with two small cauteries slightly slanted downward on the area under the chin.
And with two cauteries also on that which is between the fifth and sixth ribs, slanted slightly backward; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest, and another above the stomach. And with three cauteries from behind: one on that which is between the two shoulder blades; and with two cauteries from the two sides of the chest, below the cautery which is between the two shoulder blades.
And you should not press your hand with the cautery, but rather it should be on the skin in plain view, smelling.
And some ancients have said that there are men who use a cautery made of iron similar to a rod: and they ignite it, and insert it into that which is between the ribs, until they reach the abscess with it, and pus emerges: as we have said regarding the abscess of the liver.
But in this perforation with the cautery there is fear that either the weak man dies immediately, or a fistula occurs in the place, with no cure.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the Spleen, Chapter XXXII.
When you treat the illness of the spleen, with what we have said concerning the treatment in the division of illnesses, and your treatment does not help, then the cautery is to be applied in three ways, all of which are correct.
One of these is to make three cauteries, or four, drawing them along the length of the spleen according to the pattern of the liver cauteries, whose figure preceded.
And let there be between each cautery a distance of the width of a finger, or a little more or less.
And let the method of the cautery be as we described for the cautery of the liver, equally.
And do not press your hand with the cautery, and let the infirm person lie supine on their back.
And another method of cauterization is this: that you heat a cautery having two prongs, as we described in the chapter on the dislocation of the humerus, and raise the skin, which is in a direct line with the spleen, where the left arm of the sick person reaches.  And let your raising of the skin be according to the width of the body, so that the cauteries fall along the length of the body.
Then insert the two very hot prongs until with them you penetrate the skin on the other side, then withdraw the cautery; thus four cauteries are made.
And if you wish to cauterize with another cautery having three prongs.
Then treat the cautery after you have let it emit pus for many days, for it is more beneficial than all the other preceding treatments.
Concerning the method of cauterization in dropsy.
The cautery does not fall upon dropsy unless properly applied.
When you treat a dropsical patient with the methods of treatment, which we have mentioned in the division, and the treatment does not benefit, then it is necessary to cauterize him with four cauteries around the navel, and one cautery above the stomach, and another cautery above the liver, and a cautery above the spleen, and two cauteries behind the back between the vertebrae, and one directly on the chest, and another directly on the stomach: and let the depth of the cautery be close to the thickness of the skin.
Then leave the cautery open, so that it may discharge pus for a long time.
And do not remove the sick person from treatment after the cautery with him: for it is necessary that two intentions be gathered in him, and healing will hasten to him with God's help.
The form of the cautery with which the belly is cauterized should be nail-shaped, according to its previously stated form; and that with which the back is cauterized should be olive-shaped, according to its previously stated form.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the feet and legs.
When abscesses occur in a hydrops patient, and they are filled with yellow water, it is necessary to cauterize on the back of the foot, in the space between the auricular and annular bones, and lift your hand with the cautery, and do not twist it; then raise your hand, and do not repeat it at all.
For the yellow water will sweat out, and the cautery should be of this shape.
Then make two cauteries on each leg, and let the cautery be made with the knife-like extremity of the cautery; and let the cautery be made along the length of the leg, one below the knee, and the other below that, approximately in the middle of the leg; and make two cauteries above each thigh; and leave the cautery open without treatment for a long time, so that some water will sweat out from it; then treat it with the other things we have mentioned.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in abdominal distension.
Chapter XXXV.
When the distension of the belly is from cold and dampness, until it weakens the retentive and digestive powers which are in the stomach and intestines; and it is treated with the methods of treatment, and is not healed; and you see the sick person tolerating a cautery of exuberant power, then cauterize him with a large cautery above his stomach, according to what preceded in the cauterization of the stomach with a circular cautery, and four fine cauteries in the circumference of the navel, with a fine clavi-shaped cautery; and a large cautery above the hypochondrium, or two cauteries.
And if you see the dampness excessive, and the sick person tolerating it, then cauterize him with a cautery above the breastbone, and with a cautery above each flank; and perhaps you will add two small cauteries to the stomach near the large one.
For it is a beneficial treatment, whose aid does not err.
Concerning the method of cauterizing Hemorrhoids.
Chapter XXXVI.
When in the anus are many old hemorrhoids, or one, and they are from thick, cold humors, or corrupted dampness, and are treated with what we have said in the division, and the treatment does not help: then cauterize with three cauteries above the lower spine of the back a little below the navel triangularly, and with a cautery below the navel as if with two fingers.
And if you think that his stomach is chilled, and his food is not digested, and you see his face become ulcerated, then cauterize him on the stomach with a large cautery, as previously described; and cauterize him on his liver, and another on his spleen, with a key-shaped cautery; and leave the cautery open for a time; then treat him until he is healed.
Chapter XXXVII.
When you excise althelul, then heat a cautery that resembles a ray. Then insert it, heated, into the excised althelul; and with the last part of your hand, until the cautery reaches the opening of the vein from which blood flows: do it once, or twice.
If therefore the althelul are many, then cauterize each one with the cautery according to what we have recounted. Then treat them with that which benefits them from ointments, until they are healed.
And if you cauterize it with a large cautery upon the alcatin, it is more effective in helping.
Concerning the method of cauterizing a fistula in the anus.
Chapter Thirty-eight.
When the sick person does not obey the incision and work that we have described in its place, and is frightened by it, perhaps he is cured with a cautery.
When therefore a fistula occurs to someone, and becomes old, pus runs from it, and corrupted humours. Therefore it is more fitting that you should probe it with a fine probe. Then ascertain its depth with the probe.
Then heat the cautery, which is similar to a ray. Then introduce the heated cautery into the fistula itself, and note the amount by which it has penetrated.
And repeatedly apply the cautery to it, until all those corrupt bodies are burned, once, or twice, or three times, according to your necessary amount.
And guard yourself from burning the nerve, if there is one there, or a large vein; or if the fistula leads to the bladder, or to the intestine, then guard yourself from these places altogether.
And do not do this unless the fistula is in a fleshy place, and you judge that it is not penetrating.
Then treat the place until it is healed.
If however the place is sealed, and the matter is cut away from it, and remains like that for a long time, then know that it is now completely healed.
And if matter is not cut away from it, then know that it is penetrating, or a corrupted bone is deep within it, or something like that from those things: whose discussion will come in its place.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the kidneys.
When pain in the kidneys occurs from cold or from thick wind, and the strength of the sufferer is diminished by it, then it is necessary to cauterize it on the almethen over the kidneys, cauterizing over each kidney with a cautery iron, the description of which has preceded.
And perhaps we cauterize it a second time on the almeidatu itself. Therefore three lines will come. Therefore the last will be more helpful.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the bladder.
When weakness and softening of the bladder occur due to cold and dampness, to the point that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize it on the lower part of the summit above the bladder, where the hairs of the pubic bone begin.  Cauterize with one cautery from the right side of the summit, and another from the left: and let the length of this cauterization on each side be according to the width of a thumb's breadth. And cauterize with a lower dorsal cautery, or with two cauteries if that is necessary: and let it be as described previously (or according to what preceded) for the cautery's grip.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the uterus.
When cold and dampness affect the uterus, hindering conception, corrupting her menses, delaying their expulsion, and causing pain upon their arrival, then it is necessary to cauterize with three cauteries in a circle around the navel, as we described in the cauterization of the bladder, and with a cautery above on the lower back, or with two cauteries: and let the cautery's grip be as before.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the rectum in dislocation.
When mucous humors are discharged into the rectum, and cause its exit from its proper place; and a sign of this is that one leg is extended over the other when they are compared, and the location of the dislocation is found empty there.
Therefore, it is necessary to cauterize the affected area over the rectum itself with a similar circular cautery; after marking the circumference of the rectum with ink, as it revolves; and the rectum itself should fall in the middle of the circle.
If this cautery is not available to you, then cauterize it with three cauteries, with a large olive-shaped cautery; and let the depth of the cauterization be according to the thickness of the entire skin.
Then treat it until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterization in Sciatica.
When pain occurs in the hip socket, and its cause is cold and moisture; and the patient is treated with what we have said in the division, and the treatment does not help, and it becomes chronic: then it is necessary to free the patient from the thick humors with foul pills, or with artificial pills, and similar ones. Then cauterize it.
And the method of cauterization in it is according to two methods: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire.
Cauterization with fire is according to many methods.
One of which is to cauterize above the hip socket itself with three triangular cauteries, according to this form. And make them a good, or bad depth. And let the space that is between each cautery be the quantity of the thickness of a finger: and let the cautery be olive-shaped.
Sometimes however a cautery is made in the middle above the hip itself. Therefore there are four cauteries.
And if you wish to cauterize with a circle, whose form preceded in the cauterization of the dislocation of the hip, one: so that the circle contains the entire hip.
And burn the thickness of the entire skin.
This is the method of cautery in sciatica, when the pain is adhering to the hip itself, and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
However, if it extends to the thigh and leg, then cauterize it with two cauteries upon the thigh, upon the place to which the sick person there points, where the pain is, and with your cautery upon the calf four fingers towards the wild side for a short time: and let the cautery be a knife-cautery.
And let the depth of the cautery be according to the depth of the thickness of the skin.
If the sick person indicates that the pain extends up to near the toes of the foot, then cauterize it there where he indicates with a point cautery, three, or four, or more, if it needs them.
And if he indicates the pain to be below the knee towards the leg, then cauterize it there with one knife cautery.
And beware in all your cauterizations, that you do not finally use the cautery in such a way that you burn a nerve or a large artery, and thereby draw upon the sick person an evil harm or contraction.
And indeed I have already seen one and another, of those who have cauterized above the ankle and have been too thorough in the cauterization, and the leg has swollen until the swelling reached the foot, and the entire foot has been perforated and corrupted; then a dissolution of the belly followed, and death after that.
If the pain is in two parts at the same time, cauterize each according to this method.
And certain wise men said in the cauterization of the buttock, this is the method of the cautery. Make a similar cautery of iron, whose diameter is half a palm: and let it be at its opening according to the thickness of a date stone, or a little less: and within that cautery let there be a third cautery.
And let the space that is between each cautery be the size of a thumb's knot; and let the cauteries be open on both sides; and let their height be like one knot, or two knots; and make a handle for them of iron, fitting well into the cauteries.
Then let it be heated in fire, until it glows red and throws off sparks; then let it be placed over the buttock of the sick person lying on their healthy side. Therefore cauterize him with three round cauteries at one time.
Then leave him for three days. Afterwards, plaster with butter; and leave the wound open for many days. Then treat him with ointment, until he is healed.
Albucasis says, "By this sort of cautery, my sons, we have used little, because of the fear of its appearance; and because we found few who would wait upon it.
However, it is a good cautery for the one who suffers, and finds its place with him.
A cautery, however, is with burning medicines, so that you make two samia having a pierced armlet, into which descends the hinge of its door, of bronze or iron, the length of a wall: on whose height there are two knots, or like them.
And let the space that is between each be the width of a finger, and let them be open below and above: and let that contain each other, according to this form.
Then place it upon a box of ashes, the sick person lying on his healthy side. And press your hand upon the box of ashes strongly. Then pour between the two circles hot water, which has been heated with fire for a short time, and hold it for a quantity of an hour: and let the sick person tolerate its burning and its cauterization, for he finds burning as if it were fire, until the burning subsides.
Then raise your hand with the instrument, and wash away the hot water from the whole ash with fresh water: then leave it for three days.
And place butter upon it until the blackness of the cauterization is removed: and leave it for several days, and pus will run from it. For it is the final remedy.
Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
If the pain extends to the hip or leg, make a poultice on it containing water, just as you did for the ash equally.
And this is the method for hot water; and I have already written it in the treatise on the rectification of medicines. Take alkali and quicklime, each one part; grind both, and put them in a new pot whose lower part has a small hole, according to the quantity that the radius will enter; and place another glass pot beneath the bottom of the pot; and pour over the alkali and lime sweet water, which submerges both by a finger; after you have compressed them with your hand with good compression; and leave the pot until the sharp water descends to the lower part of the glass pot.
Then collect all that water and pour it over other lime; and strain it until it is renewed.
For then it will be very strong and sharp, which is used in many surgical operations and in cauterizing other limbs. Because it produces the operation of fire itself.
And among the medicines with which the anca is cauterized, are also like tesisie, and honey of anacard, and lime with soap, so that both are mixed together.
And Galen, indeed, narrating, recounted the cure of a certain man from the ancients, with which the pain of the anca and the sciatic vein were cured; and he magnified it to be very great. And he said that he did not need any other treatment, and that he was healed in one instance, until he recounted that perhaps he entered a bath carried, and emerged from it already healthy.
And it is to be taken from green seitaragi; and if green is not found, let dry, fresh material be taken; and let it be rubbed much with some fat, and placed upon the anca, where the pain is, or the leg, or in the thigh; and let it be bound, and let it be left for the amount of three hours, or for the amount that the sick person feels the sedation of the burning. Then put him into a bath.
When therefore his body becomes moist, put him on a couch. For the pain recedes, and he is healed.
And know that it is not necessary for anyone to use a cautery with these remedies, unless after the evacuation of the body.
And Dioscorides, indeed, said that the dung of goats, when a sciatic vein is cauterized with it, helps it: and the cautery is done in this way. Take wool, and soak it in old oil, and place it over the deep place, which is between the big toe and the heel, and is closer to the heel.
Then take dry goat dung, and inflame it in fire, until it becomes charcoal.
Then place it upon the wool, and leave it until it is extinguished. Then repeat it. Therefore do not cease to do this, until sensation reaches the middle up to the ankle, and the pain ceases.
And this method of cautery is called the Vaccine cautery.
Concerning the cauterization of back pain.
Sometimes pain in the back occurs due to many causes, either from an accident, or a blow, or an evacuation of excess fluids, and similar things, from the infusion of cold and damp matter: and indeed the cautery does not apply except in this single instance, which occurs from the infusion of cold matter.
Therefore, after the patient's evacuation with foul pills and similar remedies, three lines should be made on the back, where the pain is, according to the width of the afflicted area itself.  After marking each line with five cauteries, or more depending on the patient's tolerance and the severity of the case, apply the cauterization using a cautery-pointed instrument.
And if you wish, cauterize with three or four cauteries, using a cautery with a medial point, following this form.
Concerning the method of cauterization in the early stages of a hump.
This illness often occurs in young infants.
And the sign of its beginning in children is that there is a constriction in their breathing upon elevation and movement; and you will find at the end of the thoracic spine and its joints that it has already begun to project beyond the other vertebrae.
Therefore, when you see it, and wish to treat it, then cauterize it with a cautery that is circular in this form.
And let the cautery cover the entire part of the vertebra equally; and if you wish to cauterize the circumference of the vertebra with a pointed cautery, make two or three lines; and let the points be close together.
Then treat the place until it is healed with what we have said.
And beware, that you do not apply the cautery to the hump that arises from nerve spasm.
Concerning the method of cauterization in gout and joint pains.
When the pains in the joints are from cold humours which flow to any part of the body, then when such pains occur in the feet, it is the custom of physicians to properly name it gout.
Therefore, when gout of a cold cause is treated with methods of treatment, and the pains are not removed, then the cautery will remove them.
And it is to cauterize it, after evacuation, around the joint of the feet, with many cauteries: and let the cautery be oily.
And if you need to puncture the surface of the foot, then do so with a pointed cautery. If the pains ascend to the knees, or other joints, and this often happens, then cauterize it above each knee with three or four cauteries, from every side, with this same cautery, the Claviform.
If, however, more cauteries are necessary for you, then cauterize it. And do not immerse your hand with the cautery, rather let it be about the thickness of the skin only.
If, however, the pains ascend to the knees and back, then administer what we said about cautery in its chapter.
If the pains are only in the hands, then puncture in the circumference of the joints, like two lines revolving.
If, however, pain remains in the fingers, then puncture them, over every knot a puncture, and over the palm of the hand.
If, however, the pains ascend after days to the elbows, or to the shoulders, then cauterize both from every part.
And do not remove the sick person from good regimen, from the taking of medicines. For if a good regimen is made, and phlegm is evacuated, it will be healed with the help of God with this cautery.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in Ruptures.
When a rupture occurs in the groins, and the intestine, or the spermatic cord, descends toward the testicle, and it is in its early stages and close: then it is necessary to order the sick person to abandon his eating on his particular day. And let him use laxatives, so that the stool descends from his belly.
Then let him sit between your hands upon his back; and order him to hold his breath until it emerges, or the spermatic cord, or the intestine appears.
Then reduce it with your fingers, and mark with ink under the rupture above the pubic bone, with a mark whose extremity is similar to half a circle towards the upper part of the body.
Then cauterize with an iron cautery, of which this is the shape, until it turns white and throws off sparks. Then reduce the intestine, or spermatic cord, to its hollow. Then let the attendant place his hand over the area so that the intestine does not come out; and you have now opened between the patient's legs, and placed a cushion beneath him, and another servant on his thighs, and another on his chest who holds his hands.
Then place the cautery on the mark itself, with your hand holding the cautery straight. And hold it until you reach the mouth with it; or repeat with it another time, if you have not reached the mouth with it in the first instance.
And prudently observe for the exit of the intestine during your cauterization, lest you burn it, and death or great harm befall the patient through it.
And know that when you do not reach the mouth with the cautery, your operation is not effective.
And the cautery for infants should be fine, according to their size.
Then treat with the other ointments until it heals.
And let the patient lie on his back for forty days, until the wound is sealed.
And it is necessary that you place nourishment for the duration of his healing, which will soothe his stomach, so that the intestines do not expel during straining and defecation.
Then, when he wishes to rise after forty days, then administer a properly made bandage, and let him keep it for another forty days.
And let him be lessened in labor, and in filling of food and drink, and in loud voices.
For when he uses this regimen, he is thus healed with complete healing.
And I will speak of the treatment of Ruptures with incision in his chapter.
However, when Ruptures occur in the rest of the belly, and are beginning, and force so that they are not added to, then cauterize the Rupture with a round cautery according to its size: and let that which is burned be from then as its third part. Then treat him with what we have said: and it will not be added to.
And already some first ones have said that a Rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery, according to this shape.
After it is marked with ink, and a line is placed which is across in the upper side of the Rupture, and another line to the lower. And place your hand in the middle with the cautery iron.
But the first cautery is easier and better.
Concerning the method of cauterization in pains due to a fall or blow.
Chapter XLVIII.
When in certain limbs pain arises from a fall or blow, and that pain persists for a long time, and treatment with medicines is not effective, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery iron.
And let the cautery of every limb be according to its size, and its smallness, and its weakness, and its strength, and the location of the twisting, and the pain from it.
If therefore it is healed by the first cautery, it is well. If not, then repeat the cautery on it.
Because it is customary for these pains to shift from one limb to that which is near it. Therefore it is necessary to follow them with cautery until the sick person is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in Leprosy.
Leprosy patients are sometimes greatly helped by cautery. And especially the species of leprosy that arises from the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy: therefore, when you wish to cauterize it, consider it.
Therefore, if the leprosy is extensive, and you have treated it with what we said in the division, and it neither recedes nor stops; and you fear that the corruption may encompass the entirety of his constitution; then cauterize him on the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the noted head, and a second below it towards the forehead near the end of the hair, and two above the two horns, and one behind on the nape of the neck.
And hold your hand with the cautery for a short while, until it imprints a small impression on the bone, and the tissues are loosened from it: so that the expiration of gross vapors from it may be relieved; and cauterize also with the cautery upon the spleen itself, according to what has preceded.
However, if the leprosy has already spread over the patient, and is clearly visible, then it is necessary to cauterize with those cauteries that we have mentioned on the head, and with a cautery on the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries on the two jawbones, and with two cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck, and six on the vertebrae of the back, and one large one on the haunches near the end of the tail and one above it, in the very flesh of the buttocks; and two on the armpits: namely, one on each armpit, and two on the knees: one on each knee, and two on the shoulders, and two on the elbows, and two on the soft parts of his chest; and cauterize him on every joint, from the joints of his fingers and his toes; and cauterize on every heel of his feet, and the soles of his hands.
And beware of the nerves that are upon the heel, so that you do not burn them.
And sometimes a cautery is applied to the breastbone, and another to the stomach, and another to the liver.
And know that the more you apply the cautery, the more beneficial and helpful it is.
And know that the sick person does not find pain in the cautery as healthy people do, because his body is already numb.
And there should be cauteries of different sizes, according to the limbs and joints, just as was stated previously in the kinds of instruments.
Then treat the cautery with herb flour and honey, and with other treatments, until it heals.
Concerning the method of cauterization in numbness.
When any limb is numb, and is treated with medicines, and oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then cauterize that numb limb with cauteries, according to the deserved size of the limb, or its smallness.
And let your cautery enter the thickness of the skin a little. Then treat it with ointment, until it is healed.
And sometimes a cautery is applied to something of numbness that happens to the hand and foot, in the vertebrae of the back near the exits of the nerves that move that limb; and the numbness is removed.
But let no one presume upon it, unless he is wise in the anatomy of the limbs and the exit of the nerves moving the body.
Chapter LI.
When Albaras is old, and nothing from the skills of medicine helps it, then cauterize over it itself with a deep cautery for a short time, according to the thickness of the skin, until its whiteness is removed and its color changes.
Then treat it with flour of herbs, and lentils with rose oil, and arnogloss leaves, and dove's blood, and swallow's blood: of each one part; mix all these together, and apply them on a cloth, and let them adhere to the place, until it heals.
Concerning the method of cauterization in Cancer.
When Cancer is beginning, and you wish to stop it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it revolves.
However, the wise have said that it should be cauterized with the final cautery in the middle.
However, it does not seem right to me, because I fear that it will ulcerate; and I have already seen this happen several times.
And it is better that it be cauterized around it with a circle, as we have said; or with many cauteries.
Concerning the method of cauterization in Adubelati.
When someone has Adubelati, and it is delayed in maturation, either because of excess causing it, or due to the infirm's age, when he is decrepit with little blood, or due to the time, and the force to hasten the maturation of Adubelati, then cauterize around it with many small cauteries by puncturing. Then leave it. For it will hasten with maturation.
If however you wish to perforate it with a cautery, then heat the cautery, whose form is this.
And place it in the middle of Adubelati, until it penetrates the skin; and let the cautery be from that which follows the infirm, so that the course of pus may be relieved.
Then treat it until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing a corrosive evil.
Chapter LIII.
A corrosive is nothing but corruption walking in a member, and it corrodes it; just as fire consumes dry wood.
If therefore you see a corrosive in a place that can tolerate a cautery with fire, then use many cauteries of fire, small and large, according to what is suitable for the place where the corrosive is.
Then cauterize it from every side until you eradicate the entire corruption, and nothing of it remains at all.
Then leave it for three days, and put ground sulphur with oil on the cauterized places, until all the eschars and all corruption are eradicated.
Then treat it with ointments that generate flesh.
If therefore you see that after three days healthy flesh is born in a way where there is no corruption, it is good.
And if not, then repeat the cautery on that which remains of the corrupted places.
And it is sometimes cured by corrosive medicine. For it itself arises in place of the cautery.
However, cautery with fire is of quicker help; and I have already said that its cauterization with corrosive medicine is in division: so take it there when you need it.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in warts, and corns.
Chapter LV.
This ailment often occurs in the lower parts of the feet: and it is a thick, filtered substance that hurts the foot.
And cautery in them is indeed according to two methods: cautery with fire, and cautery with hot water.
As for cautery with fire, you should make a concave cautery, similar to a hawk's wing, made of iron, in a size that completely contains the wart on all sides, and with thin edges.
Then place it, heated, over the wart. Then revolve the hand with the cautery in a circular motion around the wart, until the cautery reaches the depth of the wart, and leave it for three days, until it begins to emit pus. Then plaster it with crushed wild mallows and salt, and leave the plaster over it overnight. For it will be eradicated from its roots.
Then treat the area with a generative flesh ointment, until it heals.
If they are not converted, and often occur on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet, then apply a cautery to them with warm water.  And take a cannula of bronze, or iron, or of a vulture's wing, and place it on the wart, or corn.
