Then pour a small amount of hot water into the cannula, hold your hand, and rotate it with the pressure of your hand for a short time, so that the edge of the cannula presses on the root of the wart, and the hot water finds its way to the depth, to the root of the wart; and the sick person should wait for a while on the mordication of the hot water for an hour.
Then leave it, for the wart is eradicated with its roots.
Do this with each of them one by one, until you have treated all those that are on the body.
Then treat them after they are eradicated, with that which produces flesh from ointments.
Concerning the method of cauterization in Tremor.
When tremor occurs in someone due to cold in a nerve, or from a quartan fever, or otherwise: then it is necessary to cauterize it with four cauteries, or five, over the vertebrae of the back, and between each vertebra with a cautery, and on its chest with a cautery, and on its stomach with a cautery.
For tremor subsides with the cautery of olives, and the health of the cold ailment speeds.
Concerning the method of cauterizing Albathir.
Chapter LVII.
Sometimes foul Albathir are expelled in the body, that is, pustules, which are made from corrupted gross matter.
Therefore, it is necessary, first, when they appear, that all pustules on every head be cauterized with a fine cautery of myrtlewood, whose tip has been ignited with fire. Or with the root of long aristolochia. Or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes they are cauterized at the beginning of their expulsion in this way: and they are not increased; and the superfluity which makes them is broken, and they are healed from them.
However, it is necessary that this be done after evacuation with bloodletting.
Concerning the method of cauterization in a blood flow from an artery in a wound.
Often a flow of blood occurs from an artery that has been cut in a wound that occurs externally, or at the incision of an abscess, or cauterization of a limb, and things similar to them: and it is difficult to cut it off.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then quickly apply your index finger to the opening of the artery, and press it firmly, until the blood is contained under your finger and no more comes out from it.
Then place small, olive-shaped cauteries, and many large ones, in the fire: and blow over them, until they become very hot.
Then take one of them, either small or large, according to the wound and place where the artery is broken, and place the cautery on the vein itself, after you quickly remove your finger; and hold the cautery until the blood is cut off.
If it is driven out when you lift your finger from the opening of the artery, and extinguish the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from the cauteries that are prepared in the fire.
Therefore, do not cease to do this, one after the other, until the blood is cut off.
And beware, lest you burn the nerve which is there, and cause another harm to the sick person.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, it is then not possible to cut it off, especially when the artery is large, unless by one of the four methods. Or with a cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction, when it is not extracted. For when it is extracted, its extremities contract, and the blood is cut off. Or it should be tied with a strong ligature. Or medicines of a nature to cut off the blood should be placed on it, and it should be bound with a fitting constriction of a pillow.
However, he who performs its excision with a ligature and constriction with a cloth, or with the application of burning things and similar methods, does not truly benefit it, except rarely.
If, however, this should happen to someone, and a physician, nor medicine, be present for him, then let him begin with the placement of the index finger on the opening of the vein itself, as we have described, and with its strong constriction, until the blood is blocked from the upper part of the wound, and above the artery. And the finger should not be removed, above it with very cold water, continually, until the blood is congealed and thickened, and cut off.
And meanwhile, let him consider what it needs in terms of cautery or medicine.
Concerning incision, perforation, and phlebotomy in wounds, and similar matters.
We have already stated in the first chapter that every illness in which cautery with fire and burning medicine is appropriate, and the occasions for it, its causes, its instruments, and the forms of cauteries.
And I have placed there chapters from the head to the foot.
And I shall proceed in this chapter along the same path, so that the seeker may have his question relieved.
And before I begin that, it is necessary that you, my children, learn that in this chapter is the terror that is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And therefore it is necessary that there be more caution in it, because in the operation which is in this chapter, evacuation with blood frequently occurs, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or the incision over an abscess, or the opening of an exit, or the treatment of a wound, or the extraction of an arrow, or the incision over a stone, and similar things: from all of which trepidation and fear are associated, and death falls in many of them.
And I warn you, lest that which causes hesitation befall you, for sometimes in this art the ways of men clash with the ways of illnesses. For among them is he who is angered by his illness, and death is light to him; because of the intensity he finds in his illness and the length of his malice. And with illness is the fear that signifies death.
And among them is he who gives his lyre, and enriches you with it, hoping for health, and his illness is harmful.
Therefore, you should not agree with him who comes to you from among them, whose manner is such. And let your caution be stronger than your greed and your rapacity. And do not presume upon anything from it, unless after certain knowledge, which verifies to you that to which praiseworthy succession has arrived.
And use in the treatment of all your sick, prognostication and indication of that to which health may suitably come.
For in that is help for you in acquiring fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire you, children, with his understanding, and may he not take away from you direction and inspiration; for it is in his hand.
And I, indeed, have arranged this chapter, distinguishing it by chapters, according to what preceded in the chapter on the cautery, from the neck up to the feet: so that your inquiry, which you desire from it, and of which you have need, may be alleviated.
Concerning the treatment of water that collects in the heads of children.
Chapter 1.
Know that this ailment frequently occurs to children at birth, or when the midwife tightens the child's head without ease.
And sometimes it happens also from a hidden cause that is not known.
And indeed I have not seen this illness in others than in children, and death has hastened to all whom I have seen afflicted by it.  Therefore, it seemed good to me to omit treatment in this case.
And indeed I saw one of them, a child whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger every day until it could no longer sit upon itself, because of the size of its head; and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture, indeed, either collects between the skin and the bone, or collects under the bone above the sinus.
And if, in that case, the moisture is between the skin and the bone, and the abscess is small, then it is necessary to make a single fissure in the middle of the head, with a width: and the fissure's length should be about two spans, until the moisture runs out.
And this is the form of a lancet.
If, therefore, the moisture is more increased, and the abscess is larger, then place two fissures, according to this form.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open from every side, and water is pressed out when you press it with your hand inwards, and that is not hidden from you: then it is necessary to make three fissures in the middle of the head, according to this method.
And after the incision, remove all the moisture. Then bind the fissures with cloths and bandages. Then apply a poultice of wine and oil to it until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage, and treat the wound with sutures and ointments.
And do not allow the head to be bound with equality. And feed the sick person all dry foods with little moisture, until the limb strengthens and heals.
And another method of incision is to consider where the size of the abscess and the collection of water appear.
Because sometimes there is more in the occiput, or in the forehead, or in the right, or in the left.
Therefore, proceed with the incision where the abscess and collection of water appear to you: and cut it according to what is possible for you.
And observe, lest you cut an artery, and a flow of blood occur, and the sick person die from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Concerning the incision of arteries behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When discharges occur to someone in the eyes or chest, and it is prolonged, and treatment with medicines does not help, then the ultimate treatment in that case is the incision of these two arteries.
Therefore, when you wish to incise them, it is necessary that the sick person shave their head. Then rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear. Then the sick person should tighten their neck with any cloth they have. Then consider where the vein beats. And the places of both are the places which are depressed behind the ears: and it is rarely hidden, except in some men.
Then mark with ink each of the two [spots]: and cut each with a lancet on both sides, the incision reaching the bone, and let it be made across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the lancet under the artery and cut it upward with the incision.
And let the length of the incision be like two fingers joined together.
For when a vein is cut, blood flows out with a pulsating discharge, which leaps inward with a frequent bound.
If however the artery is not visible to the sense, then it is necessary to measure from the ear a space of three fingers' breadth. Then mark with ink, and cut to the bone.
And that which must be emitted of blood is six ounces, and perhaps you emit from it more or less. All of that according to the amount which appears to you from the weakness of the sick person and the filling of his artery.
Then consider the wound. Then if anything remains on the bone from the sifac, then cut it, so that a hot abscess does not occur; then bind the wound with linen strips.
Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
Concerning the extraction of arteries, which are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When a man experiences chronic hemicrania, or acute catarrhs due to acrid humors and heat in the temporal muscles, or severe chronic pain, and similar afflictions: and if treatment with the methods of medicine does not help: then we are experienced in these illnesses with the extraction of arteries from the temples, or their cauterization, as we have described.
And the method of operating on their extraction is that the infirm person should shave the hairs that are on the temples, and direct himself toward the artery appearing in the temple. For it indeed shows itself to you by its pulse, and is rarely hidden except in a few people, or by intense cold.
If it is hidden from you, then the infirm person should tie their head with any cloth of theirs; then rub the area with a rough cloth. Or apply steam to the area with warm water, until a clear appearance is evident to you. Then take the spatula which I have just formed. Then scrape the skin with it easily, until you reach the artery. Then insert a hook into it, and pull it upwards until you have pulled it out of the skin, and cause it to escape from the tunics that are under it on all sides.
If however the artery is delicate: then wrap it with the end of the hook. Then cut a part of it according to the amount by which its ends will be lengthened and drawn together, and so that a flow of blood does not occur. For if it is not cut, and if it is not severed, the blood does not ascend completely.
Then evacuate six ounces of blood, up to three.
If the artery is large: then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a strong, double thread, of silk or of strong cords, so that putrefaction does not quickly occur before the wound is consolidated, and a flow of blood occurs.
Then cut away the excess that is between the two ligatures. Do that at that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery up to the bone, with a surgical cautery, until its extremities are cut, and it will remain in place of the same work.
And it is a better operation, unless the patient has a fever or a hot temperament. Because cautery is among those things that help to end humidities. Therefore, it is a better aid.
And after the extraction of the arteries, the place should be filled with old linen, and pads properly made should be placed upon it.
And after the removal, treat it with dry medicines that cause flesh to grow, and with bandages, until it is healed.
If meanwhile, during the operation, a flow of blood occurs from an artery, then hurry to cut it with a cautery, or to fill the place with zegi; and bind your hand, until the blood is cut off. If none of those are present for you, then place your finger on the place, until the blood is congealed. And embrocate the place with water of great coldness, until the sharpness subsides, and it is bound according to what is necessary.
And from that which is lighter and easier to extract than an artery, is, that you cauterize the vein with this cautery having two knives; and let the sharpness of the two knives be similar to an almond. However, they are of much less sharpness than two knives. Because if they are sharp, like two knives, cold comes quickly to both: and they do not cut the flesh with speed. Therefore, since there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire is retained in them, and the flesh is cut quickly: with this, therefore, the work is better than any other work, and lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of cautery.
And let the space between the two knives be the thickness of a finger, after you mark two places with ink; and place both very hot until they reach the bone, and cut the vein in two places: so that that which is between its extremities may be extended. For they do not consolidate completely with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
Concerning the treatment of acute and persistent running of tears from the eyes.
When the tears are constant, and their flow is from the veins that are on the outer surface of the head, and no medical treatment with medicines helps them, and you see the patient's face already red, and he feels a crawling sensation on his forehead, like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are weakened, damp, so that their hairs are already corroded with the corrosion, and his eyelids are inflamed from the intensity of the tears; then the ancients used this operation in their treatment, and they named this treatment with the Knife.
And it is necessary to instruct the patient to shave the hair on his forehead. Then make three equally spaced incisions on the forehead, following the length of the forehead.  The length of each incision should be about two fingers.  One incision should be equidistant with the length of the nose, in the middle of the forehead; the second, slightly off the temples; and the third, on the opposite side.  Be careful not to cut the arteries which are on either side. Keep your hand away from the area where the two jawbones meet. The distance between each incision should be the equivalent of three fingers placed together.  Prepare a piece of sponge, or many dry cloths, to absorb the blood.
Then introduce a spatula with two sharp ends, whose form is as follows. From the incision that is on the temple side, up to the second incision, and scrape away all the skin that is between the two incisions, with the spatula, which is above the bone. Then do the same from the middle incision to the second incision. Then withdraw this spatula, and introduce into the first incision another instrument, called a sharp scalpel, with one smooth side, not sharp, on the other side. And this is its form.
And place its sharp side towards the flesh attached with skin, and its smooth side towards the bone; and push it, until it reaches the middle fissure; and cut with it all the vessels, which descend from the head to the eyes; except that the incision continues to that which appears of the skin.
Then do that in the middle fissure until another fissure.
And after an equal quantity of blood flows out, express the places from the fragments of clotted blood.
Then place in every fissure a linen cloth from old cotton, and place over them cushions soaked in wine, or oil, or vinegar. And see that a hot abscess does not occur.
And on the third day, loosen the bandage, and administer a plentiful infusion with lukewarm water. Then treat it with basil ointment, and the other remedies with which wounds are treated, until it is healed.
Chapter V.
Concerning the treatment of acute tears and catarrhs to the eyes from internal parts of the head.
When acute, sharp, persistent catarrhs occur in someone; and you see their eyes weakened, small, and their gaze already weakened; and the eyelids ulcerated, and hairs fall from them, and a sharp, painful headache deep within the head, followed by sneezing; then, understand from these occurrences that these materials, and catarrhs, do not arise unless from many veins.
And the better treatment for them is this. And it is to order the sick person to shave their forehead. Then make one incision in the middle of the forehead, or a little wider elsewhere, and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision reach the bone; and draw your hand away from the temporal muscles, which move during chewing, until the bone is exposed; and you will dry up all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate the two edges of the incision with old linen or linen strips. Then bind above with pledgets; and soak the pledgets with wine and oil, lest a hot abscess occur.
And when you remove it, and see that the hot abscess is already lessening, then you must rub the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that causes flesh to grow; such as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony: and prepare a plaster from them, and apply it to the growth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter VI.
Concerning the treatment of that which falls into the Ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four kinds: either a mineral stone, or something like a stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans; or it is a fluid substance, such as water, vinegar, and similar things; or some animal.
Therefore, when a stone, or a kind of stone from those which are not added nor grow in the ear, falls into the ear, expose the ear to the sun.  If you then see the stone, then instill into the ear something of violet oil, or sesame oil. Then administer the extraction of it, and make the person sneeze with condiments, and occlude the nostrils at the arrival of the sneeze, after you have placed around the ear fragments of cloth or wool, and extend the ear upwards.
For often it comes out with this treatment.
If however it does not come out, then administer its extraction with forceps, that is, with fine tweezers: the form of which is this.
If therefore it comes out with the forceps, it is good.
And if not, then administer its extraction with a fine, single-pronged hook.
If therefore it does not come out through that, then make a tube of bronze, and insert the end of the tube into the ear cavity deeply, and plug what is around the tube with softened wax mixed with oil, so that there is no passage by wind except through the tube: then extract with your wind by a strong suction.
For many times it comes out with what we have recounted. If however it does not come out in this way, then take a small amount of birdlime, or of glue, that is, prepared glue, with which birds are caught, and place it at the end of a stick after you have wrapped a bandage around it properly; then insert it into the ear cavity with ease, after you have dried the ear from moisture.
If therefore it does not come out with all that we have recounted, then hasten to incision, before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the method of incision is, that you should first open a vein in the cephalic (vein), and draw out some blood according to his strength. Then make the patient sit between your hands, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear, at its fat, in a depressed area of it; and let the incision be crescent-shaped, until you reach the stone; then remove it with whatever instruments are possible for you.
Then stitch the incision as quickly as you can, and treat it until it heals.
If the thing falling into the ear is one of the small grains that grow and swell, then administer its extraction.
If it does not yield to you, then take a thin, slender spatula, of this form.
And use it to remove that kind of grain that falls into the ear, and do not do so when you think that grain has already been moistened by ear vapor, until you have broken it into many small pieces. Then extract them with hooks, or with a fine probe, or with suction, as we have said, for its removal is thus eased.
However, for water that enters the ear, the patient should use sneezing, at first, with a compress; and now he has filled his ear with some old lint; and lies on the ear in which is the water. If therefore it emerges through it, it is good.
And if not, then take many stones, the length of a finger, thin, smooth, and heat them a little with fire, and let the patient insert one of them into the opening of his ear; and let him hop on one foot on that side, and strike with another stone the stone which is in the ear. Therefore, let him not cease to do that with stone and stone until all the water emerges.
And now the water emerges in such a way, that you take one from papyrus, or from reeds, and insert one end of it into the ear; and you set fire to the other end with fire, until the greatest part of it is burned. Then again another reed, and do that many times, until all the water emerges; and extract it with a cannula according to what has been stated about the stone.
In the extraction of an animal entering it, if it is of small body, like a flea, and similar to it, treat it as I said in the division.
But if it is of large body, which appears to the senses, then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks; and its removal is easier than all those held in the ear.
In the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the division, and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not help, then you should look at the ear in the sun. If therefore anything of the worms appears there, then extract it with forceps, or with fine hooks.
If, however, nothing appears there, then take a cannula, whose form is this, narrow below, wide above; and introduce the slender end into the ear according to the amount that the sick person tolerates; then suck with it with strong suction. Do that many times, until all the worms come out.
If, however, they do not obey you to exit, then smear the surrounding area of the cannula with wax, as I said in the stone.
If they do not come out with what we have said, then administer the distillations that the ancients have already experienced in the killing of worms; and you will find it in the treatise on distillations.
And let your pouring, with which you pour oil and medicines into the ear with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it from silver, or bronze, narrowed below, in which there is a small hole, wide above.
Let the impelling thing, which is in the concavity of the cannula, be made of bronze properly. And if you wish, take a reed, and wrap a cloth around its end with a suitable wrapping; then pour oil, or juice, or whatever medicine you wish from these into the cannula; and let it be in the ear; then introduce the reed with the cloth from above, and squeeze it with your hand with moderate pressure, until the oil is propelled into the concavity of the hearing, and the sick person feels it internally; and that which is poured into the ear, let it be warmed with fire for a short time; and beware that the thing which is distilled into it is not very cold, for the medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the treatment of accidental stoppage of the ear.
Sometimes infants emerge from their mothers' wombs unperforated.
And it happens also to some people that the ear passage is obstructed by a wound, or by flesh growing in it.
And this obstruction sometimes occurs deep within the ear cavity, and the sight does not reach it; and sometimes it occurs near the outer part of the cavity, and sight reaches it.
Therefore, that which occurs deep within the ear, and which the sight does not encompass, is in many instances difficult to cure.
And in that which is visible, you should place the patient's ear in the sun, and look at it.  Then if you see the obstruction appearing, open it with a thin spatula, whose form is as follows: its tip has a small width, part of which is pointed; and the rest of the spatula has two smooth sides, so as not to injure the ear.
If therefore the obstruction is from flesh grown in the ear canal, then grasp it with a thin hook and cut it away with a forceful ease until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is deep within the ear, then take a thin, smooth rod and warm it slightly with fire; then introduce it into the ear canal.
If therefore you sense an obstruction, as the patient finds with a difficult hearing, attempt to open it with ease, and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
Then place in the ear a pessary according to the size of the opening, wrapped in Egyptian ointment.  Do this for several days until you are certain of the wound's healing.
Or take a pessary, and steep it in rose water, and sprinkle ground zegi powder on it, and use it.
If you see that the ear has already become abscessed, then it is necessary to remove the pessary, and apply another pessary wrapped in a cloth made with rose oil, until the hot abscess subsides.  Then treat it until it heals.
If, however, a blood flow occurs, then immerse a sponge or cloths in cold water, and place it over the ear. And use the remaining treatment, which cuts off the blood flow, as previously described in the treatise on distillations.
Concerning the treatment of the Altheli of the eyelids.
Chapter VIII.
These Altheli that occur in the eyelids of the eye are sometimes moist, and sometimes dry.
Therefore, it is necessary to grasp them with tweezers, or with a hook, and cut them with a scalpel from their roots. Then place ground zegi upon the affected areas.
If however blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine lenticular cautery.
And to cauterize them is better after the incision. For they often return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned, and they do not return.
Concerning the treatment of eyelid granulations.
Chapter IX.
Often something occurs in the eyelids that is similar to granulations in its strength and hardness, and on account of that it is called granulations: and it is a collection of thick moisture in the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
Therefore, the operation on them is, that you consider then, if it is granulation proceeding from that which appears on the eyelid, and they move to every part, and its removal is easy.
Therefore, make a fissure over it across its width. Then scrape it from every side with ease, until it disappears. Then suspend it with a hook, and cut it off.
If, however, its excision is not prepared for you unless after you pierce the eyelid with an incision for a short while, then that harms the sick person in some way.
If the fissure is large, then bring it together with sutures, and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For ointment restores it and causes it to flesh over.
And if the granulation is sloping towards the inner surface of the ear, then invert the eyelid and suspend the granulation with a hook; moreover, you need an incision and press it from every side.
If the eyelid is extended by incision, that will not harm it in any way.
Then wash the eye after the incision of the granulation with salt water, and treat the place with that which causes fleshing, until the sick person is healed.
Concerning the treatment of the affliction of the eyelids.
Chapter X.
Axirnath is a fat that is generated in the membranes of the upper eyelid, and it most often happens to children. It weighs down their eyes, and they have catarrhs in the mornings, and cannot look at the sunlight, because tears rush to them, and because of this you see them lying on their faces always, and they sleep on them, and their eyelids are moist under their eyebrows, which are already covered by swelling.  And the prominence of the Axirnath is visible to the eye, and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the Axirnath between them.
And the method of its treatment is that the sick person should place their head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth, and prepare on it strips, and make a circle from that strip according to the amount that Axirnath contains on all sides. Then place it over it, and press with your fingers on all sides, so that the Axirnath is collected in the middle of the circle.
Then find in the middle of that moisture, with a lancet, slits along the width, and do not let the slit be larger than that which is made in phlebotomy in depth.
Or it is necessary that you cut through the entire skin, until you reach the Axirnath, for in many arrangements the Axirnath proceeds from the slit in the form of a white fat piece.
Therefore extract it with the cloth, which you have folded between your fingers to the outside, and you turn your hand to the right and left until it is dispersed; and beware, do not increase the slit, so that you do not reach the eye with the lancet and wound it.
If however the Axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the slit, you shall add a little to the slit with ease, until the Axirnath proceeds. Then extract it, as we said.
Then immerse cloths in vinegar and water, and place them on the area, and bind it with bandages.
And there are men who rub salt, and place it in the concavity of the slit, so that it liquefies what remains of that moisture.
Then treat it until it heals.
If however it happens in a place with a hot abscess, then treat it with soothing plasters, until it heals.
Concerning the ways of Ataxmir of the eye, that is, when the eyelid of the eye softens, whence it comes to be wrinkled as it should not be.
Chapter XI.
When additional hairs grow in the eyelids, beyond the natural hair course, and they become aged: then they harm the eye, and cause various ailments, such as constant tears, and softening of the eyelids, and cloudiness, until it becomes the cause of the eye's destruction.
And Ataxmir of the eye is indeed treated according to four ways. Or by cautery with fire. Or with a sharp medicine, according to what has been stated in the chapter on cautery. Or Ataxmir is treated by incision and suture. Or by tubes, according to what I will say.
It is necessary that you place the head of the sick person in your bosom. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If therefore it turns, it is good. And if not: insert the needle into it, in which is the thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle with thread penetrate from above, and let it be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the edge of the eyelid where the hairs grow; and pull the thread upward with the eyelid, and turn it with the radius.
Then find in the inner part of the eyelid under the added hairs with a spatula proceeding from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct.
Then pull out the thread, and place under the eyelid a small pad of cotton, or cloth; then mark on the eyelid with ink, which should be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the shape be made according to the size and elevation of the eyelid, because it is already diversified in humans.
For among them is one who needs a large amount to be cut from the eyelid, according to the amount that softens from the eyelid.
Then with a spatula, find above the two lines that you marked, and begin from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct, and let the fissure be close to the natural hairs, as is the thickness of a ray.
Then insert the hook into one of the two corners of the skin. Then scrape the whole of it. Then bring together the two lips with a suture, with a needle and fine wool thread; and wipe away the blood, and make the excess thread adhere to the two eyebrows, with certain invisible things, if you wish to do so; and if not, do not worry.
Then leave the suture and threads for up to three or four days; afterwards, care for it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without a suture, and care for it with that which dries and constricts it. For the eyelid is raised at the sealing and bringing together of the wound: but it will be better.
This method of treatment the ancients taught Ataxmir.
However, there is care for the sick person in it, and it is from good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is in Ataxmir also: and it is, that you mark on the eyelid a shape, which is like the shape of a myrtle leaf, as we have narrated.
Then raise the eyelid with three separate hooks, or aggregated, according to this form which follows, then cut what is superfluous with small scissors, according to this form, with equality in the incision.
If however it is not possible for you to hold the hook, and it is not equal to it: then take a needle in which there is a thread, and introduce it into the middle of the shape; and introduce another thread near the larger tear duct, and a second thread near the smaller tear duct; and gather the threads between your fingers with equality.
Then raise your hand with equal elevation, and raise the skin upon which the mark is, as we have narrated.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture, and care for it until it heals.
If, however, an abscess occurs at your incision, or your fissure: soothe that abscess with a cauterizing agent, and similar things, from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid, too, that its hairs are turned.
Therefore, it is necessary that you use what we said concerning the incision: but a suture is more artificial for both. Concerning the treatment of Ataxmir with reeds.
And it is, that you turn the eyelid, and slit the fissure that is within, according to what I have described. Then skillfully make two reeds, or two thin pieces of wood, whose length is according to the length of the eyelid, and their width less than the width of the spatula; and you have already incised at the extremities of each from both sides where you hold the threads.
Then carefully place each of them over that which is left over from the eyelid, and tightly bind the two reeds from both sides with a strong constriction, and leave it for several days. For the tight skin will die, and turn black, and putrefy, until it falls off by itself.
If therefore it is delayed, then cut it with scissors. Then care for it until it heals.
When therefore it is consolidated, the eyelid is raised: and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of two canes, which you will make according to this figure, and quantity in length and width.
Chapter XII.
Concerning the raising of pricking hairs in the eye with a needle.
The procedure in that is to take a fine needle, and insert into it a fine, smooth silk thread. Then tie its ends, and make a very small knot on each end: and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then place in the Alinxotati another fine thread beneath it in brief, and tie its ends if you wish.
Then let the sick person place their head in your lap, and be near the sun so that the operation may be revealed; because it is a subtle, or minute, operation.
Then insert the needle with the alinxotati into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you pull it out above the natural hair.
Then pull the hair up above the alinxotati, and insert that hair into the alinxotati: if it is one, or two, or three, not more.
Then pull your hand with it, until the hair emerges with the alinxotati from the eyelid with the natural hair.
However, if the alinxotati is pulled out, and the hair does not emerge with it: pull the alinxotati downwards, with the thread which you have placed in it, until the alinxotati emerges from the upper opening, and you see it with the vision of the eye: then you will pull the alinxotati and the thread; and remove both, and close the eye; and leave it closed for two days, or three, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, then either associate a long hair from the natural hairs with it, and raise it with it; or leave it, until it lengthens after a period, and then raise it.
Chapter XIII.
When this Alxatrati happens to the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this Alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Therefore, the accidental kind results from the healing of a wound, or incision, or cautery, and things similar to them.
And the method of treatment in it is that you find that consolidation, and that you separate its lips, and place in that which is between them a linen pad, and bind it, until it is healed.
And you should not use in its treatment things that dry and constrict; for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was: rather, use in it things that soften, such as fenugreek; and embrocate with water in which cooked althea, flaxseed; and with the unguent dyaquilon, which has been liquefied with some oil; and wrap with it a linen pad, and treat it with that.
And the treasure of your treatment is to strive with all your ingenuity that it not consolidate according to the form that it initially had.
And this operation, which we have mentioned, is nothing but a certain rectification of the Alxatrati, so that the form of the place does not return to what it was completely.
Concerning the treatment of Alxatrati which occurs in the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkling or contraction upward to the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This Alxatrati which occurs in the lower part, is the one truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And the accidental form results from a wound, or a fissure, or a cautery, and similar things.
And the method of operation in it is that you should take a needle, in which there is a double thread, and dip it into the flesh, and cause it to penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread reaches the two ends of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh upwards with the needle, and cut it with a broad spatula. If therefore the shape of the eyelid returns as it should, it is good.
And if not, take a radius, and place it in the fissure, and turn the eyelid with it: and make two fissures in the inner side of the eyelid, and let the ends of the two fissures be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and form an angle from them: until when they come together they make their shape this shape, and it is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh by the amount that is its sharp lower side, from that which follows the eyelid. Then bring together the separated parts: sew it with a wool thread.
Then treat them with what we said, of softening medicines, and bandages, until they are healed.
And if an injury occurs from a fissure, or suture, or cautery: then it is necessary that you make a flat fissure under the hairs of the eyelids, also according to what preceded. Then separate between the two lips with a bandage, according to what we said.
And the summary of the treatment of Alxatrati, when it is on the top or below, is that the operation should proceed according to the form of the Alxatrati.
For it is indeed of many varieties in form: and the skilled craftsman prepares his skill in whatever way is possible for him, until he restores the figure to its natural form, or near to it, and the injured person himself, according to what suits him from the operation and instruments in many arrangements.
Chapter XV.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation happens to many people, so that the upper eyelid becomes consolidated with the white of the eye and prevents it from its natural operations.
Therefore, you should insert the tip of the probe under the eyelid and lift it upwards; or extend it with a hook; then cut the adhesion with a thin spatula, which should not be sharp, but rather slightly blunt, so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula is removed, and does not injure the eye with its sharpness. And your incision should be such that you gently scrape away the adhesion until the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the entire adhesion is healed.
Then pour salt water into the eye, washing it with the water; or distill into it from a sieve made of dissolved zinc with water.
Then separate the eyelid from the eye with linen thread; and place over the eye wool soaked in egg white; and after three days use the sieve, and with consolidants, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the toenail and the prominence of the tear-producing flesh.
Chapter XVI.
Know that the toenail is according to two methods. Or it is nervous: and it is similar to hard, fine sifac. Or it is not nervous, similar to frozen white moisture: which when it touches iron, or you try to grasp it with a hook, is severed, and the hook does not become fixed in it.
And both types do not begin, unless from the greater tear duct, until it goes gradually, until it covers the sight, and hinders the light, and the movement of the eye.
And the method of operating on it is: that the sick person should place his head in your lap. Then open his eyes, and elevate the eyelid with your hand. Then collect the nail with a hook of few doublings, and extend it upward. Then take a needle, and insert into it a hair from the hairs of a horse, or ox, or cow: and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle a little, and fix it in the middle of the nail: and pierce it with the needle, and tie the nail with the thread, and extend it upward: and scrape with the hair the side of the nail which follows the pupil, as if you were sawing it with the hair until its end.
Then cut the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a thin spatula, or with small scissors: and leave the natural tear duct tissue, so that an incessant flow of tears does not result from the incision.
And sometimes the nail is extended with a hook alone, or with a thread, as we said. Then it is scraped with a gentle spatula, of which this is the shape.
And be careful, lest the spatula touch the horny coat, and a rupture occur in it, and the pupil be pierced.
Then, after the incision, put some crushed salt into the eye, or a substance made from ziniar, and leave it until the next day. Then treat it with what is necessary until it is healed.
If the nail is not nervous, and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor is a hook fixed in it, then this type of nail is often treated with medicaments, and is healed as I have described in the division.
If you wish to cut it, then open the sick person's eye, and take a thin, gentle, weakly-sharpened spatula, according to this form. And scrape the nail from above with care. If you see that it is becoming thin and breaking, and the scraping is affecting it, then distil from your hour into the eye a sieve made of ziniar, or a red sieve, or crushed salt; and bandage the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the operation until the whole of it is removed. Unless there is a hot abscess in the eye; for then leave it, and treat the abscess until it is healed.
Then repeat the operation on it with scraping, until it is healed.
However, if the fleshy prominence of the tear duct is harmful to the eye with great injury, it should be suspended with a hook, and a portion of it should be cut; and do not make the incision too deep, so that the flow of tears does not occur.
Then instill into the tear duct a red, or one made from ziniari, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of Alguardenic, and the flesh grown in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes in some people's eyes, red flesh grows, either composed or superimposed, until it covers the pupil, or approaches it, or presses against the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outwards; and it resembles a rose-hip.
And the treatment for it is also that the sick person should place their head in your lap. Then open his eyes, and collect all that flesh with suitable hooks: and hold it with one forceps, or tongs.
Then cut into the flesh at first, until the whole of it is finished with a scalpel. And be careful of the eye, so that you do not injure it during the operation: and let your incision, with which you cut it, be either with a spatula, which we have described in the incision of the hoof; or with small scissors, with which the asebel is collected, according to how its shape will appear after this chapter.
When, therefore, your incision is complete, and the whole of the alguardenici is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt: or distill into it red powder, and similar corrosive medicines: and place over the eye externally a cloth with the white of an egg: so that it is safeguarded from a hot abscess.
If any alguardenici remains, and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a hot abscess: then leave the eye, and treat it with that which calms the abscess.
Then repeat the operation on it, until it is healed.
And similarly do with the added flesh which happens in the eye of this kind.
However, it is necessary that you avoid the treatment of every weak eye by natural or accidental means, by operating with iron: and do not put your hand into any of these operations until you see that the limb is tolerant of that operation.
Concerning the collection of Sebel from the eye.
Chapter XVIII.
Sebel are red veins that weave over the eye, and hinder the operation of its vision: and weaken the eye with the length of days.
Therefore, you must first consider if the eye in which the Sebel is present is strong, and there is no other ailment in it beyond the Sebel: then collect the Sebel from it.
And it is that you should instruct the sick person to place their head in your bosom.
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your prudence: and let the hook be a thin double hook, according to this form, or let there be two hooks graduated in one body, according to this form.
Then collect those veins with fine scissors carefully, and wipe away the blood hour by hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye, and are dissolved: and beware that you do not injure the eye with the tips of the scissors.
And let your operation be in the midday sun, and be very firm in your operation: so that you do not injure anything but those veins.
And when you have finished, then instill into the eye green or red juice, that by its sharpness it may corrode what remains of the scab.
If however it is not possible for you to collect it all in that hour, then plaster the eye with that which soothes the pain of the eye, and leave it for a few days, until its pain subsides; and ensure it is free from a hot abscess; then again treat it in the same way, until it is healed.
And this is the form of the scissors.
Concerning the reduction of a nose injury.
Chapter XIX.
Physicians call a nose injury a fistula, which you treat with a cautery, or with a sharply burning medicine, according to what its previous description has indicated.
And if it does not heal, then there is no skill in it, unless you open up the abscess at its origin and remove all the moisture and pus in it until the bone is exposed.
When the bone is exposed, and you see corruption or blackness on it, then scrape it with an instrument, whose shape is this.
And it is called a "rough head".
Make it from tempered iron, and let its head be round like a small pestle, pointed with a file, or with a fine-pointed awl, and place it on the diseased part of the bone.
Then revolve it between your fingers, and press your hand a little, until you know that the corruption has been scraped away; and do this several times.
Then restore the place with drying, astringent medicines.
Therefore, if the place is consolidated, and flesh grows on it, and the course of pus is cut off; and it remains for forty days, and is not renewed, and does not become a boil, and no accident occurs in it; then know that it is now healed.
And if not, then it has no cure, except to reduce the fistula to the nostril opening, according to this form.
And it is, that you uncover the second bone with iron, or with a sharp medicine; and when the bone is uncovered, then take a drill according to this form, let its iron tip be triangular, and its wood be a tapering pyramid down to the tip, as you see.
Then place it on the bone itself, and let it be near the tear duct, and move your hand away from the eye a little. Then rotate your hand with the drill, until it penetrates the bone, and the patient feels it: by this that he finds wind exiting from it, when he holds his hand over his nose.
Therefore then restore the place with astringent drying medicines, as I taught you.
When therefore flesh is born, and the place hardens, then the matter that was being discharged outwardly returns to the nose; thus it is easier for the patient.
Concerning the reduction of the prominent eye.
Chapter XX.
When the eye protrudes in its entirety, and no harm or diminution occurs in vision, then it is necessary to free the patient; then bleed him; afterward apply cupping glasses to the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then place on the eye a liniment made from acacia, aloe, frankincense, and sarcocolla. Then bind the eye from above with many small pads over a plaster.
And the manner of the small pads is: that you take soft cloths, many, and each cloth should be folded over four times; make many of them; then place them one over another, according to the quantity required by the eye; then bind them with a firm bandage over them.
And again apply cupping glasses with suction without scarification.
Then remove the cupping glasses, and leave the eye bound day and night.
However, if the bandage softens again, then it is necessary that it be bound tightly; then remove the bandage after one day and night, as we have said.
If therefore you see the eye has now returned to the condition of its tunic, it is good; and if not, then apply the plaster again, and pads, and bandage, and cupping glasses, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the uvea tunic.
Chapter XXI.
When rupture occurs in the uvea tunic, then it protrudes and emerges outside the eyelids like a uvea grain, and disfigures the human form because of it, and the desire is to incise it: then the operation in it is as I narrate: and it is, that you insert a needle into the root of the uvea from the inferior to the superior part.
Then insert another needle carrying a double thread from the lacrimal side, and make it penetrate; and leave the first needle according to its position.
Then cut the place of the double thread, and tie with it the part of the uvea in a circle around the needle; and tighten it much.
Then extract the needle, and place upon the eye wool steeped in the white of an egg; and leave it, until the thread falls with the uvea.
Then treat the eye with that which strengthens it, until the wound is healed.
And sometimes the uvea is tightened from every side with a circle of cloth, until the eye adds great prominence.
Then it is pierced with a fine spatula, which reaches the depth of the eye, and the white fluid runs out, and the eye descends, and enters immediately; then tighten it until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of Alcomnat.
Chapter XXII.
This illness, called Alcomnati, is nothing but pus that collects in the eye, similar to descending water, and it is not the eye itself.
And the method of operation in it is that you make the infirm person sit on a prepared seat, or upright.
Then take its head in your hands from two sides, and move it until you see the pus descend to your eyes below. Then let it be fixed, and do not let it descend, and let the light be freed: and let the sick person see things as they saw: and if it does not descend lower, know that it is water.
If, however, the descent of the pus is not prepared with what we have said, then make the sick person sit between your hands. Then take a fine spatula, and split with it above the corneal membrane slightly at the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea, in the fold, until pus emerges.
When it emerges, then instill warm water in which you have mixed honey into the eye: or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey: then treat with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of water that descends into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter XXIII.
We have already discussed the types of water in the division, and what is suitable for piercing, the speaker declaring and explaining.  Then take that same thing according to health.
Then it is necessary to make the sick person sit between your hands, square, facing away from the light, near the sun; and bind his healthy eye tightly; then with your right hand, if the eye in which there is water is the left eye, or with your left hand, if it is the right eye, press the eyelid of his eye.
Then take with your right hand an almagda, if the eye is the left eye, or with your left hand, if the eye is the right eye.
Then place the end of the almagda near the crown, along the thickness of a ray, on the apparent white of the eye near the smaller tear duct; then propel the almagda with force, and you revolve your hand with it, until it penetrates the white of the eye; and you feel that the almagda has now reached the empty thing.
And it is necessary that the measure of the almagda's journey to the depth be the measure of the space from the pupil to the end of the blackness; and it is the crown of the eye. For you will see them in the very pupil of the eye's vision, because of the clarity of the cornea.
Then place the almagda to the place where the water is. Then press it downwards one after another.
If therefore the water descends immediately, then the sick person sees that above which he opens his vision immediately, and the almagda in his eye.
Then rest a little. If therefore the water descends, then put it down a second time, apart from the almagda exiting.
When therefore it is fixed, and does not ascend: then extract the almagda with ease: and you revolve your hand gradually.
Then dissolve in water some of the clear gem salt: and wash the eye with it from the inside. Then place over the eye all the linen, or wool soaked in rose oil, and egg white; and bind the healthy eye with it.
We, and those of our time, place on it pounded cumin with egg white.
If the almagda does not obey you in entering the eye because of its hardness, since it is from people whose eye is very hard: then it is necessary to take a spatula, which is called Alberid, of which this is the shape.
Therefore, gently scrape with it only the conjunctiva, and do not place your hand in the opening: and it is only to make a subtle path to the almagda's location.
Then introduce the almagda, as we have said.
And at the completion of your work, prepare a suitable sleeping area for the sick person, where they sleep on their back in a dark room: and forbid all movements and coughing: and place their food such that it soothes their nature: and they must not move their head to the right or left at all.
And let the bandage remain in accordance with its arrangement until the third day.
Then remove it in that dark room, and test his vision: and make him see objects. Then replace the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not appropriate to do that at the time of treatment, or immediately after the incision: rather, it is necessary to delay it, because water ascends quickly through violent looking.
If, however, a hot abscess occurs: then it is necessary to remove the eye before the seventh day, and treat it with whatever quiets the abscess, until it subsides; then clear his vision, and apply an oral remedy to his face to regulate his vision under it for some days, while he remains in that dark room.
Then let him gradually emerge from the house, and move about in his own rooms.
And know that in a zalte, a student is not excused from sight at times; then let him dare to undertake the operation.
And now indeed someone from those who are from Alayrach has come to us, who said that a perforated magdan was made in Alayrach, from which water is drawn.
But truly I have not seen anyone in your land who has done this; nor have I read it in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of the species from the magdan, so that he who is ignorant of them may stand upon it.
Let them be made properly of bronze; and let their extremities be triangular with this same fineness, and pointed.
Chapter XXIV.
Concerning the treatment of flesh grown in the nose.
Sometimes various fleshy growths arise in the nose, among which is something resembling a scorpion with many feet; and from it is cancerous flesh, stony, of a dark color; and from it is flesh that is soft, not of a dark color.
In that therefore which is soft of these fleshy growths, not deceitful nor cancerous, it is necessary that you make the infirm person sit between your hands facing the ground, and open his nostrils, and insert a hook into those growths; then pull them outward, and cut what you have grasped from them with a fine, sharp spatula on one side, until you know that the entire flesh has been removed.
If anything remains from that whose removal is not possible, then scrape it away with one of the fine instruments with ease, until nothing remains of it. If, however, blood dominates it, or a hot abscess occurs, then apply to it what is appropriate; or if it is from malignant abscesses, then hasten to cauterize it, until the blood is separated, and all flesh is removed.
Then vinegar, and water, or wine, should be applied to the nose after the incision. If, however, the nose opens, and moisture proceeds from it to the palate, then know that it is now healthy.
If, however, the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then know that within it is flesh grown on the upper nasal bones, to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Therefore, it is necessary to insert a linen thread of some thickness, and tie many knots in it; and place between each knot a quantity of a finger's width, or less; and let the infirm person, as much as possible, direct one end of the thread into the nose with a rod, or with whatever is possible for him after he has made it like a needle; and pull his odoriferous thing until it reaches the abscess, and emerges above his throat.
And many times children like Attomi do a similar thing in schools; and it is an easy thing for him who wishes.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end which emerges above the nose, and the other which remains in the nose. Then, and with instruments, cut away the flesh with the knots which are on the thread. Do that until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
Then pull out the thread; and place in the nose after cleansing the blood, a pledget which you have soaked in Egyptian ointment: do this for three days, or more, until the ointment eats away all that remains of the flesh.
Then place at the end of the matter, into the nose, a lead cannula, for a few days, until it is healed.
If however it needs a treatment that dries it, use that.
To make a nasal douche, with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it from silver or bronze: it should be like a small lamp, open like a shell: and its passageway should be similar.
And if you wish, make it a closed cannula, like a tube.
And the shell of the instrument for nasal irrigation should be full, revealed; which has a handle at its end, with which you hold it when you heat oil or whatever you wish from juices and fluid things in it.
Chapter XXV.
Often a wart is born at the end of the nose, and it grows larger and increases with the days, until its appearance becomes unsightly.
And therefore it is necessary, that in the beginning of its appearance, you should cut it off and eradicate all of it. Then place on the site either a cautery or a burning medicine that stands in its place.
If, therefore, its incision is delayed until it grows large, then consider, if it is stony, hard, of dark color, and insensitive: then do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous abscess.
For I have often seen that when a man cuts an abscess, and great harm results to the one who had it.
And if the abscess is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that its incision is possible throughout, then evacuate the infirmity, and cut it without fear, without apprehension, and treat the site with something that dries and constricts, until it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
Concerning the suturing of the nose, lip, and ear.
Know that when a separation of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, little assistance is given in it by operation, except in some individuals.
Therefore, when something of that sort happens to someone, you should consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood; and then bring the edges of the wound together with suture; afterward, treat it until it heals.
And if the edges of the separation of continuity are already separated, and each part is healthy, then you should scrape away all the part from its apparent skin, until it bleeds. Then bring the two edges together with suture, and tie them, and powder upon it the blood of the dragon and crushed frankincense; and place upon the powders a piece of diafinicon plaster and some other of the consolidating plasters; and leave it bound for two or three days. Then untie it, and change the medicine; and leave it until the threads cut themselves off. Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
The method of suturing is, that you bring together the separation of continuity, either with needles, as we have described in the suturing of the belly; or bring together the separation with thread, as I have made known to you.
Chapter XXVII.
Concerning the extraction of lip nodes.
Many people often have hard abscesses, similar to the grains of herbs, in the interior of their lips; and some of them are smaller, and some larger.
Therefore, it is necessary to turn back the lip, and to split over every node of it, and to suspend it with a hook, and to cut it from every side.
Then fill the place after the incision with crushed zegi, until the blood is stopped.
Then it should be washed with vinegar, and the place where there is astringency should be treated with it, until the wounds are healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
Concerning the incision of fleshy tissue on the gums.
Flesh often grows on the gums, which the ancients called abuluss.
Therefore, you should suspend it with a hook; or grasp it with forceps, and cut it near its root, and allow the pus or blood to run.
Then place on the affected area ground zegi, or other astringent, drying powders.
If that flesh returns after treatment, for it often does, then cut it again and cauterize it; for it does not return after cauterization.
Chapter XXIX.
On the extraction of teeth with iron.
Sometimes rough, foul, and sometimes darkened, yellowish, and greenish, hard crusts collect on the surfaces of the teeth from the inner and outer sides, and between the gums, until the decay reaches the gums, and the teeth become unsightly because of it.
Therefore, you should have the patient sit between your hands, with his head in your lap, and scrape the molar and tooth, where the crusts appear to you, and matter similar to sand, until nothing remains of them, and similarly do with the blackness, greenness, and yellowness, and other such things, until they are cleaned.
If, therefore, the matter that is on them from the beginning of the scraping is removed, it is good; and if not, then repeat the scraping on them another, second, and third day, until finally the desired result is achieved.
And know that a tooth requires scrapers of various shapes and forms, according to how the operation is prepared.  Therefore, the scraper used to scrape the tooth from the bottom is different from the scraper used to scrape between the teeth, according to different shapes.
And have these shapes of scrapers prepared, which you see.
Chapter 30.
Concerning the extraction of toothache.
You ought to treat the tooth from its pain with all ingenuity, and be slow in its eradication. For there is nothing to replace it when it is eradicated, because it is a lofty substance: until when there is no excuse for its eradication, then it is necessary when the infirm is forced to eradicate it, so that it may be strengthened, and the aching tooth be certified by you.
For often pain deceives the infirm, and they think that it is in the tooth's saha, and they eradicate it: then the pain does not recede, until the infirm's tooth is eradicated.
For we have already seen this from the operation of phlebotomists.
Therefore, when the aching tooth itself is certified by you, then you should scarify around the tooth with a spatula, in which there is something of virtue, until the gum is loosened on all sides: then move it with your fingers, or with delicate forceps at first slowly, until you crush it.
Then place large forceps in a good position on it, and the patient's head between your knees: which you have already held well, so that it does not move: then pull the tooth according to its straightness, so that you do not break it.
If therefore it does not emerge, then take one of those instruments, and insert it under it from every side with ease, and attempt its movement, as you did at first.
If however the tooth is perforated or corroded, then it is necessary that you fill that hole with cloth: and tie it with the end of a thin ray, so that it does not break at the time of your constriction upon it with forceps.
And it is necessary that you act meticulously with the scarification around the gum from every side very much.
And maintain your strength so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and returns upon the sick person from that malice which is greater pain to him at first.
And beware, lest you do that which ignorant phlebotomists do in their haste, and their boldness in its eradication, beyond what we have described.
For they often cause great illnesses in men, which are more difficult to break, and the roots of it all remain, or some of them; or to extract it with some of the jawbones: as we have testified many times.
Then it should be rinsed after its eradication with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
If a flow of blood occurs from the places (for it often happens), then rub some zegi there, and fill the place with it; and if not, cauterize it if the zegi does not help you.
The shapes of the fine forceps with which the tooth is first moved should have long extremities, a short handle, and be thick, so that they do not double when pressed against the tooth; they should be like those with thick handles, so that when you tighten them, they do not yield or double: with short extremities, and made of strong iron, or well-made steel, with straight extremities; and on their extremities there should be teeth, some of which enter others, and grip with a fitting, strong constriction.
And sometimes the extremities are made like the shape of the beak of a bird called a stork, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter Thirty-one.
Concerning the eradication of teeth, and the extraction of fractured mandibular bones.
When, during the extraction of a tooth, something remains, then it is necessary to apply to the spot a poultice of butter for one or two days, until the area softens; then introduce to it algasti, or forceps, whose extremities are similar to the beak of a bird called a stork. This, however, is the form of the forceps.
Their extremities should be made like a file from within, or like aliskilfegi.
If, therefore, it does not yield to you for extraction with these forceps, then it is necessary that you carefully approach the root and expose all the flesh, and introduce an instrument called a small lever, whose form is this.
Let it be short in extremity, somewhat thick, and let it not be soaked, so that it does not break.
If, therefore, the root emerges through it, it is good; and if not, it is necessary to assist with other instruments whose forms are these.
The form of the first is triangular in extremity, in which there is a certain thickness.
The form of a triangular, slender extremity.
And with other instruments and tools which preceded in the rationale of the teeth.
Also, let there be help with this instrument, which is likened to a large hook, of this form, triangular extremity twisted, in which there is something of thickness, so that it does not break; and let it not be soaked.
And know that there are many instruments for the teeth, and similarly, the remaining instruments are not counted, and the skillful craftsman, subtle in his art, finds for himself the instrument, according to that which operation and the illnesses themselves signify to him.
Since there are illnesses for which the ancients did not say there was an instrument, because of the diversity of their species.
If, however, a bone breaks from the jaw, or from other bones of the mouth, or rots: then probe over it in its place with that which is fitting to it from among the instruments and forceps which I have mentioned in the extraction of roots, and help with a chisel, the shape of which is this.
Let there be something of thickness in it gradually, so that the bone is tightened or held with it, and does not flee and go, until the bone emerges; and restore the place with medicines suitable to it.
If however there is small rot in the bone, then scrape it from its rot, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter XXXII.
Of the sawing of teeth that protrude over others.
When teeth protrude not according to their natural course, a foul form is made by it; and especially when this happens in women and girls, namely those who are for sale.
It is therefore necessary that you consider. For if a tooth has grown after another tooth, and is not in place, or if sawing or filing it is not possible, then extract it; and if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, of which this is the form.
It is similar to a small beak, and is made of tempered iron, with a very sharp tip.
And let your incision be such that you cut it over many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that another of the teeth is not moved.
If however it is prominent, exposed to its filing: then file it with a file made of tempered iron, of which the form is this.
Let the whole be of tempered iron, and its handle of small, pointed points, like the file with which needles are made; with which the tooth is filed little by little over many days with ease, so that the tooth is not moved and falls; then smooth it, finally scrape it with certain scrapers.
If, however, there is a tooth from which a part breaks off, and it injures the tongue: then it is necessary to file it also, until the roughness of that fracture is removed, and it is made equal; and it is flattened, and it does not injure the tongue, nor corrupt speech.
Chapter XXXIII.
Concerning the retention of teeth with gold wires.
When the front teeth are injured and displaced by a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on it without it falling, and you have treated them with astringent remedies and the treatment was not successful: then it is appropriate for them to be bound with gold wire; for silver turns green and rots after days, but gold never changes.
And let the wire be of medium fineness and thickness according to the amount that it needs to span between the teeth.
And the method of retention is to take a wire and insert twice its length between two healthy teeth; then weave the two ends of the wire between the loosened teeth, whether one or more, until you reach with the weaving to a healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving back to the place where you began; and tighten your hand with delicacy and skill until it is firmly held; and let your tightening of the wire be near the roots of the teeth, so it does not slip out.
Then cut the excess ends of the wire with scissors; and bring both ends together and twist them together; and hide them between the healthy tooth and the loose tooth so they do not harm the tongue; then let it be so tight as to remain.
If however it loosens or breaks, tighten them with another wire and it will remain so during the whole time.
And this is the method for teeth, and the shape of retention for two healthy teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one or two teeth return after falling out to their places and are retained as we have described, and remain; and only a skilled, prudent, and experienced craftsman can achieve this.
And sometimes a piece of bone is fitted; and it is shaped like a tooth and placed in the socket, whereby the tooth is strengthened and tightened, as we said; and it remains and serves with it for a long time.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
Sometimes this ligament which occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which a man is born, or accidental, from a wound that has already healed.
And the operation on it is that you open the sick person's mouth at his head in your presence, and lift his tongue; then cut that nervous ligament across its width, until the tongue is released from its restraint.
If there is something of hardness and knotting in it, and it is from the consolidation of a wound: then apply a hook to it, and split it across its width, until you cut the ligament, and the knotting is released.
And be careful, lest there be a fissure deep within the flesh, and you cut an artery there, and a flow of blood occur.
Then wash the place of the incision with rose water, or with vinegar, or with cold water.
Then place a linen bandage under the tongue, which the sick person should hold all night, so that it does not consolidate again.
If a flow of blood occurs, then place crushed zegi over the area. If, however, blood overcomes you, then cauterize the area with a lentiginous cautery, which is appropriate for it; then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
Chapter XXXV.
Sometimes an abscess occurs under the tongue that resembles a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and perhaps it grows so large that it fills the mouth.
And the treatment in this is to open the mouth of the sick person in the presence of the sun, and consider the abscess.
If therefore you see it to be of dark color, or black, hard, to which the sick person has no feeling: then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
And if it is inclined towards whiteness, in which there is moisture, then insert a hook into it, and cut it with a thin spatula, and free it from every side. If blood overcomes you during your operation, then place powdered zegi upon it until the bleeding ceases; then return to your operation, until you extract it with its entirety.
Then wash it with vinegar and salt, and treat it with the appropriate remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of an abscess of the two tonsils.
Chapter XXXVI.
Sometimes there occur between the throat, that is, glands resembling algodad, which occur externally; and they are called the two tonsils. When you treat them with what we said in the division, and they do not heal, then consider.
If therefore it is a hard abscess, of a dark colour, with little sensation, then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of red colour, and its root is thick, then do not touch it even with iron, fearing a flow of blood, but rather leave it until it matures. And then either you will pierce it, or it will break open by itself.
And if it is of white colour, round, and its root is thin, then it is necessary to cut it.
And the operation on it is, that you should consider before his operation, if the abscess has quieted, its heat having been completed by rest, or has lessened with some diminution: then therefore make the sick man sit before the sun, and let his head be in your lap: and open his mouth, and let the servant take between his hands, and press down his tongue with an instrument, of which this is the shape.
Let it be made of silver, or bronze, which is as thin as a knife.
When therefore you press with it the tongue, and the abscess is shown to you, and your sight falls upon it, then take the hook, and fix it into the tonsil, and pull it outward as much as you can, besides which pull out with it something from the coverings.
Then cut it with an instrument, of which this is the following shape. It resembles shears: however, let its ends be curved, so that the opening of each of them is in a straight line with the other sharply: therefore take care that it is made of tempered iron, or steeped in alfuled.
If however this instrument is not present, then cut it with a spatula, of which this is the form. Let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have cut one tonsil, cut the other according to this same type of incision.
Then after the incision, let the sick person gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
If however a flow of blood occurs, let him gargle with water in which the peels of pomegranates have been boiled: or the leaves of myrtle, and similar astringents, until the flow of blood is stopped: then treat him until he is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses arise in the throat, besides the two tonsils. Therefore, cut it according to what I have said about the incision of the two tonsils equally.
And I myself have already treated a woman from an abscess that arose between her throat, which was drawing to a purple hue, few in senses, which had almost completely closed her throat: and the woman breathed through a narrow passage, and I had already forbidden her from eating and drinking water: and she had already reached death if it had remained for one or two days.
And the abscess had ascended so much that it had emitted two branches, until they emerged above the two nostrils.
I therefore hastened quickly, and fixed a hook in one of them. Then I pulled it, and a large portion was drawn from it.
Then I cut it off where I grasped it from the nostril.
Then I did the same to that which proceeded from the other nostril.
Then I opened its mouth, and pressed its tongue; and afterwards I fixed a hook in the abscess; then I cut off a part of it, and only a little blood ran from it: therefore the woman's throat was healed, and she immediately began to drink water, then took food.
