Therefore I did not cease to cut from that abscess many times for a long time; and the abscess succeeded the place that I was cutting, until it was extended too much and with it that.
Therefore I devised and cauterized the abscess between the throat.
Therefore it remained from the addition; then I went away from that place, and I did not know what God did with her afterward.
Concerning the incision of the abscess of the uvula.
Chapter XXXVII.
When the catarrh descends to the uvula, and it becomes an abscess, and is long, then it is called a perpendicular one; and if it is in the lower part thick, round, then it is called a grape.
When it is treated with what we have said in the division, and the treatment does not help; and you see that the hot abscess has ceased from it, and is subtle; then it is necessary that you should cut it.
And that which is collected round from it, and has no length, and is bloody, or of a brown or black colour, and has no feeling, then it is necessary to avoid its incision. For there is fear upon the sick person in it.
Therefore, when you see it according to the manner I have described, from its whiteness and length, you must make the sick person sit facing the sun; and press his tongue with the instrument of which the description preceded; then fix the hook into the uvula and pull it downwards; and cut it with one of the two instruments that I have mentioned in the incision of the two tonsils.
And it is necessary that you do not cut from it, except that which is added beyond its natural limit without addition.
For if you cut from it more, you will injure the voice and speech.
Then after the incision, use that which we described in the incision of the two tonsils, and treat it until it is healed.
If, however, the patient is frightened by the incision, then it is necessary that you use ingenuity in cauterizing it without fear and trembling.
And the method of the cautery on it is not, unless with sharp water, and it is that you place the head of the sick person in your bosom. Then press his tongue with the instrument that we have said. Then take from the water that I told you about in the chapter on cauterization, and make with it quicklime, not extinguished, and place it neither thick nor thin, and fill with it the concavity of this instrument, of which this is the shape.
Let the concavity in which the medicine is placed be like the concavity of a spoon's radius.
And place the instrument with the medicine upon the uvula itself, and let the sick person lie on their side, so that saliva flows from their mouth from the internal medicine, so that nothing from it descends to their throat and harms them.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you shall apply it to the uvula for a half-hour, until you see that it is already blackened, and the biting of the medicine has settled.
And if you wish to take a pledget, and wrap it around the end of the radius, and instill the pledget with medicine, and insert the radius with the pledget into the cannula from above, until the pledget adheres to the uvula.
Do that many times, until you obtain what you want from the cauterization of the uvula. Then leave it; for it will dry and fall off after three or four days.
If you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it: and after the cauterization, wipe the surrounding uvula with a pledget soaked in butter, and cleanse whatever is around it of medicine; and wash it with cold water.
And treat it externally with embrocations, and internally with gargles, until it is healed.
This method of treatment is therefore healthier and further removed from danger.
And sometimes the uvula is treated with something finer than cautery and incision: it is treated with fumigation, in this manner. Take calamint, hyssop, oregano, rue, sseah, chamomile, and absinthe, and similar herbs: and gather them all, or some of them, in a pot, and immerse them in vinegar, and boil them: and let the pot be properly plastered with clay; and let there be a hole in the middle of the pot, over which a concave instrument is placed in this way.
Let it be made of silver, or bronze; and let the end, on which is the pomegranate, be inserted into the sick person's mouth, until the vapor ascends to the uvula over the tube, until the uvula is darkened considerably.
Then repeat over it many times, until it dries.
And beware, lest you perform this treatment at the beginning of the abscess's arrival. For it usually adds to the abscess; and it is not appropriate for you to do it unless at the decline of its hot abscess.
If however this instrument is not present to you, then take a tube, and place on its end the shell of an egg, so that the sick person's mouth is not burned; because the egg shell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth; and this is from good treatment with health.
Concerning the extraction of thorns and bones from the throat.
Chapter XXXVIII.
Often a bone, or a fishbone, or something else adheres in the throat.
Therefore, it is necessary to extract that which is apparent from them upon which the sight falls, after pressing the tongue with an instrument near the sun, and it is declared to you what is in the throat.
And what does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, it is necessary that you make the weak vomit before the food is digested in the stomach. For perhaps the attached thing will come out through vomiting.
Or let the weak person swallow a piece of turnip, or a lettuce root, or swallow a piece of dry bread; or let him take a piece of soft dry sea sponge: and tie it to a thread, then let him swallow it. When therefore it reaches the place where the spines or bone are, and is extracted, then it comes out.
If therefore it does not come out with what we have said, then use an instrument of lead according to this form.
Let it be thicker than a radius slightly, and at its extremity let there be a curve, which he should introduce into his throat with ease: and let him hold his head elevated upwards.
And let him beware of touching his epiglottis, so that coughing does not occur to him, and let him push with it the bone or spine, or let the physician introduce it with his hand.
But that the infirm introduce it is better, because of his knowledge of the place of the attached thing: and let him push downwards, or pull his hand with the instrument upwards, all that according to the amount prepared for him, until it emerges.
Concerning the extraction of the Leech.
Chapter XXXIX.
When the leech is medicated with what I have said in the division concerning treatment with medicines, and it does not help, then consider in the throat of the infirm in the presence of the sun, or in the sun after you have pressed his tongue with the instrument which I have told you.
If therefore your sight falls upon the leech, extract it with a small hook, or with slender forceps properly.
If it is not possible with them, then take a concave tube, and introduce it into the throat of the infirm almost to the gullet; then introduce into the concavity of the tube a heated iron with fire. Do this many times, and let the infirm abstain from water all day.
Then let him take a vessel full of cold water, and open his mouth in it; and let him wash his mouth with it, and not swallow anything from it; and let him move the water one hour after another with his hand. For the leech immediately, when it feels the water, falls.
If however it does not come out with what we have recounted, then fumigate the infirm person with fleas, or with asa, with the instrument that I have recounted in the fumigation of the uvula.
Do that many times, for it will fall itself.
And the method of operation in fumigation is, that you take a pot, in which there are live prunes with fire; and the pot is covered with a lid, in the middle of which is a hole; and the end of the instrument is placed in that hole.
Then direct the fumigation, and let the sick person put his mouth to the end of the cannula, and close his mouth, so that the fumigation does not escape, until you know that the fumigation has now reached the leech. For the leech will immediately fall.
If it does not fall, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the sick person endure thirst, and eat salt and garlic, and do not drink water.
Therefore, it is necessary that it depart with this treatment.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is extracted from the throat, when it falls upon it visibly.
It is similar to forceps, as you see: except that it has this curvature that is inserted into the throat. And its extremity is like the beak of a bird called a stork, in which there is the roughness of a file: when it grasps something, it does not release it at all.
Concerning the opening of abscesses, and their incision, and care in general.
Chapter XL.
Abscesses have many species, of many kinds, according to the recollection of them, namely one, and one in this book; and they are diverse in their perforation and incision according to two methods.
One of which is from the species of abscess in itself, and because it encompasses humors.
And the second method is due to the locations in which it occurs in the body.
Because an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the anus; and an abscess occurring in fleshy tissue is different from an abscess occurring in joints; and each of them has its own judgment from the operation.
And of abscesses, some should not be punctured unless after the maturation of the pus in them, and its completion; and of them, some should be punctured while they are raw, and do not mature according to completion.
Like abscesses that are near the joints.
Because when an abscess occurs near a joint, and its duration continues until that which is around it putrefies, perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted: therefore, it is a more proximate cause of the contraction of that limb.
Or is the abscess in a main limb: because if you delay its puncture until it matures, it will harm either the limb or through the limb the main part.
Or it is near the anus, therefore pierce it immaturely; and it is not fitting to pierce a raw abscess, not of complete digestion, except that which is near the anus, lest it putrefy deeply, and extend to the interior of the anus, and become a fistula, or become in its terminus that which is not healed.
It is necessary to know the time of puncturing an abscess that has matured according to its completion: and this is through the calming of pain, the recession of fever, and the decrease in redness, and pulsation, and sharpness of the abscess head, and the rest of the signs.
And it is necessary to make the same puncture in the lower place of the abscess, and of more prominent elevation; and let the puncture be penetrating in the length of the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands and feet, and in places of tendons, and chords, and nerves, and arteries, and finally in all equal places where there is no reflection.
In those, however, which are reflected, proceed with the puncture along that limb.
When, however, the abscess is in fleshy places, then it is better to leave its puncture until its digestion is confirmed, as we said according to its completion.
For if you puncture it before that, the course of the virus from it is prolonged, and there is much filth and nastiness, and perhaps its lips are hardened, and its depth.
And some abscesses are punctured according to the breadth of the body in necessity, or according to the need of the limb.
And it is necessary that you administer a small puncture in small abscesses, and a large puncture in large abscesses, or many incisions by similarity of the abscess's size.
And there are abscesses in which it is necessary to hollow out the skin, and to incise when it has already become like a cloth, and it has reached its end, that it is already dead; such as that which happens in many, like carbuncles and buboes.
And some of them are cut with an incision having three angles. And some of them are incised like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Like an inguinal abscess.
And among them are those in which a round incision, and a crescent-shaped incision, and similar incisions are administered.
That which does not have a head, such as mild superficial abscesses, should be pierced with a superficial or merely a flat perforation.
And when the abscess is large, and has already collected much pus, and you pierce it, you should not be hasty and extract all the pus at that hour; rather, extract only a part of it. Then plug the abscess until another day. Then extract some more of the pus again.
Do this repeatedly according to stages, until it comes out; especially if the sick person is weak in strength, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old decrepit person: for the animal spirit is often resolved from the animal with the expulsion of the pus; and perhaps the sick person dies, and you do not perceive it.
Therefore, beware of this chapter with great caution.
And after your perforation, with which you perforate these abscesses, it is necessary that you cleanse the wound: and consider. If therefore the wound of the abscess is small, or is a flat incision, then apply bandages of linen, or old cloth. And if the abscess is large, and the perforations are many, then it is necessary that you insert a bandage into every crevice, until they meet.
And if in the abscess you have cut off a part of the skin, or hollowed it out in a circle: then it is necessary that you fill it with old cloth, or as when a cloth is woven, or shaved with linen fluff without moisture; and bind it tightly until the third day.
Then remove it, and treat it with what is necessary of ointment, until it is healed.
If, however, a flow of blood occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and vinegar, after soaking a cloth in them; and place it repeatedly over the site of the blood flow.
If, however, the flow of blood persists, then it is necessary that you administer the regimen, and the treatment, and the powders, which I have described in many places of this book of ours, and from the division.
If, however, it is during the winter, and the site of the abscess is of many nerves: then it is necessary that you insert pads with warm wine and oil; and place them upon the areas.
And if it is in summer, and the areas are fleshy: then it is necessary that you place pads soaked in water and oil, or in wine and oil, and all of it cold. Until when the third day shall arrive, as we said, then it is necessary that you open the abscess, and cleanse it, and administer in its treatment what is suitable for it, until it is healed.
This, therefore, is what is necessary from the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally.
According to the path of distinction, however, I have already spoken of every abscess, how there is a path to its treatment in particular, chapter by chapter.
Chapter XLI.
Concerning the incision of abscesses that occur on the scalp.
Small abscesses occur on the scalp, and they are of the nature of nodules, which are contained within membranes that are like sacs to them: as if they were the plucked follicles of a hen.
And their kinds are many.
Some are fatty; and some contain moisture like mud; and some contain moisture like crushed foods and gruels; and similar to these; and some are hard and stony.
And in the incision of all of them, and their extraction, there is no fear, as long as in their incision the artery does not oppose.
And the operation in their incision is, that you should test them with an instrument, whose form comes after this, which is called an Intromissum, until you know what they contain.
For if it contains moisture, then cut it with a superficial longitudinal incision, in this form.
And begin with an incision from line b to line g.  When the moisture is evacuated, then scrape the container holding that moisture, and do not leave anything from it at all.
For it often returns if anything remains from it.
Then immerse the cotum in Egyptian unguent if it is present; and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with it, and leave it until another day. For it will corrode what remained of the container.
Then repeat over the cotum with Egyptian unguent a second, and a third time, and if you need it, until you are confident that nothing remains of the container, for then treat the wound with the unguent until it is healed.
If the abscess contains a fatty tumor, then cut it according to this form, the section having the shape of a cross; and insert hooks between the two sides of the incision, and scrape it from every side; and try your skill in extracting the tunic that contains it.
If however the artery resists you, then do what I will tell you afterwards.
And similarly do in an abscess, if it is stony, by the same incision and treatment, as we said.
And the incision on a stony abscess is easier, because there is little blood and moisture. And sometimes there are some of these abscesses that are on the head of certain people, in which there is no moisture at all.
This is because I incised an abscess on the head of an old woman, and found the abscess like a solid and hard stone, rough, white, which could not be broken; and if anyone struck it while throwing, they would wound themselves.
In other abscesses, however, occurring on the head, apart from these abscesses which occur on the heads of children, and at the roots of the ears, the incision of all of them should be a superficial incision.
Or make a perforation of them always from their lower part, so that the flow of pus is eased downwards.
Then treat them with whatever is appropriate for them in terms of treatment.
Concerning the incision of Strophulae on the neck.
Chapter XLII.
These abscesses often occur on the neck, and under the armpits, and in the groin: and sometimes they occur in the rest of the body.
And those that occur in the neck, are single, and multiple: and are generated from each other: and every strophula from them is within the tunic proper to it, as is in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have narrated.
And the varieties of these strophulae are many. And from them are stony ones; and from them are those containing moisture; and from them are malignant ones, which do not yield to treatment.
Therefore, in that one of them which you see of good disposition to the touch, whose proximity to the skin color is manifest, and which moves in every direction, and is not attached to the nerves of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, nor is deep: it is necessary to remove it by a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body, from line b to line g, and to scrape it from every side; and stretch the two lips of the skin with a hook, or with many hooks, if you need it, as we said in the abscesses of the head, and extract it gradually.
And it is a precaution that the vein or nerve not be cut.
And let the scalpel not be excessively sharp, lest it injure your hand with the incision, or disturb the sick person, and you cut what is unnecessary.
However, if you cut a vein or artery, or if it prevents you from the operation, then place in the wound crushed zegi, or some powders that stop bleeding, and bind the wound, and leave it until the sharpness of the abscess subsides, and the wound softens: or it starts and wants to rot.
For the blood is stopped then, therefore return to your operation, until you complete it.
Then examine with your index finger if any remaining small tumors or other small ones remain; therefore, cut them and cleanse them.
If there is a large vein in the root of the tumor, then it is necessary not to cut the tumor from its root; rather, it is necessary to tie it with a strong doubled thread and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old lint, which you have already soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then treat it.
If the entire tumor is cut, then it is necessary to bring together the two edges of the wound and suture it immediately, after you see that no excess remains.
However, if you see that excess remains due to the size of the tumor, then it is necessary to try to cut its root and remove it, and apply the suture we mentioned.
And whatever tumors contain humours, pierce it with a superficial incision, so that the place of its maturation may appear to you, and place the incision from that which follows below the body, as we have said.
Then, after the incision, apply dressings with ointment, and similar things, to corrode what remains of the corruption, until you see the wound is now clean.  Then treat it with a wound-healing ointment until it heals.
Chapter XLIII.
Concerning the excision of the epiglottis from an abscess occurring within the throat.
The ancients spoke of this excision of the epiglottis, and I have not seen anyone in our land who has performed it.  And this is the record of their words.
In those suffering from quinsy, the excision of the epiglottis should be avoided; since no benefit is obtained by it; because all the veins and lungs are weakened.
However, in those who have a hot abscess in the mouth, or throat, or both tonsils, when there is no disease in the channel, the excision of the epiglottis should be used, to avoid the loss caused by suffocation.
Therefore, when we wish to do it, we should cut the epiglottis under three, or four, circles of the epiglottis, with a small incision across, in the area between two circles, so that the incision is in the skin, not in the cartilage.  This place is suitable for the incision, since it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are distant from it.
If the surgeon is timid, then it is necessary to extend the skin of the throat with a hook. Then, he should cut the skin, until, when he reaches the channel, he should avoid the blood vessel if he sees any of them.
Then he should cut the skin, as we have described: and the incision of the channel is indicated by the phlegm that comes out of it, along with the rupture of it and the severance of the voice.
And leave the wound open for a time.
When therefore the time when suffocation is feared has passed, bring together the two lips of the wound in the skin, and sew it together itself alone, without the cartilage.
Then administer medicines that cause flesh to grow, until it is healed.
Says Albucasis. The summary of this account we have given is not applicable except when they see that some of these abscesses have already filled the throat of the sick person: and the sick person is raised up above death, and his breathing wishes to be cut off: they go to cut the epiglottis, so that the sick person may breathe over the wound by some respiration, and be saved from death: and for this reason they ordered the wound to be left open until the malice of the illness is consumed, and its malice is three days, and as if of itself: then they ordered the wound to be sewn up, and to be cared for, until it is healed.
And that which I saw myself is that a certain maid took a knife and thrust it into her throat, and cut a part of the lung's passage with it.
And I was called to treat her, and I found her moaning, like the moan of a dead person.
I therefore explored the wound, and found little blood that had escaped from the wound. Therefore, I believed that she had not cut a vein, nor a major artery, and wind was escaping from the wound.
I therefore hastened to and treated the wound until it was healthy, and nothing happened to the maid except hoarseness in her voice; and she returned after days to her better state.
Therefore, I say that there is no fear in the cutting of the epiglottis.
Concerning the incision of a hernia of the throat.
Chapter XLIV.
This abscess, called hernia of the throat, is a large abscess, according to the color of the body, and it is very common in women.
And it is in two ways: either it is natural, or it is accidental.
In the natural one, certainly, there is no ingenuity.
The accidental one, however, is in two ways, one of which is similar to fatty tumors. And the other way is similar to the abscess that arises from the knotting of an artery. And in it there is fear, and it is not fitting that presumption be made towards it with iron, unless it is small; and you should probe it and investigate it with an instrument. If therefore you find it similar to a fatty tumor, and it is not attached to any vein, then cut it, as tumors are cut, and extract it with what it contains from a cyst, if it is in a cyst; and if not, then examine its entire extent. Then treat the area with what is appropriate from the treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter XLV.
Concerning the incision on the nature of Tumors.
There are many types of tumors, and I have already mentioned all their types in the division; and it is necessary for me to explain here the difference between a tumor and a fistula, since it is a cause of doubt.
Therefore, I say that when a fistula is present, there is heat, fever, and severe pain, until the ebullition of the superfluity subsides and putrefaction is complete; then the fever and heat cease.
However, with a tumor, there is no heat, no fever, no pain, and it contains a kind of enclosed cyst, which is a proper sac for them, and it is according to the color of the body; and their beginning is like a chickpea, and it becomes like a batche (or similar object), large or small.
And they are of two types: either fatty or containing moisture. And the colors of moisture are many, as I have said in the division.
Therefore, when you come to treating a tumor, you should examine it and investigate it first with an instrument called an *intromissum*, in the way that its form will be shown in the chapter after this.
And the method of investigating abscesses and all tumors is that you should take this instrument and insert it into the moistest place you find in the abscess; and you should revolve your fingers with it gradually until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the skin. Then place your hand according to the measure of the size of the abscess.
Then withdraw the *intromissum* and consider what comes out in its wake.
If therefore running moisture of any color comes out, then cut it with a superficial incision, as I said in other abscesses.
And if no moisture comes out in the wake of the *intromissum*, then know that it is fatty; therefore cut over it with a cross-shaped incision, according to this form, as I taught you; and suspend it with hooks, and scrape the skin from all sides easily; and if possible, place a *kistin* upon it, to extract the healthy part along with the tumor.
If, however, the *kistin* is broken during the operation, and it is not possible to extract the healthy part, for this frequently happens, then remove it piece by piece until nothing remains of it.
For if some small or large part of it remains, the tumor will return most of the time.
If it overcomes you, and a little remains, then fill the wound at its completion with certain sharp corrosive powders, and bandage the wound, and place on it what sits warm over the abscess, and treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
If, therefore, the tumor is large, then sew its two lips together, and treat it with what causes it to heal.
If, however, a pulsating or non-pulsating vein is contrary to you, then hurry, and fill the place with crushed zegi, and leave it bound for two or three days, until the wound putrefies and the ebullition of blood ceases; then return to cutting that which remained of the tumor.
Chapter XLVI.
Concerning the external instruments for cutting and piercing.
Among them are the forms of probes, and they are of three kinds.
For among them there is a large one, and among them is a medium one, and among them is a small one.
Let them be made of tempered iron, with square ends, so that they readily enter the abscess.
And these are the forms of probes, and, no, that is, perforating ones: and one of them is called a *beris*, that is, a perforator: and they are of three kinds.
For among them there is a large one, and a medium one, and a small one.
They are suitable for investigating abscesses, and openings, and fistulas, and buboes by means of it, that which is between the bones.
And others are made round, smooth, and gentle, like large needles with which saddles are sewn, namely of citron, or of brass, or of iron, or of silver.
And the best of them are those made of citron.
And there are also experiences made of black lead, which are suitable for experimenting with fistulas, the concavity of which has reflections, so that they may be reflected with their gentleness with those reflections.
And they are again of three kinds. Because from them some are long, some medium, and some short, according to the quantity of the fistula's width and its constriction.
The shape of Hooks, and they are of many kinds.
Because some of them are simple, namely those which have a single curve: and they are of three kinds, as you see, Large, Medium, and Small.
And from them are blind hooks; and they are of three kinds.
And from them are hooks having two curves, and they are of three kinds: and from them are hooks having three curves, and they are of three kinds: and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the forms of scalpels, with which knots and abscesses are cut and scraped: and they are of three kinds: because from them there is a large, medium, and small one.
Their cutting edges are sharp, the other edges are not sharp: and they are not placed thus, except that a benefit is achieved with them in scraping knots, for fear of cutting a vein or nerve: and so that the sick person may be healed by them, and find a little peace from the burning pain they find in the scraping of an abscess.
They are made of metal, and are similar to a ray, with which alcohol is made: and at the broad end there is a hidden point of a spatula: and it runs inward and outward as desired, as you see.
The form of spatulas that are hidden between the fingers during the perforation of abscesses: and the sick do not perceive them: and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
The forms of cupping glasses with which the flow of blood is cut off: and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
They are made of metal or amber, round, a little in length, as you see: and let them be to the degree of thinness.
And it is necessary that these kinds of cupping glasses be large and small with you: so that you may cut off blood with speed when necessary, and then when medicine is not present.
However, do not use them for the cutting of blood in every part of the body, and do not use them except in fleshy places: such as the muscles of the thigh and hip, and the muscles of the arms, and the nipples, and the belly, and the groin, and similar fleshy, moist parts.
And sometimes from them are made other small instruments similar to hazelnuts, according to their form: with which blood is cut off when it flows from a phlebotomy site, or at an incision of a vein or artery: and sometimes they are made round in this shape.
Chapter XLVII.
Concerning the treatment of male nipples that resemble female nipples.
Sometimes the nipples of some men become inflated at the completion of sleep (that is, when they begin to be polluted in sleep) until they resemble female nipples: and they remain foul-looking, ulcerated.
Therefore, whoever abhors it, must cut above the nipples with a crescent-shaped incision, according to this shape from line b. to line g.  Then scrape away all the fat. Afterwards, fill the wound with a medicament that promotes healing. Then bring together the two lips of the wound with sutures, and treat it until it heals.
If, however, the nipple inclines downwards and softens due to its size, as happens with women, then it is necessary to slit it on its two upper sides with two similar crescent-shaped incisions, each one meeting the other at the ends of both, until there is a larger line containing a smaller one, according to this shape from line b to line g. Afterwards, scrape away the skin between the two lips, and remove the fat, and apply what we said about sutures and powders, which is necessary for treatment, until it heals.
If, however, you do not complete the incision that you ought to make, because of the patient's discomfort, or because of the flow of blood, then you ought to fill the wound with wool soaked in corrosive ointment, and leave it until it corrodes what remains of the fat. Then treat it until it heals.
Concerning the perforation of an abscess, which occurs beneath the nipple.
Chapter XLVIII.
These abscesses which occur beneath the nipple are of the nature of hard strophules, which contain cysts, and of those which contain humors.
Those of them which contain humors must be incised with a lunate incision, following this form, from line b to line g.  When, therefore, all that is within the abscess is evacuated with pus, then fill it with old wool and leave it until another day.
Then the wool should be removed, and it should be treated with a suitable ointment for it, until it is healed.
If the abscess is of the nature of knotted strophules, then incise over it, as we have said in the section on strophules, equally.
If, however, the wound extends so as to emit pus, then apply a cautery to it, according to what we have said in what has preceded.
Chapter XLIX.
Concerning the incision of an abscess that occurs due to artery and vein.
When an artery bursts, and the skin above it closes, an abscess often results.  And similarly, this happens with veins, so that an abscess and swelling occur in them.
And the signs by which one knows that an abscess and swelling are due to artery and vein are these: for when the abscess is due to an artery, it is long, aggregated deep within the body, and when you press on the abscess with your fingers, you feel as if there is a creaking sound to it.
And that which is due to a vein is round, on the surface of the body.
And incision upon this abscess is an error, and especially that which is under the armpits, and in the groins, and in the neck, and in many places of the body: and it is very large. Therefore, one should avoid treating it with iron, and that which is also from them in the extremities, or in the head, should be avoided.
Therefore, for that which is from them from the swelling of the artery's opening, make an incision in the skin lengthwise. Then open the incision with hooks, then the artery; and free it from its coverings, until it is exposed.
Then introduce a needle beneath it, and make it penetrate to the other side, and tie the artery with a double thread in two places, according to what I taught you in the extraction of the two arteries that are in the temples.
Then pierce the place, which is between the two ligatures, with a lancet, until all the blood within it is discharged, and the abscess is resolved.
Then administer treatment that produces pus, until the ligatures fall away; afterwards, treat it with appropriate ointments, until it heals.
If the abscess be due to the incision of a vein, then you should hold with your hand whatever you are able of the abscess with the skin. Then insert a needle beneath the place you held with your hand, and make it penetrate; and let there be a double thread on it, until you draw it out from the other side. Then bind the abscess with a good ligature, according to what I told you in the ligature of the grape, with two ends of thread from two sides.
If, however, you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then insert another needle with another thread beneath the entire abscess at the incision of the needle; press, and tie your threads in four places.
Then cut the abscess in its middle, until that which is within it comes out; then cut away the excess skin, and leave that which is bound from it.
Then place upon it a poultice which you have soaked in wine and oil. Then administer the treatment, which is done with bandages and ointments, until it is healed.
Chapter L.
Concerning the abscess that arises from the twisting of nerves.
Just as an abscess occurs in arteries and veins, so also an abscess occurs in nerves: this occurs in them through blows, or through excessive exertion, and similar causes; and it occurs most often in the areas of the helpers [muscles], or the heel, and in every place where joints move; and it is a severe abscess.
Its color is similar to the rest of the body, and it is in many cases without pain; and when it is squeezed forcefully, the infirm person feels a sensation similar to numbness in it.
And it is not a deep-seated collected abscess, but rather is under the skin, and it moves to every part, and does not advance forward, or back to the rear.
Therefore, those that are in the joints should not be incised with a knife. For perhaps contraction will result.
However, in that which is on the head or forehead, split the skin with a spatula. If therefore it is a small abscess, then hold it with forceps and cut it away from the root.
And if it is large, then suspend it with a hook, and scrape it; then remove it, and close the wound with sutures; and treat it until it heals.
Chapter LI.
Concerning the incision of an abscess that occurs in the belly.
Many people frequently have in their bellies, and in other parts of their bodies, growths that are called Fungalia, by reason of their similarity to fungi.
Their root is subtle, and their head is thick, and their lips are turned inward. And some of them are small, and some of them grow very large.
And indeed I have seen a man, and in his belly there were two growths that were more similar to fungi than to anything else; there was no difference between them, white, with small roots, and their lips turned inward, and split open, and moisture flowed from them constantly.
Therefore, I cut them open, and found one weighing about 10 and 8 ounces and the other about 6 ounces.  And the operation for cutting them is this: if the patient is moist, and the color of the growth is white, moist, with subtle roots, then cut it with a wide scalpel, and have cauteries present in the fire.
For often a great deal of blood is expelled at its incision; therefore, if the blood overwhelms you, then cauterize it quickly.
If you see a weak and fearful patient from the incision with iron, then take a well-made lead thread and bind it with it: the method for which we described, and leave it for two days.
Then add to the lead binding. Therefore, do not cease binding the lead, whenever it presses on the growth, until it is severed, and falls off by itself without effort.
If however the growth is of thick root, then in its incision there is something of fear and dread, especially that which is in the belly.
However, it is possible to cut half of it. Then cauterize it, so that it does not return.
Then treat it until the wound heals.
And beware lest you presume to cut a growth that is dark coloured, of few senses, and foul in appearance: for it is indeed a cancerous abscess, and I shall come to a discussion of Cancer after this.
Chapter LII.
Concerning the treatment of the navel's eminence.
The navel's eminence arises from many causes. Or from fissures of the abdomen, which is above the belly, and something protrudes from it, either wind, or an intestine, as happens in other ruptures. Or from blood that flows from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned. Or from wind constricted within it.
If however it is due to a fissure of the abdomen, and the protrusion is of this sort, then the colour of the abscess is similar to the colour of the body, and it is soft, without pain, and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the protrusion of an intestine, then its location, along with what we have described, is of more diverse variety. When therefore you press it with your fingers, it hides; then it returns, and perhaps there is a sound with it; and it greatly enlarges at the entrance to the bath and with strenuous exertion.
If however it is due to moisture, it is soft, then it does not hide when you press it with your hand, nor does it contract, nor does it increase.
If however it is due to blood, then with these signs the abscess appears a blackness from the clivus.
If however it is due to generated flesh: then the abscess is severe, large, hard, and fixed to one amount.
If however it is due to windiness, then its touch is soft.
And the operation on it is, that you should consider then, if the eminence of the navel is due to blood of the artery, or vein, or windiness: then you ought to abstain from its treatment. For in it is fear and terror, just as I taught you in the chapter, in which I said abscesses due to artery and vein.
If the eminence of the navel is due to the intestine or zirb, then you should instruct the sick person to hold their breath and stand outstretched. Then mark with ink around the entire navel. Thereafter, instruct him to lie on his back between your hands. Then surround with a broad spatula the area around the navel, over the place you marked with ink. Then extend the middle of the abscess upwards with a large hook. Then tie it tightly around with thread, or with silk cord, a strong binding; and let the knot of the ligature be firm.
Then open the middle of the abscess, extended above the ligature. If you find it already caught by the ligature, then remove the ligature, and insert your index finger into it, and push the intestine back into the belly; and if you find a hernia, then take it with a hook, and cut away its excess.
If however, it is an artery or vein, then go around it a lot. Then return to your operation. And take two needles, and insert into them two strong threads; and insert the two needles into the circumference you made around the abscess in the form of a cross, which you make pierce through.
Then tie off the abscess in four places above the needles; and if you wish, remove the needles, and leave the place until the stretched flesh rots; and it falls away by itself; or cut it when it becomes thin and rots.
Then treat it with what is necessary of ointments, until it is healed.
If the eminence of the navel is from flesh that is protruding there, or from moisture, then it is necessary to hollow out the abscess, as we have said. And extract the blood, or moisture, which you find in it.
Then treat it with what solidifies the place.
Chapter LIII.
Concerning the treatment of CANCER.
We have already discussed the classification of cancers, and how to treat them with medicines; and we must be cautious when treating them with iron, so as not to cause ulceration; and we have discussed cancer generated in the womb, and the precautions to take in treating it.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, such as cancer in the breasts or in the thigh, and similar parts, by removing the entire growth, especially when it is small and beginning.
However, when it is old and large, one should not approach it.
For I could not cure anyone of it, nor did I see anyone before me who succeeded in doing so.
And the treatment for it, when located as we said, is to proceed and free the afflicted from the black cholera at times. Then, if you find it with evident fullness, phlebotomize it.
Then prepare the afflicted with the preparation in which they will be positioned for the operation.
Then insert into the Cancer the hooks that suit it. Afterwards, hollow it out from every side with the skin, according to examination, until nothing remains of its roots: and let the blood run, and do not cut it quickly, but rather squeeze the places, and wipe away all the thick blood with your hand, or with any suitable instrument there.
If, however, a contrary flow of thick blood from the incision of an artery or vein occurs, then cauterize the vein until the blood is stopped. Then treat it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of dropsy.
Chapter LIII. We have already recounted in the division of the species of dropsy, and how the collection of water occurs: and the signs of every species, and its treatment with medicines.
And that which is cured with iron is only the species of ascites alone: and it is not approached with iron for the species of tympanitis: nor for the species of hyposarca at all; for that is mortal.
When you have already treated this species of dropsy, tympanitis, with medicines, and your treatment has not availed: then consider. For if weakness has already overtaken the sick person, or there is another illness with him besides dropsy, such as if there is coughing, or looseness of the belly, and things similar to them: then beware of treating him with iron; because there is fear of that.
If, however, you see the sick person of exuberant strength, and there is no illness with him besides dropsy alone: and he is neither a child nor an old man: then the method of operation in him is that you raise the sick person standing between your hands, and a servant behind him press his belly with his hand, driving the water to the lower part of the chest.
Then take a pointed, spiny spatula, according to this form. Let it be of two pointed parts, long, with sharp ends, like a spatula. However, let there be something of shortness gradually, so that you do not proceed with it in the operation all the way to the intestines, and harm him.
Then consider. For if the generation of dropsy is from the intestines, then it is necessary that you extend the incision from the navel a quantity of three fingers, downward in a direct line above the pubic bone.
If however the generation of dropsy is because of illness of the liver: then let your incision be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if its generation is because of the spleen, then let the incision be on the right side: and it is also not necessary that the incision be on the side on which the sick person wishes to lie, so that superfluities do not run to that weak place.
Then pierce through the entire skin with the instrument. Then introduce it into that incision, and raise your hand with the spatula between the skin, and if you do, as if you are scraping it, and let the amount that is scraped be the quantity of a fingernail, or approximately that.
Then pierce, if you do so, until the spatula reaches the empty space: and it is the space of water. Then draw it out, and introduce into the hole the instrument, which has this form. And it is similar to a cannula: it is made of silver, or bronze, or brass, smooth, polished: to which at its lower part is a small hole, and on its sides are three holes, two on one side, and one on the other side: and the end of it is cut, according to the manner of cutting a reed, as you see: on its upper end is a ring.
For when the instrument arrives at the water, then it itself descends immediately upon the instrument, therefore evacuate from the water in an hour a medium quantity.
Because if you evacuate from it more than is necessary, perhaps the sick person dies in an hour through the resolution of his animal spirit; or a swoon occurs to him which approaches death.
But evacuate from it according to the quantity of its virtue, and according to what the dispositions of the sick person indicate to you by the strength of his pulse, and by his condition.
Then remove the instrument, and retain the water.
That is, because it is immediately retained on account of the skin that retains the opening which is above the sifac; of which I spoke, that you should perforate it according to that manner.
Then again use the instrument on the second day, if you see the sick person tolerate it; and also extract a small quantity from the water.
Do this for several days without fear, and guard yourself from error, until only a little remains of the water.
However, if you fear for the sick person, and you leave out a great deal of water, then treat him with burial in hot sand, and much sweat in a bath and sun, and let him endure thirst; and treat him with drying medicines, until he is healed; and cauterize the stomach, liver, and spleen, after the extraction of the water, according to what I have described in that which came before.
Concerning the treatment of infants born with their urinary passages not perforated.
Chapter LV.
When someone emerges from the womb of their mother, and the opening of their urethra is not perforated, or is a narrow opening, or is in the wrong place.
Therefore, it is necessary to help with its perforation immediately when it is born, with a very fine Spatumili, according to this method.
Then place a fine leaden nail in the opening, and tie it; and hold it for three days, or four.
When, therefore, it wants to emit urine, it is removed, and it urinates. Then replace it.
And if you do not place lead, it is now possible that the urine which flows over the place will not allow it to close.
However, the one whose opening is constricted is treated with lead, as we have said, for many days, until it is enlarged.
But those in whom the opening is in an improper place are injured. Because in those who are born, the opening is at the end of the penis: therefore, they cannot urinate forward until they raise their penis upward with their hand. Nor is there generation for him, because the semen cannot reach the uterus in a straight line: and it is a very foul sickness.
And the method of treatment in him is that the sick person should be placed on his back. Then extend his penis with a strong pull of your left hand, and cut off the head of the penis from the location of the glans, with a sharp lancet or a pointed scalpel; just as you would cut a reed, or as you would shave something, so that its middle comes out like the head of the penis, and that the opening falls in the middle, as is necessary.
And beware of blood flow during your operation. For it often happens. Therefore, apply to it whatever stops the blood, and treat the wound until it is healed.
Concerning matters affecting the foreskin and the head of the penis, and concerning its corruption, blackness, and adhesion.
Chapter LVI.
These affections frequently occur in the penis, and there is an eminence of foul flesh.
And from it there is another that is malignant, and another that is not malignant.
Therefore, the non-malignant one should be suspended with a fine hook, and cut away until the whole is cleansed. Then place upon it a poultice soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then treat it afterwards with diachylon ointment, until it is healed.
If, however, it is malignant, of foul color, then it is necessary to apply a cautery to it after its incision and scraping.
If, however, the albather is in the foreskin of an uncircumcised captive, and part of the albather is within the foreskin and part outside, then it is necessary to remove the albather that is inside first, until it is consolidated, then treat the external part: because when you treat them simultaneously, the foreskin is not safe from being pierced.
And such things also occur in the testicles, and the foreskin, with blackness and corruption. Therefore, it is necessary to cauterize all that is already blackened, and that which is about to be corrupted, or is already corrupted.
Then apply a line over it after that with honey that has already been mixed with the flour of pomegranate bark, and ground, sifted herbs.
Then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
If, however, a flow of blood occurs, then apply the cautery with the lunar cautery, according to this form.
For the cautery is helpful for two conditions at once, namely for the flow of blood, and a wound when it becomes corrupted.
If the head of the penis is eroded, and the whole thing is destroyed within an hour, then it is necessary to insert a lead cannula into the urethra, so that the patient may urinate over it.
In the adhesion of the foreskin to the head of the penis, and this adhesion indeed occurs only in one whose foreskin is healthy, and circumcision is not required upon him: and sometimes adhesion occurs because of a wound, or an abscess: therefore it is necessary to exfoliate it with a blunt Spatumili, until the ligament is loosened, and the head of the penis is freed from every part.
If it is difficult to distinguish them exquisitely, then it is necessary to exfoliate somewhat from the head of the penis, and from the foreskin. This is because when the foreskin is thin, perhaps it is perforated quickly due to its thinness.
Then separate the foreskin and the head of the penis with a thin linen cloth that has already been moistened in cold water, so that they do not adhere again.
Then treat it with astringent wine, until it is consolidated.
Chapter LVII.
Concerning the cleansing of infants, and the treatment of errors that befall them.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity, like other wounds; however, since it is from our operation, by our will, and its use is proper to boys, it is necessary for us to mark it with a better operation and a lighter path leading to health.
Therefore, I say that the ancients did not speak of circumcision in any of their books, since they do not use it in their laws, and it is only what we have acquired through experiment.
This is because I have found the majority of artisans and phlebotomists using cleansing, circumcising with a razor and with scissors; and they use leeches, and ligation with thread, and incision with a nail.
And indeed I have already tested all these methods, and have not found a better cleansing than with scissors, and ligation with thread.
Because in cleansing with a razor, the skin often escapes. Because the foreskin has two layers: therefore perhaps the upper layer of skin is cut, and the lower layer remains; therefore, it is necessary to make another incision to cut the other, and future pain will result. And with the cleansing done by leeches, that is, digital, which the surgeons use, the incision of the extremity of the male member is not secured; because perhaps it enters into its opening.
In cleansing with a claw, perhaps the iron slips out and spoils your operation; and the skin of a child is short-lived by nature, and they are often born in such a way that they do not need cleansing; and I have already seen that.
However, cleansing with shears, and ligature with thread, has shown me a good experiment; because shears are proportional to the incision; because the sharpness that is above is like the sharpness that is below. When therefore you press your hand, the two sharpnesses are proportioned, and they cut according to one operation at one time; and a compression of a similar shape is made, containing the skin of the penis from every side, with which no error falls at all.
And the proper method of operation is, that you make the boy, especially if he is one of those who understand a little, estimate that you do not do anything except tie a thread to his penis only; and you leave him until another day.
Then make him rejoice and be glad in every way possible for you, from that which his understanding receives. Then make him stand between your prepared hands, and let him not be seated; and conceal the shears in your sleeve, or under your foot, so that the boy sees them not at all, nor anything of the instruments.
Then introduce your hand into his penis, and blow into the skin, and pull it upwards until the head of the penis emerges. Then cleanse it of the dirt that has accumulated on it.
Then tie the marked place with a double thread. Afterwards tie a second ligature a little lower than that. Then hold the place of the lower ligature with your index finger and thumb, applying a good grip, and cut between the two ligatures. Then quickly lift the skin upward and pull out the head of the rod. Then allow the blood to run slowly (for this is better) and place powder on top; and over the powder, a cloth on which is spread the yolk of a hard-boiled egg in rose water, stirred with good fresh rose oil: and leave it on top until the next day; and treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
The shape of the scissors for cleansing is this.
Let the two ends be blunt, and be sloping; so that the branch of the nail does not protrude from them; and let the length of the two sharpened parts be the same as the length of the two handles, equally.
An error, however, occurring in cleansing is: because perhaps the inner skin is entirely or partially escaped at the incision.
Therefore, it is necessary to extend it from your hour with your fingernail before the place becomes an abscess; and cut it according to equality.
If you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then pull it out with a hook and cut it.
However, if three days have passed, and that which is under the rod remains swollen, an abscess: then leave it until the abscess is mature, and scrape it off easily, and cut it according to what is prepared for you, and beware of the head of the rod. For it will not harm it.
Therefore, treat it with that which consolidates a wound from the powders, which we have described in the treatise on powders; and if it is cut from the skin beyond measure, and extends to the upper parts, it will not harm it even then.
Therefore, treat it with what we have said, until it is healed.
Chapter LVIII.
On the extraction of retained urine in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder occurs from an obstruction caused by a stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or a growing flesh, and similar things to those that we have mentioned concerning methods of treatment in the division.
And if the urine is not released, and you see that its retention is due to a stone that is already in the neck of the bladder, then it is necessary that he be weak, bent over on his knees. Then let his feet be placed on his back, and let him stretch his belly from the upper part of his back, and let the weak man strain his breath as much as possible. Thus, pressure is applied to the neck of the bladder for the expulsion of the stone to the rear: therefore, the urine is released.
If, however, it is not released with what we have said, and the matter is severe upon the sick person, then it is necessary that you administer its extraction with an instrument, which is called a syringe, of which this is the form: it should be of silver, and be thin, smooth, concave, like the quill of a bird in its thinness, and long like one and a half palms; with a thin tube at its head.
And the method of the urine's exit through it is that you take a doubled thread, and tie wool or tow, a good ligature, to its end; and insert the thread's end into the lower part of the syringe; and cut with scissors if any wool protrudes, but insert it into the tube with force, as if hoisting it up.
Then anoint the syringe with oil, or with butter, and the white of an egg; and make the sick person sit on a seat, and embrocate the bladder and his penis with moist oils, and olive oil, and warm water.
Then insert the syringe into the penis with ease, until it reaches the root of the penis.
Then double it upward from the navel's area.
Then drive the syringe within it, until it reaches the bladder, and the sick person senses that it has reached the empty space: and this is not done in this manner unless because the passage, in which it travels, is winding.
Then extend the thread with wool gradually. For the urine will follow the wool.
Then pull it out, and cause the urine to run through the urinary path: and again use the syringe, and do not cease to do it, until the bladder is emptied, and the sick person finds relief.
Concerning the quality of bladder clysterization with a clepsydra.
Chapter LIX.
When a sore occurs in the bladder, or blood clots in it, or pus is collected in it, and the force is to instill water and medicines into it: then it should be done with an instrument called a Clepsydra, and this is its form.
It is indeed deaf, that is, closed. And in it are three openings, two on one side and one on the other side, as you see.
And the hollow place, in which that which propels it is situated, should be according to the quantity that fills it without addition, until when something is pulled with it, humidity is drawn out; and when it is propelled, it expels it to a distance; according to which the clepsydra does, by which naphtha, that is, Greek fire, is projected in the battles of war.
Therefore, when you wish to project the moisture into the bladder, introduce the end of the clepsydra into the moisture, and pull with that which propels it upwards, for the moisture is drawn into the concavity of the clepsydra.
Then introduce its end into the tube, according to what we have described in the syringe. Then propel the moisture with that which propels it, for that moisture arrives at the bladder immediately, so that the sick person feels it.
The form of a fine enema, by which the bladder is enematized.
Let it be made of silver, or brass: whose head be higher, similar to a small alms-dish, and beneath it a sinus into which a ligature falls.
Then take a ram's bladder, and put into it the moisture with which you wish to enema the bladder. Then tie it between two sinuses with a strong ligature of double thread; and warm that moisture a little over the fire.
Then introduce the end of the enema into the tube, afterwards press your hand on the bladder with a fitting pressure, until the patient feels that the moisture has reached the bladder.
If however a bladder is not present, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it, and pierce it all around its circumference. Then introduce a strong thread into those openings, as they revolve. Then close the circle, as a flask is closed; after you put in it what you wish of the moistures, and oils, and waters.
Then tie it to the instrument, and do with it as you did with the bladder by pressing with your hand, until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter LX.
Concerning the extraction of the Stone.
We have already spoken in the division of the species of Stone, and its treatment with medicines; and I have spoken of the difference between a stone generated in the kidneys and the bladder, and the signs of each of them; and I have spoken of the stone in which operation is done with the hand and incision: and the stone generated in the bladder properly is that which is fixed in the urethra; and I am recounting it with clear explanation and brevity.
Therefore, I say that a stone generated in the bladder happens most often in children.
And among its signs is that the urine departs from the bladder like water in its subtlety, and white sand appears in it, and the infirm person rubs his urethra, and suffers with it, and hangs down often. Then it becomes erect, and the anus emerges with it in many of them.
And the cure is easy for children, as long as they do not reach fourteen years of age. And it is difficult in the elderly. However, in young people it is moderate, in that it lies between those two: and the cure is easier for the one whose stone is larger; and if it is small, the cure is conversely more difficult.
Therefore, when we come to the treatment, it is necessary first to cleanse the sick person with an enema, which will remove all the faeces that are in their intestines; for it sometimes prevents the discovery of the stone during the examination.
Then let the sick person be taken up with their feet, and shaken, and moved downwards, so that the stone may descend to the depths of the bladder, or jump out of a high place at times.
Then have him sit between your hands prepared, and let his hands be under his thighs, so that the whole bladder may be inclined downwards.
Then examine him and touch him externally.
If therefore you feel a stone in the space, then immediately proceed with the incision over it.
But if it does not fall under your touch at all, then it is necessary that you wipe your index finger with the oil of your left hand, if the sick person is a boy; or the middle finger if he is a full grown youth; and insert it into his anus, and examine for the stone, until it is under your finger; and turn it gradually towards the neck of the bladder.
Then press upon it with your finger, and drive it outwards against the place whose incision you desire; and order a servant to press the bladder with his hand, and order another servant to extend the testicles upwards with his right hand; and with his other hand, to remove the skin that is beneath the testicles in the part near the place where the incision is.
Then insert the Spatumile, which has this shape. And find it in the space between the anus and the testicles, and not in the middle, towards the left flank; and let the incision be over the stone, and your finger be in the anus; and let the incision be transverse, so that the incision is wide externally and narrow internally, according to the quantity that it is possible for the stone to emerge from it, not larger.
For perhaps the finger, which is in the anus, compresses the stone at the incision, and it emerges without difficulty.
And know that the stone, which has angles and edges, makes its exit difficult because of this.
And if it is smooth, like a gland, and round, its exit is easy.
Therefore, to the one with angles and edges, add a fissure.
If it does not emerge thus, then it is necessary to devise something over it: either to string over it with a well-formed instrument, the tip of which is like the Moon, that it may string over the stone and not escape from it; or to introduce a slender instrument, curved at the end, beneath it.
If however you cannot do so over it, then widen the opening a little. If something from the blood overcomes you, cut it away with a zegi.
If however the stones are more than one, then drive the largest first towards the bladder's opening. Then cut over it. Afterwards, drive the small one after that; and similarly do if there are more than two.
If however it is very large, then it is ignorance to cut over it with a large incision. For it happens to the sick person one of two things, either that he dies, or that a constant dripping of urine happens to him; because the place does not completely heal.
But administer its expulsion, until it comes out; or devise means for its fracture with forceps, until you extract it piece by piece.
When therefore you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with frankincense, and aloe, and dragon's blood; and bind it, and place over it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or rose oil, and cold water; as is done for a hot abscess.
Then lay the sick person on his back, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day. When you have therefore loosened it, embrocate the place with water and much oil.
Then treat it with diapason ointment, and basilicon ointment, until it is healed.
If however a hot abscess, and corrosions, and similar things, happen added to the wound: such as if blood clots in the bladder, and prevents urine from passing; and this is recognized from the passing of blood with the urine. Therefore insert your finger into the wound, and extract that blood. For if it remains, it provokes corruption of the bladder and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and apply whatever is suitable for treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary in the entire course of treatment, to bind the two hips, and apply, because of the medicines that are placed on the place.
If a stone is small, and is in the channel of the penis, and is fixed in it, and the exit of urine is hindered, then treat it with what I described before you reach the incision. For often you will be spared from incision by this treatment, for I have already tested it.
And it is to take a perforator of iron, of which this is the form, triangular end, sharp, fixed in wood.
Then take a thread, and tie the penis with it under the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then introduce the piercing iron into the penis with ease, until the perforator reaches the stone itself: and turn the perforator with your hand on the stone gradually: and try to pierce it, until you make it penetrate from the other side.
For the urine is discharged immediately.
Then press your hand upon that which remains of the stone from the outer part of the penis. For it breaks, and emerges with the urine, and the sick person is healed.
If however this treatment is not prepared for you, due to something preventing it: then tie a thread below the stone, and another thread above the stone. Then cut above the stone on the penis itself between the two ligatures. Then extract it. Afterwards, loosen the ligature, and cleanse the congealed blood that forms in the wound.
And the ligature of the thread below the stone is not necessary, except so that it does not return to the bladder; and the other ligature on top, except so that when the thread is loosened after the stone's exit, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound; and for that reason it is necessary for you to elevate the skin upwards when you tie the upper thread, so that it returns to its proper position and covers the wound, as we have said.
Chapter LXI.
Concerning the extraction of the stone in women.
Little stone is generated in women.
However, if a stone happens to any of them, then the cure is difficult, and it is forbidden due to many methods.
One of them is: that the woman is perhaps a virgin.
And the second is, because you do not find a woman who reveals herself to a physician, when she is chaste, or having husbands.
The third is, because you do not find a woman well-knowing this art, especially the operation with the hand.
And the fourth is the distance from the stone's location: wherefore it needs a deep incision, and in that is fear.
If necessity provokes it, then it is necessary that you take a female physician, well-knowing: and little is found.
If truly you keep her private, then seek a chaste, subtle physician, and present a female midwife well-versed in matters of women, or a woman who hints at a part of this art: make her present, and especially to her, let her do all that you command her from the inquiry concerning the stone.
First of all, that she should see if the woman is a virgin: then it is necessary that she insert her finger into her anus, and seek the stone.
If therefore she finds it, she should hold it under her finger, and then command the midwife to insert her finger into the weak woman's vagina, and inquire about the stone, after she places her left hand on the bladder, and compresses it with a good compression.
If therefore she finds the stone, then it is necessary that she gradually move it from the opening of the bladder downwards with all her strength, until she reaches it with the root of the thigh.
Then let her cut upon it at the midpoint of the vulva near the root of the thigh, from whichever side it is prepared for her, and she feels the stone in that part: and let her finger not be removed from the stone, holding it tight; and let the incision be small at first. Then let her insert a ray upon that incision.
Then let her insert a ray upon that incision. If therefore she feels the stone, then let her add to the incision according to the proportionate quantity that the stone emerges from it.
And know that the species of stones are many. For among these there is a small one, and a large one, a smooth one and a rough one, a long one, and a round one having branches.
Therefore know its species, so that this may be shown to you about that which you wish.
If therefore the blood overcomes you, then add in the place of the crushed zegi, and hold it for one hour, until the blood is cut off.
Then return to your operation, until the stone emerges: and do as I have prepared for you from the instruments, which I have spoken of in the extraction of stones in men, so that you may help yourself by them in your operation.
If, however, the flow of blood overcomes you, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery that has been cut: then place powder upon the place, and bind it with suitable ligatures: and do not change it: and leave the stone, and do not extract it. For perhaps the weak woman will die. Then treat the wound.
When, therefore, the sharpness of the blood subsides after days, and the place putrefies, then return to your operation, until the stone emerges.
Chapter LXII.
Concerning the incision of a watery hernia.
A watery hernia is not present unless there is a collection of moisture in a white sifac, which is under the skin containing the testicle: and it is called sifac.
And sometimes it is in its own membrane, which nature has prepared for it in the part of the testicle, until it is corrupted: which is another testicle, and the skin of the testicle is between the white sifac, which we have said: and this does not occur except rarely.
And the generation of this hernia indeed is from the weakness which happens to the testicles: wherefore this matter is poured out upon them. And sometimes it happens from a blow to the testicles.
And this moisture has many colors. For either its color is sloping toward yellowness, or it is blood-red, or foul, black, or watery white, and that is what is most.
And the signs by which it is known where the collection of water is, are these. For if it is in the white sifac which we said, then the abscess is round, somewhat the length of an egg; and the testicle is not apparent, since the moisture encompasses it on every side.
And if the moisture is in the proper membrane to itself, then the abscess is round with a part of an egg, and because of this, a man thinks it is another egg.
And if the moisture is between the skin of the testicles and the white sifac, then it falls under the sense.
Therefore, when you wish knowledge of the color of the moisture, then experience the color of the moisture in the abscess with the inserted square, whose form preceded. Therefore, according to that which emerges in the trace of the Intromiss, judge concerning that which is between it.
When, therefore, we come to treatment with iron, then it is necessary that you instruct the sick person, that he be bled if possible for him, and you see his body full.
Then he shall be laid on his back upon a somewhat high object, and place many cloths beneath him.
Then sit you upon his left side, and instruct the attendant to sit on his right side, who shall extend his rod to one of the two sides of the skin of the testicles, and to the part of the navel of the belly.
Then take a broad spatula, and cut the skin of the testicles from the middle lengthwise, nearly to the pubic bone; and let the incision be according to the straightness of an equidistant line that divides the skin of the testicles, until it reaches the white sifac that contains it; and scrape it and observe it, or beware of this, so that you remove it; and let your scraping, with which you scrape it, be on the part that hangs more with the egg; and subtly examine it with the scraping, to the extent that is possible for you.
Then pierce the sifac full of water with a wide perforation, and extract all the water.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with hooks, and extend the sifac upwards, and do not touch the skin of the testicles containing it; and cut the sifac however you are able to cut it, either with its entirety or in pieces, especially its thin side. For if you do not carefully examine it, there is no security in its incision, lest the water return.
If, however, the egg protrudes outward from its skin during your operation: then when you have completed the incision of the sifac, return it. Then aggregate the two lips of the testicular skin with sutures.
Then treat it like other wounds, until it heals.
If you find a corrupted egg, black with other discoloration, then you must tie off the vessels, which are the suspensories, fearing a flow of blood.
Then cut off the testicle with the suspensory, and remove the egg: afterwards treat it with what we have said.
If indeed water is aggregated in two parts, then know that there are two branches: therefore cut the other part according to what you did to the other equally.
And if it is equal to you, so that it is one operation, then do so: afterwards wipe away the blood. And introduce into the incisions wool soaked in oil, or in rose oil: and place outside wool which you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread it over the two testicles, and the belly area: and place over it doubled cloths, and they are cushions: and tie them over with a bandage, which has six according to this form.
Then take two cloths, and sew them together, and fill them with dyed wool, according to this likeness: and make the ends with which they are pressed from cotton, or damp wool: and they are aggregated with a constriction in the lumbar region of the breeches: and one end takes over the anus up to the back, and the second takes to the side of the comb: and two ends over the two thighs below, and two other ends over the two groins, and the whole constriction is aggregated in the lumbar region of the breeches.
And sometimes this branch is artificially treated with a cautery at the incision site with an iron: and it is that you take a thin, knife-like cautery, and cut with it the skin of the testicles: and it is heated, as we have described: until when a whitish substance containing water is revealed: then take the cautery, according to this form.
It resembles the Greek letter.
Then pierce it with it, and it is heated, until all the moisture comes out.
Then stretch the sifac with hooks, and scrape it with a heated fine cautery, and cut it as much as possible, until you remove all of it.
And be careful, from the egg, lest the fire touch it.
And know that this operation is safer, and further from blood flow, and better than the operation with section.
If, however, opposition occurs to you at the time of the section, or a hot abscess at the time of the cautery, or a blood flow, or anything else: then it is necessary that you treat whatever causes opposition to you from it with the treatment that suits it, according to what has been stated before.
If, however, the patient is timid and does not expect this operation because of its horror, then administer the perforation as I describe it: and so it is.
When it is certified to you by what we have said about the signs that the branch is watery, then it is necessary that you make the patient sit on a raised seat.
Then pierce the abscess in its lower part with a broad spatula, and place the perforation along the length of the body, and let the opening be wide until all the water flows out.
Then place a poultice on the location, and bind it, and let it run until the remaining water is discharged; then treat it until the wound heals.
If, however, there is resistance to the outflow of water at the time you pierce it, and that resistance is only because a white cloth creates resistance in the wound opening, and prevents the escape of water, then it is necessary that you insert a pen into the wound, or an instrument with which the water of hydrops is extracted, the shape of which preceded: or add a little to the wound operation.
And know that the water returns and collects after six months, or about that time. When therefore it collects, then let it be pierced according to the same method, and the sick person will pass their days.
Chapter LXIII.
Concerning the treatment of fleshy tumors.
Know that in the section concerning this tumor, there is a fear leading to destruction in many conditions. Therefore it seems to me that its removal is saved from it.
And I am remembering the operation on it, and the appearance of the abscess.
I say therefore that many abscesses occur in the bodies from which testicles are composed; and this happens from many causes, namely.
