Or from a sharp or acrid superfluity that is discharged to the testicles. Or from a blow.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and pain is not found in it; and it is a serious abscess.
And perhaps it is a stony abscess, whose color is dark, and there is no sensation in it.
And sometimes it arises from the knotting of arteries and their inflation; as its recollection has already preceded; or from the inflation of veins.
Therefore, that which is from them, from the inflation of the arteries, is recognized by the disposition of the abscess, when you compress it with your fingers. And it is not necessary that you touch it completely.
But that which is made from the inflation of the veins, nothing is spread from it by compression with the fingers.
And it is possible now that a section be made on this type, and on the remaining flesh; and it is, as to sever the skin of the testicles. Then extend the egg upwards, and pull it out with a white sifach: and free the suspensorium from the vessels, and tie the vessels; and cut the suspensorium after you have freed it from every part of the egg's parts.
If therefore the egg is consolidated with those born flesh, then it is necessary that you pull the egg and cut it.
And if there is an adhesion between something of the siphaket, or in that which is between the vessels, then it is necessary that you free the whole adhesion and cut it with a circular incision.
If however there is a birth of flesh in the place of adhesion which is behind, then it is necessary that you cut the whole of it and extract the egg, as we have said.
When therefore your operation is complete, then fill the wound with wool infused in rose oil and wine; then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
Chapter LXIV.
Concerning the treatment of Ramicis, which is with the Vite.
Vitis is an abscess enclosed in a certain covering, similar to a bunch of grapes, with a softening of the testicles: and it causes difficulty in movement and exercise, and walking, for the afflicted.
And in the treatment of this illness there is fear, because of that which preceded its remembrance.
But it is necessary, as we say, to perform the operation on it according to what the first ones did.
Therefore I say that it is necessary to have the sick person sit on a raised seat. Then move the suspensory of the testicles downward. Then hold the skin of the testicles with your fingers, with the vessels that are near the penis; and let another servant hold them, and stretch them with forceful extension.
Then cut with a broad, sharp spatula in a transverse section directly across the vessels, until the vessels are exposed.
Then scrape away from every side, as we told you in the extraction of the arteries that are in the temples.
Afterwards, insert a needle with double thread into them, and tie it at the first of the places where the veins occur, and tie it again at their last. Then cut it in the middle, in a straight section along the length of the body, and remove that which aggregates from putrid, foul humors.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds, in which the force of producing pus, so that the vessels that nourish one testicle may fall. For there is no evil through it.
However, if all vessels in the veins occur, then it is necessary that the testicles be extracted along with the vessels, so that the egg is not deprived of nourishment due to the incision, and it wastes away, or dries up, and there is no benefit with them.
Chapter LXV.
Concerning the treatment of intestinal colic.
The outcome of this colic is from a section that occurs in a distended sifach over the abdomen in the groin area, from the mirach of the abdomen.
Why do the intestines descend from that rupture to one testicle. And this rupture is either from the sifach, or from its extension.
And these two types occur from many causes: either from a blow, or a jump, or a shout, or the lifting of a heavy object, and similar things.
And the sign, when it is from the extension of the sifach. It is that which happens gradually over a long time, and does not happen suddenly: and it is an abscess equal towards the depth; because the sifach compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it, when it is from the section of the sifach, because it causes a very severe pain to occur immediately at its beginning; and the abscess is different, appearing beneath the skin nearby; and that is because of the exit of the intestine, and its course towards the exterior of the sifach.
And sometimes it emerges with the intestine, a zirb; and this is called a intestinal ramex and zirbia; and sometimes it happens with flatulence.
And sometimes faeces run into the intestine and are retained there; and it is a harm to the sick person, because it causes a mild pain, and especially when pressed.
And in the treatment of these kinds of illnesses, there is fear with iron; therefore, one must be cautious in its use.
And the method of operation is that you should instruct the sick person to draw back the intestine with their hand into the inner cavity if it obeys the withdrawal. Then let him lie on his back with his neck between your hands, and raise his legs.
Then stretch the skin that follows the skin upwards, and cut the skin of the testicles completely along its length. Then insert hooks into the two lips of the incision, according to what you need to open the incision with them; and let the incision be, as much as possible, so that an egg can emerge from it.
Then scrape off the siphak and what is beneath the skin of the testicles, until when the white, hard siphak is exposed on all sides; then insert your index finger into that which follows the testicle, into that which is between the white siphak, which is beneath the skin of the egg, and the second siphak, and break off with it the adhesion that is on the posterior part of the egg.
Then move the skin of the testicles inwards with your right hand; and with this, stretch the white siphak upwards with your left hand; and lift the egg with the siphak towards the incision; and command the assistant to stretch the egg upwards; and you break off the adhesion that is behind the complete separation.
And examine with your fingers that there is nothing there of the intestine wrapped in the hard white siphak. If however you find anything of it: then push it towards the lower belly.
Then take a needle with a thick thread, made of linen from ten threads; and insert it at the last siphak, that is, the tunic, which is under the skin of the testicles that follows the incision; then cut the ends which project from the thread, until there are four threads. Then arrange some of them over some others with a figure which is in the manner of a cross; and tie them with the alsifach, of which we have spoken, because it is under the skin of the testicles, with a violent ligature, until no vessel which nourishes them can send anything to them; so that a hot abscess does not occur from this. And place another ligature again outside the first ligature, distant from it by no more than two fingers. And after these two ligatures, leave out from the sifach, which is under the skin of the testicles, a quantity of the size of a finger; and cut the rest completely around, and remove the egg from it.
Then cut into the lower part of the skin of the testicles, with the incision from which blood and pus run, as we have narrated in that which has gone before.
Then apply wool soaked in oil, and place it in the wound; and apply the ligature which we have narrated. And sometimes the white sifach which we mentioned is cauterized, after its incision, fearing blood flow. For this happens many times. And leave the ligature until it falls away by itself.
If, however, its falling is delayed, then it should be soaked with warm water; and place upon it that which we mentioned, until it falls away.
Then treat the wound with the rest of the treatment for wounds, until it is healed.
Chapter LXVI.
About the windy rupture.
In this rupture, I have not seen anyone who dared to use iron for its treatment.
And indeed the first ones said that it happens as we have described in the rupture that is with the vine. That is, to tie the vessels after the incision, on them, with ease, below; then cut in the middle, and finally treat with that which opens it, until the vessels fall away; and treat the wound, as we said, until it is healed.
Chapter LXVII.
Of the rupture that occurs in the groins.
When rupture occurs in the inguinal region, as we have said, and the area protrudes, and nothing from the intestines descends to the testicles; and if something does descend, it is little, and returns at all hours; but if time passes with it, a fissure is added to the scrotum, until the intestines descend into the scrotum.
And that occurs from the stretching of the scrotum, which is in the inguinal region, as we have said. That is, because the scrotum stretches; then the area softens, and the place becomes suppurative, and protrudes.
And its treatment is with a cautery, as its preceding account stated.
And sometimes it is treated with an iron, according to this method: and it is so that the sick person lies on their back between your hands. Then cut the suppurated and protruding place of the rupture, by a cut across its width, according to the quantity of three fingers.
Then tighten the scrotum and what is under the skin, until when the white scrotum is exposed, which is under the skin that follows it.
Then take a rod, and place it over the protruding place from the scrotum, and press it to the depth of the belly.
Then sew the two protruding places on the end of the rod from the scrotum, and connect one of them with the other with a suture; then withdraw the end of the rod.
And do not cut the scrotum at all, nor stretch the egg, nor anything else, as I have made you know in the treatment of intestinal hernia.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds. When therefore you cut the threads, cleanse it, and restore the wound, until it is consolidated.
For the scrotum contracts, and the protrusion of the rupture is not added.
And cautery is better in this place, because it is closer to health.
Chapter LXVIII.
Of the softening of the skin of the testicles.
Many times the skin of the testicles in some men is softened, for which reason the flesh which is between them is softened, and its appearance is made foul.
Therefore it is necessary for him who is asked for treatment of it, that he place the sick man on his back, and cut away all the skin that has been softened from the natural skin.
Then he shall unite the two lips with sutures: and if you wish, sew first the excess softened skin with three or four sutures, and make the sutures firm.
Then cut between the sutures.
Then treat the remaining wounds with care, until they are healed, and the threads fall out.
Chapter LXIX.
Concerning Castration.
Know that castration is excommunicated in our law; and because of that, it would be permissible for me not to mention it in this book of mine; and its mention is not necessary except for two methods.
One of which is that it should be in the knowledge of the physician when questioned about it, and so that he may know its treatment for the one who has experienced it.
And the other method is that we often need the castration of certain animals, for our benefit through it, such as rams, boars, and cats, and similar animals.
Therefore, I say that castration is done in two ways. Or by attrition. Or by fissure and section.
In that which is done by attrition, the method of operation is this: that we make the animal sit in warm water until its testicles are softened and relaxed, and hang down; then rub them with your hand until they are dissolved and no longer appear under the touch.
In castration by fissure and section, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament, and cut above every testicle with one cut, until when two testicles are exposed; then cut them after you have skinned each, and do not leave on them any of the sifaket other than the thin sifak which is on the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that which is done by attrition, because attrition perhaps leaves some of the testicles, wherefore the animal desires intercourse.
Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter 70.
Concerning the treatment of a hermaphrodite, there are two methods.
A hermaphrodite is from men in two ways. One of them is that there appears in it, in the space below, or in the skin of the testicles, in the area between the two testicles, a figure like a woman's vulva, with hairs; and sometimes urine flows from what is in the skin of the testicles.
In women, however, there is one form, and it is a woman's vulva on top of the pubis, like very small male testicles protruding outward; one of which is like a man's penis, and two like two testicles.
And the treatment of three types, two from men and one from women, is: because it is necessary to cut away the added flesh until their impression disappears. Then treat them as with other wounds.
In the second type, however, of men, from which urine emerges, which is in the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor is there any treatment at all.
Chapter LXXI.
Concerning the incision of the vulvar swelling, and of the protruding flesh in women's vulvas.
A swelling may perhaps be added to the natural structure, until it becomes disfigured and unsightly in appearance; and sometimes it becomes so large in some women that it expands like that in men and reaches the point of intercourse.
Therefore, it is necessary to hold the excess swelling with your hand, or with a hook, and to cut it; and do not be extreme in the incision, especially in the deep root, so that a flow of blood does not occur.
Then treat it with the treatment for wounds, until it heals.
However, the flesh that grows is flesh that arises in the orifice of the womb, until it fills it; and perhaps it protrudes outwards in the likeness of a tail; and because of that, some of the ancients called the illness "tailed. Therefore, it is necessary to cut it, as we cut the swelling, and to treat it until it heals.
Concerning the treatment of Alratica, which is a non-perforated woman's vagina.
Chapter LXXII.
Alratica is, as it were, a woman's vagina that is not perforated, or has a small opening. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is from an illness that preceded: and indeed it comes about either from added flesh growth, or from subtle, or thick syrups: and it comes about either deep within the womb, or on its sides, or at its upper, or lower part: and it hinders intercourse and impregnation, and childbirth, and perhaps hinders menstruation.
And knowledge of it is made through the observation of the midwife, if an apparent obstruction is near.
If however it is not apparent, then seek it with your fingers, or with a rod.
If however the obstruction is from a thin syrup, and is near the two lips, then hurry, and break it apart.
And it is, that you place pads upon the two lips.
Then place your index fingers of both hands together, and let the woman be on her back with her legs spread. Then spread the two lips with strength, until the thin syrup is broken, and the obstruction is opened.
Then take wool, and soak it in oil, and place it over the place; and let the woman apply it every day, so that the place is not consolidated again.
If however the syrup is thick, dense: then it is necessary to cut it with a broad spatula similar to a myrtle leaf.
If however the obstruction is born of flesh, then suspend it with hooks, and cut it; and have with you medicines that soothe the flow of blood without causing a mordication, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense mixed with the white of an egg.
Then administer a wide lead cannula, so that the wound does not heal quickly, hold it for a few days; and administer a bandage of dry linen, then treat the rest with further treatment until it is healed.
And sometimes there arises in the womb a birth of other flesh; it is therefore necessary that they be cut in this way: while it is not a cancerous abscess; for a cancerous abscess that is in the womb, there should be no opposition with iron at all.
Chapter LXXIII.
Concerning the treatment of hemorrhoids, Athehelil, and Bothor rubeae in women's vulvas.
Hemorrhoids indeed are an opening of the veins, until much blood flows from them continually.
Therefore, when hemorrhoids become chronic, they become thelil: and I have already stated their species and their signs in the division; and I will say here which of them receive a cure, and which do not.
Therefore, I say that hemorrhoids which are in the orifice of the womb, and do not appear to the senses, then there is no cure with iron for them.
However, those of them which are in the orifice of the womb over which the senses fall, are those that are cured.
Therefore, it is necessary to put the woman in a cool house. Then extend the thelil with shears, or with a rough cloth, and cut them from their roots.
Then powder over them, at the flow of blood, from one of the powders of the incisions without mordication, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense, and similar to them.
Then let her feet be pressed against the wall for one hour. Afterwards, place her in cold water.
If the flow of blood persists, then make her sit in a decoction of sumac, and pomegranate bark, and gallnuts, and similar things.
Then take barley flour, and mix it with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster with it on her back.
If the blood is thus cut off with it, it is good; and if not, apply cupping glasses to the back and nipples, without scarification.
Then take wool and immerse it in the juice of tarathit, or the juice of arnoglossa, or the juice of alaulat branches, that is, aglanter, Pontic wine, and apply it to the sore.
When the abscess rests, then treat the area with ointments until it is healed.
Albarth, however, is red, similar to the heads of alhaasse, and is of harsh appearance.
Therefore, it is necessary to incise what appears from it, according to what I said in the thelil equally, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter LXXIV.
Concerning the perforation of the exit of the womb.
Many types of abscesses occur in the womb, such as cancer, and stony abscess, and ulcers, and adubelati, and alekilati, that is, herpes, boils, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and fissures, and thelil, and hot abscess.
And I have already discussed all these illnesses and their types, and their signs, and their cures in the division: it is necessary to discuss in this treatise the hot abscess which occurs in the womb, when it is from the abscesses that collect pus, and you wish to know how its opening is done with iron.
Therefore, it is necessary to consider. Then if the pain of the abscess in its inception is sharp with its percussion, and heat, and fever: and you see it reddening: if sensitivity falls upon it, then do not hurry with its perforation: and treat it with that which aids maturation, until when the sharpness of the symptoms subsides.
Then it is necessary that you make the woman sit on a seat that slants two seats, and lean back, or be placed upon her back, and draw her feet to the lower part of the belly: and let her thighs be spread out, and let her place her arms under her knees, and tie each with a suitable bandage to it.
Then let the midwife sit on the right side, and administer the instrument with which the opening of the womb is made.
And it is necessary, when you wish to administer this instrument, that you probe deeply into the womb of the woman: so that that which enters from the instrument be no more than the depth of the womb, and hurt the delicate one.
If, however, the instrument be more than the depth, then it is necessary that you place cushions on the covering of the womb's depth: so that the entire entrance of the instrument into the depth of the womb may be prevented: and it is necessary that it place the turning which runs on the instrument, from the upper side: and that the attendant hold the instrument, and that the attendant turn the turning, until the depth of the womb be opened.
When therefore the exit appears, and it is touched by hand, and is smooth and soft, then it is necessary to separate it at its head with a broad spatula.
When therefore the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place on the wound a linen cloth soaked in rose oil, or green oil, in which there is some astringency: and place the cloth, and extract the section into the depth of the womb.
And place externally upon the womb, and upon the vulva, clean wool soaked in water in which mulberries have been boiled.
Then treat it after two days with the ointment that is suitable for it, until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and wound are washed, so that it is injected with water and honey: then with water in which the lily root, or long aristolochia, has been boiled: then return to the ointment.
If the wound is hidden inwardly in the womb, then it is necessary that you refrain from treating it with iron, rather treat it with what we have said in the division.
Chapter LXX.
On the teachings of midwives, how living embryos, born in a non-natural form, are treated.
It is necessary for the midwife to know, first of all, the form of natural birth.
Therefore, among its signs is that when you see a woman pressing downwards, and desiring to smell the air, and relief is upon her from the burden of childbirth, and the embryo's exit is hastened: then know that this birth is in a natural form, and its exit is upon its head, and the afterbirth with it: either hanging or suspended from the navel.
Therefore, when you see these signs, then it is necessary that her abdomen be pressed, so that the embryo descends quickly.
For when it descends upon its head, the afterbirth descends with it, and is cleansed from those superfluities by a good cleansing.
Any birth other than this one is unnatural, blameworthy.
For sometimes the fetus emerges upon its feet, and its body emerges before its head, and its feet, and one of its hands, or one of its feet: or its head and one of its hands emerge: or its abdomen emerges: and perhaps it is turned over upon its neck, and similar examples of blameworthy forms.
Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to be prudent, astute in all these matters, and to guard herself against error and deception: and I am showing every form from them, and how the nature is within it, and how it is signified by it, and how one should act upon it.
When the fetus emerges upon its head in its natural expulsion, and it becomes difficult for the woman, and the birth becomes difficult for her, and you see that her strength is already weakened: then have her sit upon a seat, or let women hold her: and apply vapors to her vulva with decoction of fenugreek and with moist oils.
Then let the midwife insert a small spatula between her fingers, and with it split the afterbirth, and cut it with her fingernail, until that which is in it from moisture runs out: and press the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If, however, it does not descend, then it is necessary for the woman to be given an enema of fenugreek mucilage with sesame oil.
Then order her, after the enema, to compress herself; and make her sneeze with condiments, and hold her mouth and nose for one hour. For the fetus will emerge immediately.
If two hands of the fetus emerge first, you should gently and gradually draw them back. If, however, it does not yield in its entry, then place the woman on a stool, and raise her feet upwards; then shake the stool on the ground; and the woman is to be held so that she does not fall during the shaking.
If therefore the hands do not emerge, and the fetus dies, then cut them and extract the remainder of the fetus; and tie its hand with cloth, and extract it with equanimity; for it will emerge.
The exit of the embryo is upon its feet. When the fetus emerges on its feet, then it is necessary to push them upwards; then turn the fetus gradually; afterward, straighten it.
Then take one of its feet, and extend it gradually.
When therefore they emerge up to the hips, then command him to be pressed, and make him sneeze with concoction; for it will emerge, if God wills.
And if it does not emerge with what we have described, then return the fetus gradually until you place it in its natural form. For it will then emerge with easy exit.
If it is hindered with all that we have described, then take from marshmallow mucilage, and sesame oil, and dissolved gum: and pound all in a mortar with good pounding; then line with it the woman's vulva, and the lower part of her abdomen. Then make her sit in warm water, until it reaches the hypochondria.
When therefore you see that her lower parts are now warmed, then make her a poultice of myrrh, and apply it. When therefore the poultice has held for one hour, then make her sit upon a seat. Then make her sneeze, and bind her mouth and nose, and press the lower part of her abdomen with gentle pressure. For the foetus will depart immediately.
The exit of the foetus is upon the knees and hands. When it emerges according to this form, then gently introduce her hands into it. Then the woman shall lie upon her back, and her feet shall hang downward; and she shall lie upon the bed, and fold her hands. Then press gently upon her abdomen. Then pull out that which is under her feet from the pillows, until it is prepared at the lower part. If the fetus does not emerge, then take her feet and move them with a forceful motion. Then press gently on that which is above the hypochondria, until the fetus rises upwards. Then let her insert her hands, and gently straighten the fetus; and command the woman to bear down, until the fetus emerges.
The exit of the fetus, hanging sideways from the hands. Let his hand be inserted into the reduction. If however he cannot reduce it, then lift the woman, and make her walk.
If however she cannot walk, then make her lie down on the bed: and shake her legs with a violent shaking.
Then administer the liniment that you have prepared from mucilages.
If however she receives it in childbirth, then make her sit on a seat. Then strive in the reduction of the hand, and set the fetus according to its natural form: and make the woman sneeze, and command her to compress herself until it emerges.
The exit of the fetus is upon its neck, with outstretched hands, and its face is toward the back of its mother. The midwife should hold the hand of the fetus, then gradually turn it around, and adjust it with ease.
When therefore it is adjusted, then place upon its vulva what we have narrated of oil and mucilages.
Then command her to move slowly. When you have done that, then make her sit upon a seat, and command her to incline to her right side. Then command her to compress herself; and make her sneeze with seasonings.
The exit of the prepared fetus upon its side. When it descends to the orifice of the womb in this manner with the afterbirth, then cleave the afterbirth with your nail, until it is evacuated from the moisture.
If therefore it hangs over the face of the fetus, and its neck, then cut it from the navel: so that it may not be retained, and die.
Then urge the fetus inwards, until you place it as it should be; and command the woman to compress herself several times; for it will emerge through a gentle exit.
The exit of twins, or many fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three and four are born. However, that is unusual and rarely happens.
However, five is unnatural, and they do not entirely live: and though they multiply, they are contained by one placenta; and they are separated by a sort of partition, distinguishing between them, tied to the umbilical cord of each.
And sometimes they emerge like a single fetus emerges with a natural, easy exit; and they emerge with an unpraised exit, as we have recounted.
If some of them are difficult at birth, and reach the unnatural forms we have mentioned, then treat them as we have recounted; and let the midwife be skillful, and do what she does with ease and slowness, and devise a strategy according to every form with what is prepared for her from a leading skill towards health.
A recollection of what is formed in the womb from embryos, and miscarriages.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten are formed in the womb; and it has already been verified with me that a woman miscarried seven, and another fifteen, all formed.
That which was done by the measurement of the glorious wise man.
Therefore, all this must be in the knowledge of the midwife. Perhaps something similar to that will come to her; therefore, let her oppose it with what is necessary.
Chapter LXXVI.
Concerning the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you treat a dead fetus with what I have said in the division from medication, and it does not emerge, and you are forced to use an operation with your hand, then you must consider.
If therefore the woman is of sound constitution, and is not afflicted with any of the accidents from which harm to her is feared, then it is necessary that the woman be laid supine on a bed, on her back, and her head be lowered, and her legs raised; and the attendant should hold her from both sides, or she should be bound to the bed, so that her body is not disturbed during the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the os uteri with humid oils, along with the mucilage of marshmallow, fenugreek, and flaxseed.
Then the midwife should anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages; then she should gently insert it into the uterus. Then she should seek, with it, the place where she should fix the hooks from the fetus, and the suitable places for that.
Therefore, consider then, if the fetus is ready to descend with its head: then fix the hooks in its eyes, or in its neck, or in its mouth, or in its palate, or under its chin, or in its fork, or in places close to the ribs, and beneath the hypochondria.
If however the descent is upon its feet, then in its pubic bone and its middle ribs.
And it is necessary that she hold the hook with her right hand; and place its curve in the space between the fingers of her left hand; and she should insert it with her hand gently, and fix the hook in any of those places we have mentioned, until she reaches the empty space.
Then she should fix another hook, or a third, if needed, in opposition to it, so that there is an equal contraction, and it does not lean to one side.
Then let it be extended with equal extension, and let the extension not be only in a straight line, but rather move in every direction, so that its exit is eased; just as happens with a tooth during its extraction.
And it is necessary in that which is between it, that the extension be softened. If a part of it remains, then the midwife should anoint some fingers with oil and insert them on the side, to turn around with them what remains.
If therefore a part of the fetus emerges, then the hooks should be moved to another place, which is higher; do this until the entire fetus emerges.
If therefore a hand emerges before the other parts, and it is not possible to return it, due to its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it. Then pull it out until, when the whole emerges, cut it either at the ankle joint or at the elbow; and thus it is necessary that you do for the other hand, and for the two feet.
If however the fetus's head is large, or constriction happens to it in its exit, or water is collected in its head, then it is necessary to insert a spiny Spatumile between the fingers and split the head with it, and remove the water; or break it with the instrument called Almisdac, the shape of which is found with the shapes of the other instruments in the chapter that is after this one.
Similarly, he should do this if the fetus is of a large-headed nature, then it is necessary to separate it with algengematu; or extract it with misdach, as we have said. Then pull the bones with forceps.
If the head emerges, and is constricted at the fork, then it is to be split, until the moisture that is in the chest is poured out.
If however it does not become entangled, nor yields to exit, then cut the head into pieces, as much as possible for you.
If the lower part of the abdomen is ulcerated, or has dropsy in it, then it is necessary to pierce it, until moisture flows from it.
If however the fetus is over its feet, then its extraction is easy, and its direction to the mouth of the womb is slight.
If however it is constricted at the belly, or chest, then it is necessary to extract it with a cloth which you have wrapped around your hand: and split the belly, or chest, until that which is within them flows out.
If however the remaining limbs are removed, and the head returns, then the Midwife should insert her left hand. If therefore the mouth of the womb is open, then she should insert her hand deep into the womb, and search with it for the head, and pull it with her fingers until it reaches the mouth of the womb; then she should insert one hook, or two hooks, into it, and pull it with that.
If however the mouth of the womb is constricted, on account of a hot abscess which has occurred in it, then it is not necessary to labor upon it, but it is necessary then to administer the effusion of moist, fatty things, and apply plasters; and she should sit in waters that soften and moisten.
If the fetus is on its side, then it is possible to make it straight; then administer what we have said about a living fetus.
If this is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces and extracted.
And it is necessary that you not leave anything of the afterbirth inside at all.
When therefore you have completed your operation, then administer what we have said about hot abscesses that occur in the wombs.
If a flow of blood occurs, then let it descend into astringent waters, and administer the rest of the treatment.
And indeed I have already seen a woman, in whose belly a foetus had died; then she became pregnant again over it, afterwards another foetus also died. Therefore, after a long time, an abscess occurred in her navel, and it swelled until it opened, and it began to discharge pus.
Therefore, I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, and the wound did not heal.
Therefore, I placed over it certain plasters of strong attraction, and a bone came out of the place. Then a few days passed, and another bone came out. Therefore, I was amazed by this, since there is no bone in the belly.
Therefore, I thought that they were from the bones of a dead fetus.
Then I investigated the wound, and removed many bones, the woman being in better condition: and she thus lived a long time, and discharged a little pus from the place.
And I did not consider this extraneous, except because it contains knowledge and help for what the skilled physician administers with his hand in the treatment.
Chapter LXXVII.
Concerning the forms of necessary instruments in the extraction of a fetus.
The form of the pivot by which the matrix is opened.
Here below is the form of a stretcher, by which books are equalized, with two pivots equally placed at the two ends of two pieces of wood. However, this pivot should be finer than the stretcher, and to make it, either from ebony or from boxwood.
And the width of each piece of wood should be about two fingers, and the margin of each of them is about one finger, and the length of each of them is a palm and a half; and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two additions of the same kind of wood, which are already fixed on both sides, whose length is half a palm and a little more; and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the opening of the matrix, so that it opens with them when you turn the two pivots.
Another form similar to it, finer and lighter.
Let it be made of ebony wood, or boxwood, according to the likeness of forceps. However, at its end there are two additions, as you see here above: and the length of each of these additions is approximately a palm: and the width of each is two fingers.
Therefore, when you wish to open the matrix with it, then make the woman sit on a bed, with her feet hanging down, opening what is between her legs. Then introduce these two joined additions into the matrix: and you are holding the lower end of the instrument between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument, as you do with scissors, equally according to the amount you wish to open the mouth of the matrix, until the midwife does what she wants.
Another form of Vertigo, which the ancients called the Impelling Form, by which the fetus is impelled.
The Almisdac form, by which the large head is crushed: and it is likened to a pair of tongs, which have teeth at the end, as you see.
And sometimes it becomes elongated, like forceps of this shape, as you see, which have teeth like the teeth of a saw, by which it is cut and crushed.
The form of a hook with one spine.
The form of a hook with two spines.
The form of a scalpel with two ends for severing the head.
The form of two broad scalpels.
All these instruments are used, and the more their types multiply, and are prepared by the craftsman, the more swift is his operation, and the higher is he regarded by men, because of their number.
Therefore, do not disdain any instrument, lest it not be prepared with you. For their necessity is inexcusable.
Chapter LXXVIII.
Concerning the extraction of the Secundine.
When the Secundine is retained during childbirth, then it is necessary that you instruct the woman to hold her breath. Then make her sneeze with condiments: and grasp your hand over her mouth and nostrils. If therefore it emerges through this, it is good.
If not, then take a pot, and pierce a hole in the lid: and place in it herbs that open the mother's mouth, such as calamint, rue, dill, chamomile, sseham, cassia wood, and centaury: take all these herbs, or some of them, and immerse them in water, and place them on the fire.
Then place a reed stopper over the hole of the pot's lid, and the other end in the woman's womb: and hold it until the vapor reaches the cavity of the womb.
Then make her sneeze, as we said. For the afterbirth will emerge with ease.
If however it remains after this treatment, and does not emerge: then order the midwife to immerse her hand in sesame oil, or marshmallow mucilage. Then let her introduce it into the vagina, and seek the afterbirth with it. When therefore she finds it, then let her take it and extend it gradually.
If however it be attached deep within the womb, then let her introduce her hand, as we have described, until, when she finds the afterbirth, she extracts it gradually in a straight line, so that the womb does not fall away with a sudden pull; rather, it ought to be moved easily to the right and left sides, then an amount of traction should be added. For it then obeys, and is preserved, and escapes from adhesion.
If the os of the womb is constricted, as we have already described: treat it with sneezing, and with a medication of a pot with herbs.
If however it does not emerge with all that we have described, then beware lest you tire yourself in repeating the extraction.
But it is necessary that whatever emerges from it to the os of the womb be tied to the woman's hip. Then administer a clyster to her with the tetrapharmacon ointment. For this will indeed putrefy it after days, and it will dissolve and emerge.
However, when it putrefies, a foul odor rises from it to the stomach and head, and harms the weak one. Therefore, suitable fumigations must be administered to it.
Some of the ancients have already experienced fumigation with nasturtium and dried figs.
The form of the instrument by which a woman is fumigated during the retention of the Secundinae and menses.
Let it be made of glass similar to a flask, or of bronze: and let the wide end be placed over the fire: and let the fumigation be placed over the prunes: let it hold it, until that fumigation is consumed: and again another.
Chapter LXXIX.
Concerning the treatment of an unopened anus.
Many children are born with their anuses not perforated, which are already obstructed by a thin sifac. Therefore, the midwife must perforate that sifac with her finger; and if not, let her perforate it with a sharp Spatumili; and let her be careful not to touch the flesh.
Then place upon it wool soaked in wine and oil. Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
If however you fear that it will become obstructed, then place a lead cannula in the opening for many days, and let it be removed when the child wishes to evacuate.
And now obstruction of the anus also happens from the consolidation of a wound, or an abscess. Therefore, it is necessary to cut that consolidation, then treat it with what we have said concerning treatment and lead.
Chapter Eighty.
Concerning the treatment of fistulas which occur in the lower part.
Fistulas which occur in the lower part are swellings and thicknesses which occur near the anus externally; or in the space of one part; and it is one fistula or several.
When that swelling has become longstanding, it opens, and watery white moisture or thin pus flows from it.
And sometimes there are penetrating fistulas from these. Therefore, penetrating ones are recognized by that which comes out through evacuation and windiness, when the sick person uses evacuation; and perhaps worms come out of them.
And some of those fistulas, when they are penetrating, are in the space reaching to the bladder and to the urethra. And some of them are penetrating to the hip joint and to the tail bone.
By this, a penetrating fistula to the anus is recognized, even from one that is not penetrating, that you insert your index finger into the anus, and insert a thin probe into the fistula through the air, or iron, when there is no twisting.
If there is twisting in it, then insert into it a thin lead probe, or a horsehair, until you feel the probe, or hair, in your finger.
If you do not feel it completely, nor does anything emerge from the opening in terms of excretion, nor wind, nor worm as I said, then know that it is not penetrating: therefore, hasten to the treatment.
And when the fistula is penetrating to the bladder, or to the urinary meatus, then its sign is the excretion of urine from it, and its prevention from this that the place be consolidated with medicines.
However, if it is penetrating to the hip joint, or to the hip bone, then its sign is that the probe reaches there, if there is no twisting in it to the bone, and the finding of pain in the surrounding area, and that pus emerges from it always, and that treatment does not work in it, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas, there is no cure at all; and their treatment is labor and vanity to him, from foolish physicians, upon whom it is formed.
However, in the one that is not penetrating, and not ancient, healing is hoped for with a saw, according to what I tell you, and what I have already tested: and it is so that the sick person lies between your hands on his back, and raise his legs above his hips, and his hips are inclined to his stomach. Then introduce a lead or copper probe, if there is twisting in the fistula, until you know where the probe reaches.
If, however, the patient feels it around the anus, then it is necessary that you insert your index finger into the anus.
If, therefore, you feel the probe already to have penetrated itself detected, beyond what you feel between your finger and the fistula, or flesh: then you know certainly that it is penetrating: therefore, do not labour on it, for there is no cure of it, as we have said.
They have said, however, that in some men the flesh heals, and that from the medicine by which help is hoped for it is to apply a thin cautery according to the size of the fistula, as previously mentioned, and to insert it heated into the fistula, until it reaches around the anus. Then repeat it twice or three times, until you know that all those added, filtered flesh, which are like bird's feather quills, have been burned.
Then treat it with lint wrapped in butter, until those burned flesh parts come out; then treat it with ointments that generate flesh and consolidate it.
If, therefore, it heals, it is good.
And if not, then it will never heal with any other treatment.
And if a probe emerges, and it has not reached your finger in the anus, and there is a thick layer of flesh between it and the probe, or if such is the case: and you see a fistula in that which follows the skin's surface, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you go on cutting with the probe which is in the fistula, until the incision reaches where the probe's tip reaches, and the probe is released and falls out.
Then cleanse those filtered flesh, which resemble the quill of a bird, so that nothing remains of them.
However, if blood overcomes you, and separates you from your operation so that you cut those flesh: then it is better, and more quickly helpful, to cauterize it with fire, or with a sharp medicine.
Because cautery with fire aggregates good habits, by burning those added flesh, and by cutting off the blood, and drying the moisture.
Then treat it with bandages wrapped in butter, or pounded herbs, until the area discharges pus, and those burnt flesh emerge in the pus.
Then treat the wound with solidifying ointments, which produce firm flesh: and there are medicines in which there is astringency and drying.
For when it is restored, then it is already healed: and you do not fear repetition.
If however the fistula has already reached the depth of the anus, and is extended by bodily superfluity: then introduce your finger into the anus, and investigate with it.
If therefore you feel a membrane, and between you and it is a veil of sifac, or flesh, and it is near to penetration: then there is no operation on it, unless according to the path of trust and hope.
For this is because one of three methods is administered in it: either to cauterize it itself, as we said: or to dissect it, until you obtain its concavity, so that it is possible for you to insert ligatures, and healing from close proximity. And do not proceed too far with the dissection, so that you do not cut the muscle surrounding the anus, and that a spontaneous expulsion of excrement occurs without the patient's will. Then treat it with what we said.
For perhaps it will be healed: or you will lengthen the membrane, and cleanse it after you have cut it close to the anus, as we said, either with tongs, or with another instrument of sharp end. Then cleanse those filtered flesh that are in the incision, until where you can with scrutiny.
Then attempt the restoration of the entire wound with the incision, and the upper opening where it consolidates; and a fistula remains open in the interior part of the anus, and it will be easier on the patient.
And sometimes the fistula is sutured in this way: and it is when you insert a probe into the fistula; and it is in the side of the anus near the body's surface with the skin and the margin of the anus; then take a probe with a perforated end, which is like a needle for preparing hides, according to this form. And insert into it a thread wrapped in five threads, and about it. Then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its concavity.
If, therefore, it is penetrating the inner margin of the anus nearby, then pull the thread out of that opening: so that you insert your finger into the anus, and pull out the end of the thread, and join the two ends together; and tie each, and leave it for one or two days; and the more the thread cuts into the flesh, the better tie it, until those flesh which are between the two ends of the thread are cut and fall away; then treat the wound until it consolidates and heals.
If however the fistula's opening is not penetrating, then make it penetrate in whatever way is prepared for you, unless it is very deep. For it is not in you to do that on account of the muscle, so as not to injure it.
Then do with it as we have said, until it is healed.
A pointed spatula, by which the fistula is split, whose curvature is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not injure what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter LXXXI.
Concerning the suture of hemorrhoids, from which blood flows from their incision: and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types. Either they are within the anus, like bags of coal or prunes, or like bunches of grapes themselves. And of them there are small and large ones; and blood flows from them always, and there is one, and there are many. And some are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are mostly small moist lumps, from which flows yellowish water or little blood with persistent running; and they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is within the anus is that you command the sick person to strain: and it is expressed until it emerges from the anus, and you see the atheril for yourself. Therefore, also suspend them with hooks, or hold them with your fingernail. Then cut them at their roots.
However, if the hooks do not hold on them, because of their moisture and their softening, then take them with a rough cloth, or with a double-wool cloth, and pull them out with your fingers. Then cut them, and after the incision, powder over them certain hot powders, so that they may stand in the place of a cautery. Or cauterize them according to what went before in the chapter on cauterization. Then treat them with the rest of the treatment, until they are healed.
If the anus does not yield to evacuation, then administer an enema to the sick person containing a small mordicant, so that it may wash away what is in the anus and quickly yield to evacuation when the sick person expels it.
If, however, the piles are external from the anus, then their removal is easy: and it is that you take them with your fingernail, or take them with a hook, and cut them. Then treat them with what we have said, until they are healed.
And whoever abhors incision with iron, then it is necessary that you administer their suture in this way. Take a wrapped thread, and insert it into the needle. Then pull the piles upwards, and make it pierce with the needle at its root on the other side: and wrap the two ends of the thread, at the lower part of the needle, and let them be laterally: and tighten the piles with strong constriction. Then tie the thread, and remove the needle.
Do that with all the piles, and leave one of them: and do not sew it, so that superfluity of blood runs from it.
Then place over the anus a cloth soaked in rose-oil, or in oil of caper, and place it over the anus: and order the sick person to rest. Then leave it until it falls.
When therefore the piles fall, treat them with ointments and other treatment until they are healed.
Concerning the treatment of Fissures. For they often occur from the drying out of excrement and constriction of the nature.
When therefore they become old, and medicine does not work on them, then it is necessary to scrape them with a pointed spatula, or with your fingernail, until they become moist, and the upper crust which prevents their healing is removed. Then treat them until they heal as is proper.
If however they do not heal, then scrape them again with a more forceful scraping than the first, until they bleed a lot and are scraped off; then treat them until they heal.
Concerning the treatment of inverted and non-inverted almirmar, and of dry athelil and formicae.
Chapter LXXXII.
Know that Almirmar is nothing other than a round knot according to the color of the body, similar to the head of a nail; and it occurs throughout the body, especially in the lower parts of the feet and toes; and pain arises from them during walking.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut that which is around the nail, and to hold it with shears or a hook, and to uproot it from its root.
Then treat it; and if you wish, cauterize it according to what preceded in its chapter.
From dry athelil, however, one is a small, rough, filtered, round eminence above the surface of the body. And many times they occur on the hands of children.
And its treatment is to stretch the athelil, and cut it; or to bind it with a silk thread; or with a bristle, until it falls. And if you wish, cauterize it according to what has been mentioned with fire, or with a sharp medicine; and now the inverted athelil are eradicated, and not the inverted ones with the instrument that I will name.
A formica, however, is also a small, filtered, thick eminence above the surface of the body, going deep very much: and when cold finds it, a sensation similar to an ant's bite is felt in it, and it happens throughout the body; and most of it happens in the hands.
And its treatment is to take a cannula from a goose's feather, or a strong vulture's feather; and place that cannula over the Formica until the cannula's circle contains its parts. Then turn your hand with the cannula, until you separate what is around the Formica; and place it in the depth of the flesh, and eradicate the Formica from its root.
And if you wish, do it with a cannula of air, or iron, according to this form.
Let the upper part of the cannula be drawn to a height, so that the grasp of the deaf thing wrapped around it, and its turning, may be eased.
And if you wish, cauterize the Formica according to what is stated in the cauterization of Athelil in the chapter on the cautery.
Chapter Eighty-three.
Concerning the forms of instruments which are administered for enemas in diseases of the anus, and for loosening the belly, and for colic.
Sometimes the clyster is made of silver, or of brass, or of cast bronze, or of hammered bronze.
And sometimes it is made of these instruments small, and large, according to those in which they are administered.
Therefore, that which is administered in the treatment of very young children should be small; and those whose anuses are weak or painful, so too their enemas.
Large clyster.
Its tube, that is, the upper end to which the bladder is tied, should be wide according to this method; and it should have a distinction so that the bladder may be tied as you see, and its lower end which is introduced into the anus should be closed, that is, not open, smooth, very, drawing to a point; and on one side of it there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole.
And the size of the hole should be according to the thickness of rays, and a little thicker; and let the bladder in which the medicines are placed be made from an animal's bladder, or from parchment, which should be according to the shape of a small asofrat; whose root should be one palm.
And let it be pierced with many holes, as it is rolled, and let the space between each hole be the thickness of a finger. Then insert into those holes a strong thread made of raw thread, which should be composed of ten threads, or thereabouts, and bind the bladder to it like an asofrat. When therefore the bladder is placed in it, the head of the enema should be tied at the top between two distinguishing constrictions with a strong tie. Then administer the enema with it.
And this is the form of the bladder depicted here, and the holes.
The method of administering the enema with it is: to fill the bladder, or the vessel, with medicine that is warmed according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is not excessively hot or excessively cold.
Then the sick person should lie on their back, and raise their legs upward; and place beneath them a mat, that is, a hide upon which people lie, or a linen cloth.
Then anoint the end of the clyster with oil, or with egg white, or with the mucilage of fenugreek, or similar things. Then introduce it into the anus with ease. Then let the attendant grasp the bladder with both hands together, and squeeze it with force, until all that is in the clyster is evacuated into the intestine.
Then remove the clyster, and let the sick person raise their legs to the wall.
If the medicine compels him to an outcome, then let him hold it as long as possible. And if he sleeps with it the whole night, it is more beneficial in helping.
If, however, you administer the clyster in illnesses of the belly's loosening, and his exit is quick: then repeat the clyster until he is healed.
If there are hemorrhoids or ulcerating abscesses in the anus, then let the clyster be very thin, gentle, from the clysters with which children are administered clysters.
And this should be the form of a thin clyster above which there is tight skin.
Chapter Eighty-four.
I have already spoken in the division of the treatment of wounds that it was fitting for the book, and I will speak here of their treatment with the hand and with medicines, according to the completion.
Therefore I say that wounds are diversified according to that by which the wounds are made, and according to the place upon which the wound falls.
The things by which wounds are made are often, as the throw of a stone; or the incision of a sword, or knife; or the striking with a lance, or wood, or arrow; and with similar things from many things.
And the wounds that are made according to the places of the body are, such as a wound that falls upon the head, or neck, or breast, or belly, or liver; and similar from those members.
And I am narrating the treatments of certain wounds, so as to place that reasoning and rule over the remaining wounds.
And I will begin with simple wounds of the head, because the remembrance of compounded ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
I say therefore. When a simple wound occurs on the head, and there is no fracture of the bone: consider then, if it is from the throw of a stone, or similar things, and has only broken the skin: and is a large wound, and you fear a bad outcome of a hot abscess: then hurry, and immediately perform a cephalic phlebotomy: and do not delay it: and draw from his blood according to the quantity of his strength: if there is nothing preventing it by phlebotomy, especially if the blood that emerges from the wound is little. And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part. And let the sick person beware of fullness, and wine, and heavy foods.
If, however, something prevents diminution, then lessen food and do not approach meats, nor wines.
And place on the wound, if a hot abscess occurs, a poultice infused in rose oil alone, or with wine in which there is astringency.
If however you are certain of a hot abscess, then place on the wound, if it is fresh with its own blood, and the air has not changed it, the powder which I will describe shortly: and if the air has already changed it, then place on it certain ointments, until it is healed.
If however the wound is large, or is from a sword cut, and similar things: and its edges do not come together by means of pads, then bring them together with sutures, according to what I will describe in wounds of the belly concerning their sutures.
If however the wound has already been separated from the bone, and hangs, and there is no retention of it except in a small suspension: then cut it. Then treat the wound with medicines, and restore in it firm flesh in place of skin, and command the sick person to be nourished with foods in which there is thickness, such as heads, and cooked wheat, until it is like dough: and things similar to them.
If you find putrefaction in the skin, and it does not adhere to the bone: then cut away the entire putrefied part, and cast it away: then treat it.
If however in these wounds there is an artery, or vein from which blood flows: and it is not severed by medicines, then search over the artery. If therefore you find it not severed, then sever it with a Spatumili, or tie it. If necessity prompts when that which we have said does not help, then cauterize it until the blood is severed.
If however these wounds are small, simple, then the matter of them is easy, light: so that you pulverize this powder over them: because it consolidates them before air changes them.
Method of the Flesh-forming Powder. Take one part of frankincense, and two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of quicklime:  grind the whole thoroughly, and sieve it: and fill the wound with it, and bind it with a good bandage, until it adheres well to it and coagulates with the blood.
Then bind it over with bandages, and leave it, and do not loosen it while it remains, so that it does not fester. For the medicine is not eradicated from it until it is healed.
And sometimes the lime alone does this, when you do not have frankincense and dragon's blood present; it also accomplishes the same action on many large wounds, when it is appropriate to place it on them and bind them, and the wounds are recent with their own blood, which the air has not altered.
If it has already altered it with some alteration, especially when it is in the summertime, then the intention of applying this powder is not appropriate: therefore treat it with what we have said, so that you place some digestive ointments upon it; or place poultices of barley flour made with water and honey upon it, until they discharge pus. Then treat the remaining part with the other treatment until they heal.
However, if a fracture in the bone occurs with a wound, and it is small, then extract it with forceps; and I will now state the treatment of skull fractures in what is to follow.
Of wounds of the Neck.
If however a wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference between it in treatment, and between a wound of the head when it is simple.
If however the nerve of the neck, or the artery, is already cut: then if it is a nerve, there is no skill in it, except to avoid its treatment with anything that produces strong astringency, such as ziniar, and zegi, and similar things. Because they harm the nerves, and contract them.
And do not put anything cold on the wound at all: because the substance of nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is higher than the limbs: and let that which is used for treatment be mild from medicines, such as lime washed with sweet water sometimes infused with oil, or with rose oil, and tuttia, when washed also with sweet water: and the remaining mineral stones, in this manner, and moist ointments.
If however the wound is large, then administer sutures: or constrict its lips with pledgets. Then treat it until it is healed.
If however the wound has depth, or a sinus has occurred in its lower part, in which pus is already collected, then pierce it in the more depressed place in it.
If, however, an artery has already been incised in the wound, and its blood flow has ensued: then separate, or cut it, and tie it: or cauterize it, if necessity calls for it.
If, however, the windpipe has already been cut, or the whole [throat], and the surrounding tissues have been saved, then bring together the skin flaps with sutures over the windpipe, and do not touch the windpipe, rather, equalize it, and restore it to its natural shape. Then bind it with a proper ligature, and leave it for several days. Then treat it with ointments containing astringency and desiccation, until it heals.
If, however, the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then explore it: then if there are fragments of bone within it, extract them all, or as many as you can: and that which is not extracted, leave it until the wound suppurates, and the discharge separates and is relieved.
If the wound is recent, then powder it with the powder I have described. If, however, a bone appears in the wound, and a fistula forms, then treat it with that which is mentioned in its chapter.
Concerning a wound in the shoulders.
If there is a blow from a lance or a knife, and you see its depth, then consider. If, therefore, wind emerges from the wound when the sick person breathes heavily from it, then know that the wound is mortal.
If it does not have depth, and is fresh with its own blood: then do not put powder on it from the first hour, nor bind it, so that the blood is not retained in its depth, and returns to the heart, and kills the sick person; but put attracting ointment on it, and dissolve it twice a day.
If, however, ointment is not present for you, then place an old rag in the wound's opening, so that it sucks what emerges from it of humidities; and make the sick person sleep over the wound, so that what is collected in it may run.
If, however, three days or more have already passed since the wound, and no spasm has occurred in the sick person, nor bad tremor of the heart, nor constriction in breathing, and you see the other conditions of him are good: then know that the wound is safe. Therefore, treat him with bandages and the rest of the treatment, until he is healed.
If, however, his healing is excused, and it is always opening up, then know that it has become a fistula. Therefore treat it according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from a sword cut or a knife cut, and is simple on the surface of the chest or back, then treat it with what has gone before in terms of sutures, if it is large; or with powder, if it is small.
But if it has already made an impression on the bone, and has cut fragments from it, then examine the wound, and hasten to remove those fragments, if they are separate. If, however, they are not separate, then leave them until the wound becomes putrid, for their removal will be easier.
However, for other accidental wounds in other limbs, the judgment in treatment is the judgment that we have stated.
Concerning wounds of the belly, and the exit of the intestines, and their expulsion.
A rupture that occurs in the belly is great, medium, and small.
From a great one, therefore, sometimes the intestine, or a number of intestines, emerges.
Therefore, their introduction, and their stitching, is more vehement and more difficult.
And a small rupture also becomes difficult in another way. It follows, therefore, that their reduction is more difficult if you do not hasten their introduction to their place immediately, because it is inflated, opened, and thickened; wherefore their entrance becomes difficult.
Therefore, a middle rupture is better; because when it occurs, the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is in these two types.
Know that the suturing of the belly is according to four ways.
Two common sutures are found in belly sutures, and in the sutures of other wounds of the body; and two other sutures are proper to other wounds. However, they are more proper to the suturing of the belly; and I narrate each one to you with explanation and declaration.
Therefore, I say that when the wound is small, and something from the intestine protrudes from it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then that is due to one of two ways. Either because of the smallness of the rupture, as we said, or because there is an inflation in the intestine due to the coldness of the air.
When, therefore, it is thus, then it is necessary that you heat it, so that you immerse a sponge, or a damp cloth, in only lukewarm water. Or boil it in water with squinanthus, and cyperus, and spike, and apply it to the intestine with it, until the inflation is resolved.
Sometimes wine in which there is astringency does it, and it is better than plain water in resolving the inflation.
When therefore you see the inflation already resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which mallow or mallow has been boiled. For through it the entrance of it is relieved with a small thing.
If however its return is excused after this treatment, then cut in the disruption a little, with the instrument with which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first treatment is better when prepared, than the incision; and no compulsion is made to cut after necessity and the instrument's failure.
Let one part of it which is twisted and sharp, and the other part of it not sharp, and the fine end not be with the fineness of a spatula, but rather somewhat duller: and that is which is similar to a wedge, as you see.
When, therefore, the wound is enlarged, and the intestine enters, then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form in its proper place, if possible over it; for it is better.
When, however, the rupture is wide in the lower part of the belly, then it is necessary that you cause the sick person to lie on his back, and raise his legs higher than his head. And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then place his head and chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the belly, then place your intention and your consideration always in this, that the part in which the wound is be higher than the other part.
And thus it is necessary that you administer it in great wounds, and in medium wounds.
In small wounds, however, place it according to what is prepared for you.
Then have a pious minister present between your hands, who holds the entire rupture with his hand, and bring together its lips. Then uncover from it for him who follows the suture something after something: and this is the mode of a single common suture.
And it is, that you take a needle, or a number of needles according to the amount of the wound's width. Then release from the extremity of the rupture the amount of a ring finger, that is, a physician's: and fix one needle beyond that into which you insert a thread at both extremities of the skin, with the two extremities similarly done which are beneath the skin from the interior, until you cause it to penetrate from the other side, and you have already brought together the two extremities of the skin, and two extremities of similarly done: and four rows are made.
Then tighten with a double thread in a circular motion of the needle several times from both sides simultaneously, until the two lips of the wound are brought together with a proper joining.
Then release the thickness of a finger also, and fix another needle in them; afterwards draw it through with the thread, as you did with the first needle. Therefore do not cease to do that with whatever needles you need, until you complete the tightening of the entire wound.
And the needles should be intermediate in thickness and thinness, because very thin needles cut the flesh very quickly, and thick ones are difficult to enter the skin; therefore, they should be intermediate in thinness and thickness.
And it is not necessary to fix the needle in the two closely adjacent skin edges for too long; so that the flesh is not cut quickly, and the wound is opened before its consolidation, and it should not be stretched too far with the suture, so that the wound is not prevented from healing.
Then trim the ends of the needles, so that they do not harm the sick person in their sleep; and place cushions of cloth around them on every side that hold the ends of the needles; and leave them until you know that the wound is already healed.
And this type of suture with needles is indeed more suitable for small wounds. Because one, or two, or about that many needles are sufficient in their suture.
However, the second common suture is to gather together with the suture four edges: two edges of skin and two edges of a wound, at one time, with a needle on which a thread wrapped around is equal in fineness and thickness.
Then, when you have pierced these four edges with the needle, pull the needle back from the side from which you began, so that the thread drawn back falls from the upper part of the wound, so that it is a suture, like a suture of cloth by which cloths are drawn together, and place between each suture a quantity less than the thickness of a little finger.
However, this suture is known by men.
And with this suture I treated a wound that had happened to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the wound's rupture was greater than one palm: and about two palms of the middle intestine had come out from the intestines, and there was a rupture in the middle of the belly.
Therefore I returned it after his intestine had remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
Therefore, the wound was consolidated in a space of about fifteen days: and I treated it, and he was healed; and he lived after that for many years, engaged in his affairs.
And the physicians had judged that it would not be healed.
And it was a miracle that I did not treat it with ointment. Because I was in a place where there was no ointment, nor any of the medicines. Therefore, I placed an old bandage over the wound twice a day, and diligently washed it with honey water, until it was healed.
I will now describe two proper types of sutures, according to the account of the discourse.
And it is, that you begin with a sharp suture, and insert the needle from the outer to the inner part.
When therefore the needle penetrates the skin, and going into the muscle along the length and width of the entire body, leave the end in the side in which you inserted the needle, and let it penetrate the other end from the inner to the outer part at the other extremity.
When you have thus caused it to penetrate, then let it penetrate in this same extremity mirach from the outer to the inner part: and leave the end sifac, which is on this side, and let the needle penetrate the other extremity from the inner to the outer part: and let it penetrate with your penetration which you cause it to penetrate into the extremity sifac mirach, which is in its part, until it penetrates the whole thing.
Then begin also from this same side, and sew it with the extremity, which is from sifac on the other side, and withdraw the needle from the skin which it penetrated.
Then reduce it into that skin, and sew the extremity sifac, which is on the other side, with this extremity mirach: and withdraw it from the skin which is on its part, and do it one time, and another: and do it time after time, until you have sewed the whole outside according to that example.
And the summit of this suture is to sew the skin of the skinners in the way they sew skins: so that you sew sifac one time from one extremity of the skin; and that you leave it another time from another part, until you complete it.
The second species of proper suture, which Galen said.
His discourse relates that one should sew it according to the manner in which some physicians sew them, so that they gather every part to its corresponding part naturally: and apply the extremity sifac to its other extremity, and the extremity mirach to its other extremity. And that is done according to what I tell you.
You should place the needle at the mirach extremity nearest to you on the outside, and make it penetrate to the inside in that sole; and leave the two sifac extremities. Then turn the needle and make it penetrate from the outside to the inside in the two sifac extremities, namely on both sides.
Then turn both, also making them penetrate from the inside to the outside, into the two other mirach extremities, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the common easy suture, and is a suture whose recollection precedes.
This is therefore also Galen's discourse.
And some said. The suture of the belly is rectified with two needles. That is, that you introduce one thread in each of them: and begin with the insertion of the needle, from where it is with you, and cause it to penetrate to the other side, according to the suture of those who stitch hides equally.
And know that when the rupture is in the middle of the belly, then its suture is more difficult than the other parts of the belly.
The judgment of the treatment of a wound is like the judgment of the other wounds.
That is, because when you acquire a fresh wound with its blood, before the air alters it, and you replace the intestine, and mend it, and fit it, then place it upon the incarnating powder.
If the air has already altered the wound, then place certain ointments upon it that are present with you, until they produce pus, and the threads fall away, and the wound is consolidated, and mirach. Then treat it as is the treatment of other wounds until it is healed.
If, however, the medicines are not present with you, then place on it immediately, from when it begins to emit pus, old cotum; and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it is healed.
For you do not need other medicine in many dispositions, when the wounds are simple.
If, however, you fear that the wound communicates with the main members in the injury, then it is necessary to immerse soft wool in oil of moderate heat, or in rose oil, and place it around the places that are between the groin and under the armpits.
If, however, he feels pain, or pricks, or putrefaction in his intestines (for this often happens) then enema him with astringent, black, warm wine; especially if putrefaction has already reached the intestine, and a penetrating exit has been made to its concavity.
And know that that which is from the large intestine is of easier healing; and that which is from those which are subtle is of more difficult healing.
However, the intestine called the Jejunum does not receive healing from a wound that occurs in it at all; and this is because of its multitude of veins, and their size, and because of the subtlety of its body, and the proximity of its nature to the nerves.
If, however, that which proceeds from the wound is a zirbum, and you obtain it fresh, reduce it according to your reduction, by which you reduce the intestines equally.
If, however, a space has passed and it has already become green or black, then you must tie it with a thread above the place that has blackened from it, so that a flow of blood does not occur. For in a zirbum are veins and arteries.
Then cut that which is beyond that ligature, and hang the two ends of the thread from the lower part of the wound outside it, so that the pull on it may be relieved for you, and its extraction in the case of a zirbum, and the discharge of pus from the wound.
A recollection of the wound that occurred in the Intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the Intestine, and it is small, then it is possible that it may be restored in some people; for this reason, because I have seen someone who was wounded in his belly by the impact of a lance, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old; and it became a fistula, and excrement and wind came out of it.
Therefore, I applied a treatment, until I was not confident in its healing; nor did I cease to refine it, until he was healed and the place was consolidated.
After I saw the place already consolidated, I feared for the sick person, lest an evil accident should befall the sick person in his belly; and no such evil accident befell him from that. And he remained in better dispositions, eating and drinking, and having intercourse, and entered the bath, and exercised in his daily activities.
And now some experimenters have said that when a wound occurs in the Intestine, and it is small, then it should be tied in this manner. And it is, that you should take ants of large heads. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, and place one ant of those that have an open mouth upon the two lips of the wound. When therefore it sits upon them, and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres and does not loosen.
