Then place another ant near the first, and do not cease to do this with ant after ant according to the quantity of the entire wound.
Then reduce it, and suture the wound. For those heads remain attached to the intestine, until the intestine is fed, and healed, and no harm befalls the sick person at all.
And now it is possible that the intestine be tied also with a thin thread: that which has been scraped from the animal's intestine, attached to it after it is introduced into the needle.
And it is so that you take the extremity of this thread from the intestine, and much of it is scraped off. Then tie a thin linen thread wrapped around its extremity. Then introduce that thread into the needle, and sew the intestine with it. Then return it to the rupture.
And this method of suture with ants and intestine is only according to the path of trust and hope.
Know that a wound that is large, wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines: then there is no ingenuity except for refinement, nor is its healing at all.
Concerning the treatment of ulcers and fistulas.
Know that any wound or abscess, when it grows old and becomes an ulcer, does not heal, and continually discharges pus, and cannot be cut away: then it is universally called a fistula, wherever in the body it may be, and we call it Zuchemen.
And a fistula, in truth, is a filtered, hard, white knot; with no pain, having the concavity of a bird's feather; and for that reason some call it Risati, that is, feather.
And it is in some hours moist, which discharges pus. And perhaps the moisture is cut away in some hours. And sometimes this moisture is abundant, and sometimes scarce; and sometimes thick, and sometimes thin.
And I already mentioned in the division, when I spoke of wounds, that every ulcer which does not heal, nor flesh grows in it, is not due to anything other than one of nine causes.
Of which one is, either because of the scarcity of blood in the body, or because of its bad quality; or because between it and the edges of it is hard flesh, preventing the growth of flesh; or because there is much filth and impurity; or because the ulcer itself is putrid, and the matter that is applied to it is of bad quality; or because the medicine is unsuitable for its treatment; or because the corruption prevailing in the region is a type of pestilence, or by its property in the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta, where the digestion of illnesses is difficult, and the healing of boils is delayed by nature; or because there is one bone, or a number of bones in it.
And I have already shown all these causes and their signs, and their treatments with the medicines in the division; and it is necessary here to make you know the treatment of Fistula and Zuken, which is done by surgical means with the hand.
Know therefore, that Fistula occurs in all members of the body. For from among them are fistulas that reach large veins and arteries, or nerves, or sinuses; or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to one of the ribs; or to one of the vertebrae of the back, or to any of the joints of compound structures, such as the hand and foot; and from among them are those that proceed to the proximity of the main limb, and similar ones.
Therefore, which of these fistulas is in this manner, is from among illnesses of difficult healing, and does not receive treatment, and the occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
If, however, there is any fistula whose depth does not reach these places that I have mentioned; and you hope and trust in its cure, and wish to achieve knowledge of it, then take a probe, made of air or iron, if the fistula tends in a straight line, and investigate it with it.
If, however, there is twisting in the fistula, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead. Since lead, with its gentle body, is alleviated and curved around the twisting.
If the fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to indicate over it with a probe. Therefore, irrigate from those openings of its openings.
For the moisture with which you irrigate it goes toward the other openings and runs from them.
Then inquire thoroughly, according to whatever method is possible for you, to ascertain whether there is a bone, or a nerve, and the cavity of the fistula is distant or near; or whether it is a single fistula with many openings, and stand over all of it with your utmost strength, with your questioning, by which you question the sick person, and the discovery of pain upon pressing with your hand upon the location, and similar indications and causes of accidents of the abscess.
When, therefore, you have stood over all of it in this true manner, then proceed to the treatment with confidence: and it is that you should then consider whether the fistula is near, visible, or in a safe place distant from a joint, or from a nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places I have told you: then cut the fistula according to what has been said by me before, and remove whatever is in it by filtration and corrupt flesh, that arises within its opening from flesh, or boils, and similar things; and treat it until it is healed.
If, however, the fistula is of deep and distant depth, and is straight, then it is necessary that you cut it in depth as much as possible for you. Then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh.
Then apply strips soaked in sharp medicines, and insert them into the cavity of the fistula that you have grasped with the iron. Do this with it several times, until the sharp medicine has corroded everything remaining in the cavity of the fistula from the corruption.
Then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh, until it is healed.
If, however, it is not healed by that, then cauterize it according to what we have narrated.
If therefore the cause of the fistula is bone, and it is confirmed to you, then seek it out and cut it according to what I have told you: if nothing prevents you from a vein, or nerve, or major member, as we said.
If therefore bone is found by you, and there is something of corruption and blackness in it, then scrape it until all its corruption is removed. Then treat it with that which makes it flesh, until it is healed.
If it is not healed, and discharges pus as it did before, then know that you have not achieved the complete eradication of the corruption.
Therefore, expose it again and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with all your strength.
Then restore it. If it is healed, then good; and if not, then know that the corruption is in a distant depth of the body, which you have not reached. Therefore, you have no skill in it, except to leave it to nature.
If a small bone is corrupted, and it is possible for you, extract it with thin forceps suitable for it.
If, however, there are many bones, then carefully extract all of them, and do not leave any of them behind with your strength.
If, however, some moisture creates an obstacle for you, and there is no fear or mistake in cutting them: then cut those moistures and flesh, if there are any there, and free the bones.
If, however, you are not prepared to remove the bones from your time, then place over the wound that which will cause it to fester; and leave it, until that which is surrounding those bones from the flesh and moistures festers.
And guard the wound so that it does not heal. And constrict the section which you have cut in your operation. Indeed, place over it, if you fear, a cloth soaked in ground sulfur with oil; or a cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment, or some other green ointments.
For when those moistures, namely, fester, and the bones are separated from them, the removal and extraction of them is made easier.
If however there is one large bone, such as the bone of the leg, or the bone of the hip, and similar to them, and that which is corrupt from it is a large part: then scrape it with the utmost scraping, until that blackness and corruption is removed. Then restore the wound.
If however that which is corrupt in it is a large part, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it, and to cut it all the way to the place where the corruption has reached: then treat it until it is consolidated.
And I relate to you the case of a man in his leg, so that you may set it as an example and assistance for your operation.
There was a young man about thirty years of age, who suffered pain in his leg, from a cause that arose within his body, that is, from the interior, until the matters reached the leg, and it became greatly inflamed: and there was no external cause.
Therefore, time passed with the error of the physicians, until the abscess was opened, and many matters rushed in with it. And his treatment was delayed until the leg became fistulous, and many openings were made in it; all of which discharged pus and bodily humours.
Therefore, a multitude of physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no wise practitioner of the manual art among them, until I was called, and saw his leg and the matters that flowed from those openings with great force; even the entire body of the man was already decaying, and his complexion was yellow.
Therefore, I inserted a probe into one of the openings, and the probe reached the bone.
Then I examined all the openings, and found that they reached each other from all parts of the leg.
I then hastened and cut over one of those openings until I uncovered part of the bone; and I found it corrupted; already corroded, and blackened, and rotten, and perforated, until it had penetrated down to the marrow.
I therefore removed what was exposed to me, and prepared it from the corrupted bone.
And I judged that there was nothing in the bone except that corruption which I cut away and removed. And I had already eradicated it.
Then I placed the restoration of the wound with consolidating medicines for a long time. And it was not consolidated.
Then I tried, and I found a second layer of bone above the first layer: and I found continuous corruption with the bone: and I cut away what appeared to me also to be from that corruption.
Then I attempted its restoration. And it was not restored.
Therefore, I probed it again, and did not cease to cut the bone piece by piece, and to attempt its restoration: and it was not restored, until I cut approximately a palm's breadth from the bone, and removed it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines, and it quickly consolidated and healed: and this repetition in its operation and section was not necessary, except on account of the weakened state of the infirm person, and their scant tolerance, and my fear for them of death; since it happened to them at all hours, due to an excess of evacuations, from ill syncopes.
Therefore, they were healed with complete recovery: and hard flesh grew in the place of the bone, and a good condition was established in their body, and their strength returned: and they returned to their normal state: and walking caused them no harm at all.
If, however, a projecting bone in the body has already been fractured, then it is necessary to saw it, according to this method: and it is to take a bandage, and to tighten it on the end of the protruding bone; and to order someone to extend it upwards; and to place another bandage of thicker wool on the first bandage. Then tie it over the flesh which is under the bone; and extend its end, so as to draw the flesh downwards; and you uncover the flesh over the place that you wish to saw, so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
Place wood, or a board, properly beneath the bone below; for when you do this, it does not prevent you from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be over the corrupted place for a short time, so that there is no corruption in the hollow of the bone; and it does not appear in its appearance to the senses, and forces a sawing of it another time.
If the bone is corrupted, and is not protruding, but rather its part is continuous with another part, and the corruption is in its middle, or in a part of it, then uncover the flesh from all sides completely. Then place wood underneath.
Afterwards saw it from the first part, where the corruption is, until the sawing is continuous from the other part; and let the sawing be along the length from the corruption for a short while, as we have said.
If the corruption is in the joint, cut the corrupted joint itself, and saw the bone where it is continuous on the other side.
If, however, the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no remedy in it except abrasion.
If the corruption is in the finger of the hand, or in the finger of the foot: then the matter in it is very difficult. But you must investigate how the corruption appears to you. And abrade it, and cleanse it according to whatever arrangement is possible for you, and with whatever remedy rectifies itself for you: when it does not cause you opposition from a vein or nerve.
And know that there are many incisions and saws for cutting these bones, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness and thinness, and their size and smallness, and their hardness and their rarity. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare a suitable instrument for every kind of operation.
And know that these operations indicate to you the kind of instrument you need, when you have long study with you. And the knowledge of the methods of this art is a witness to the ways of illnesses; for then you will find for yourself what is appropriate to it from instruments for every sickness.
And I am firmly establishing for you, at the end of this chapter, the number of instruments, which you will set as examples upon which you will cut, and the proof by which you will test others.
Shape of another saw.
Shape of the razors' joints. Let the head of this razor be, according to the shape of a star-shaped nail's head; and let its points be according to the shape of the points of an elischetfegi.
And it is not suitable, unless the heads of the joints are rubbed with it, when they become corrupted, or the bone is large and great.
Shape of the larger razor.
Shape of the broad razor.
Shape of the razor in which there is a concavity.
Shape of the other, slender razor.
Shape of the other razor with a curved end.
Shape of a small, slender, taut razor.
This is similar to a saw, and the end is like a saw: it is shown in many places by the cutting of bones.
Shape of the razor suitable for the cutting of that which is perforated in bones.
Its end is triangular, with sharp points.
It is made of Indian material. And similarly, all that we have said concerning razors, and chisels, and saws should be done.
Concerning the incision of extremities, and the sawing of bones.
Extremities putrefy, either from an external or internal cause.
Therefore, if you have treated that corruption with medicinal remedies, and the treatment does not help, and you see the corruption extending into the limb, to which nothing reacts: then it is necessary to cut that limb, to the point where the corruption reaches, so that it escapes from death or from the greater harm that is caused by the loss of the limb.
And the sign of this happening is that the limb darkens until it smells foul: as if fire had burned it, or it rots after the blackness; until that rot is cut into, reaching the limb itself, and creeps throughout the whole body.
Therefore, hasten with its incision, and likewise if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of certain poisonous creatures, such as the sea scorpion, and viper, and rattlesnake, and similar ones.
If, therefore, the corruption or injury is in the extremity of a finger, then cut off the finger, and do not give space to the corruption to spread to the rest of the hand.
And likewise, if it happens in the hand, then cut it off at the root of the forearm, and do not give space to the corruption, until it reaches the forearm.
And if it invades it, then cut off the arm at the elbow joint.
If however the corruption passes, and you see it approximately at the elbow, then in that is the death of the sick person. And to administer beyond that from treatment, is a matter of skill.
And similarly do with the foot, when corruption invades a toe, then cut it off at one of the joints; and if it reaches the sole of the foot, then cut off the entire foot. If however it ascends to the knee, then cut off the leg at the knee joint.
If however the corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no remedy in it, except for release, and leaving the sick person to die.
And the method of cutting or sawing off a limb is to tie the ligament below the one you wish to cut, and tie another ligament above the spot. And let the attendant stretch one ligament downwards, and let the attendant stretch the other ligament upwards. And separate the flesh that is between the two ligaments with a broad spatula until all the flesh is exposed. Then it is cut or sawn.
And it is necessary that you place linen cloth on all sides, and that the saw not touch a healthy part; and that the sick person suffer added injury, and a hot abscess.
If, however, an internal bleeding occurs while you are operating, then cauterize the place quickly; or put some powders that stop the bleeding upon it. Then return to your medication, until it is completed.
Then bind the wounded limb with a bandage suitable to it, and care for it until it is healed.
And I tell you that it happened to a man in his foot, this same happening that I tell you.
That is, because it happened in his foot with a blackness, with a burn resembling the burning of fire: and that corruption first happened in his toe, until it began the whole foot.
Therefore, the man hastened from himself, after he saw the corruption fall upon the limb with its intensity, which he found from its pain and burning: and it fell upon him at the joint, and he was healed.
And after a long time passed, that same corruption happened to him in the index finger of his hand.
Therefore, he came to me, and I attempted to recover that superfluity with what I placed upon his hand from medicines after the purification of his body: and the superfluity was not repelled, and it began to proceed to another finger: until the corruption began in the hand.
Therefore, he called me to cut his hand: and I did not want that, hoping from me upon the repulsion of that superfluity: and I also feared for him at the incision of his hand death. Because the man's strength was over the fall.
Therefore, after he despaired of me, he returned to his land: and it came to me concerning him, that he hastened and cut his entire hand: and he was healed.
And I have not brought forth this narration, except that it may be a help concerning that which falls from the kind of this sickness: and that it may be a signification by which it may be signified, and according to which it may be done.
Concerning the treatment of Sinuses, and the quality of clysterization with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in certain fleshy limbs, and the space of the abscess is prolonged, until it forms a collection of pus: then it breaks, or is pierced, and that which is in it of pus comes out, and a hollow place remains as if it were a vessel: and the skin which is over it is like a cloth which has now become thin: and it does not reach its final stage of corruption, nor does it affect the mouth, nor the nerve, nor the ligament: it deserves to be called a Sinus; and it is not called a Fistula: but if its space is prolonged, until the corruption affects any of these members, then it is called a Fistula, or zuken.
And its treatment is to cut that entire skin as it rolls back, and especially when it is thin and made like a cloth; and you are confident that it does not adhere to the place, because of its corruption.
However, if you hope that the skin adheres, and that corruption does not result from that final consequence; and if there is thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with a clyster; and it is, that you consider the Sinus.
Then, if it is large, and the pus that is emitted from it has a foul odor, then clysterize it with an Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar, and oil, and ziniar, taking equal parts, to be mixed in a vessel; and let them be boiled over the fire, until the medicine reddens; and it becomes the thickness of honey.
Then take from it what you need, and dissolve it with water and honey, and clysterize the Sinus with it; and constrict its opening, and leave the medicine in it for the amount of two hours.
Then extract it with pressure; do this for so many days, until the Sinus is cleansed, and the foul odor is removed.
And sometimes it is clysterized with ash water, when this medicine is not present to you; and it is, that you take the ashes of vine wood, or the ashes from the trees of the holly, and pour water over it. Then strain it, and clysterize with it, until you are confident that the Sinus is now washed.
If the sick person does not tolerate Egyptian medicine, nor ash water, then clysterize him with water and honey, to which some crushed ziniar has already been mixed. Or clysterize him with honey and wine mixed, since the property of honey is to cleanse and wash; and wine causes the sinus to adhere, especially when there is an excess of astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you do this repeatedly, and are confident that the corruption of the Sinus is now removed, then clysterize him with that which causes flesh to grow there.
And it is to take from the dyafinicon plaster, and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or clysterize him with some medicines and other ointments, whose properties I have written in the treatise on ointments.
If the opening of the Sinus is constricted, so that the clyster tube does not fit: then enlarge it a little with iron; or place in it a linen cloth wrapped in Egyptian ointment, or in basil ointment, until it is enlarged.
And similarly if the opening is also wide, then bring together its lips with suture; and leave in it a quantity into which a clyster may enter without addition.
And similarly if the opening from which pus runs is elevated upwards, then cut it in the lower place which is in it, so that pus runs from it downwards; because when pus is constricted in the depth of the Sinus, it prevents flesh from being generated in it.
If it is not possible for you to cut the Sinus approximately below the place you want, then try to prepare the limb in such a way that pus runs from it with ease, as is prepared for you. Try to prevent any pus from remaining in it; and take strengthening medicines that are placed on the wound, suitable to what you wish. And spread them over a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such a size that it covers the entire Sinus. Then cut with scissors a hole in the direction of the Sinus opening, slightly larger than the Sinus opening. Then spread gentle ointments over another cloth, according to the size of the hole; and place it over the first, at a distance from the Sinus opening, so that the pus may be discharged more easily from it; and do not remove the large cloth, except after some days at a time.
However, the one over the sinus opening is the one you remove every hour, so that what collects in it from pus may exit; and so that you may know whether the matter running from it, is much or little; or digested or undigested.
And with this again consider the location of the sinus itself, whether its master feels pain in it, or whether there is no pain, and whether there is an abscess in it, or not.
Therefore, when your treatment proceeds in this way, and you see the matter that exits from the sinus opening, then know that the sinus is now adhering or is approaching adherence; then dissolve it in the morning, or on the third day; and when you dissolve the wound, then replace the cloth and renew the ointment.
However, if thin matter exits from the Sinus after a long time, then do not despair of its adherence, for its healing is near, because there are people whose flesh generation is slow in their wounds, and there are those who act contrary to that.
However, if undigested pus exits from the sinus after many days, then know that the sinus does not adhere.
If, therefore, the adherence of the sinus is delayed and its condition prolonged, then add to the drying of the medicines, but let the physicians make them, so that the medicines are moist in their substance and dry in their power, such as the dyafinicon ointment, when superfluity of addition from zegi is added to it.
And from the last remedies used for treatment, is to take dyafinicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil, and sprinkle upon it old wine, equal in its substance.
Then mix it with that, and use it.
And it now does a similar action to this, when you do not have dyafinicon ointment present, honey, when you boil it until it thickens, and use it. Or take myrrh, and aloe, and frankincense: grind the whole, and powder it over honey while it is over the fire. Then line a cloth with it, and bind it over the sinus; and line with the honey you boiled, until it thickened; and powder spices over it, and place over the cloth, and bind it.
And sometimes long aristolochia, and the roots of the celestial lily, and the lily, and the herb flour, and centaury are administered in it. Use them, simple or mixed, grind them, and sieve them: and powder them over the honey I spoke of, and use it.
If the sinus condition persists and is not cured by this treatment, then know that there is corruption deep within it; or it is already making an impression on the bones, or on the other hard members, or ligaments; then treat it with the treatment of fistulas, as was previously described.
Concerning the treatment of whitlow, and of a worn-down nail, and the incision of a grafted finger, and of the section.
A whitlow is an abscess that arises beneath the nail of the thumb of the hand, or of the foot; and perhaps it arises on the other fingers.
Therefore, when its condition prolongs itself, and its treatment is neglected, and the abscess becomes a hot abscess, or it becomes corrupted and emits pus, until it corrodes the root of the nail; and perhaps it corrupts the whole of it, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, until there is a foul odor; and the end of the finger becomes wide, and its color becomes dark.
Therefore, when you treat it, with what we have said in division, and your treatment does not help, then it is necessary to cut with an iron the entire excess that remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For the cautery is very helpful.
If however the bone is sound, and the nail also healthy, and the empty additions of the nail now press the flesh inwards, and begin to prick it and injure it, then it is necessary that you place a thin radium under the angle of the nail that pricks the flesh, and drive it upwards; and cut the flesh with ease, and put on that which remains of the flesh burning, corrosive medicines, until all of it is removed. Then treat it with ointments, until it is healed.
If, however, corruption has already impressed itself upon the bone, then it is necessary to cut that bone and extract it, for it will never be healed while corrupt bone remains within.
If you see corrosion and corruption advancing into the finger, then cut it at one of its joints, according to the preceding description of it. Then treat it until it is healed.
If, however, there is abrasion or impact of the nail, and a severe pain occurs, then it is necessary to first bleed the weak person. Then cut the nail with a sharp spatula in a transverse cut from above to below.
And be careful that the cut does not reach the flesh beneath the nail, for you will cause severe pain to the afflicted person by that, and it will be a cause of added flesh growing in that place.
Then treat the place with that which soothes the pain.
The extra finger, which is born in some people's hands, is perhaps entirely fleshy, or perhaps contains bones in some of them, and perhaps a nail is on it. And some of them are born at the root of the joint, or their origin is in some of the finger's knots. Therefore, the one that arises in some knots of the finger does not move; and the one that arises at the joint of the finger perhaps does move.
Therefore, the one that is fleshy among them, its incision is easy. That is, to cut it at its root with a broad spatula.
But the treatment of the one whose origin is in the root of the joint is difficult. Therefore, abstain from its incision.
In the one, however, that is born on the finger at the last of the nodes, then it is necessary to cut first its flesh with a round incision up to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for it. Then treat it until it is healed.
The consolidation that occurs between the fingers often happens either by the nature of the human being, that is, it is born with it, or from the consolidation of a wound, or from the burning of fire, and similar things.
Therefore, it is necessary to separate that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form. Then place linen strips or cloths soaked in rose oil between them, so that they do not consolidate quickly, and separate them: or place a thin sheet of lead between them, until the place is consolidated as it ought to be.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs between certain fingers and the palm, then cut that consolidation, according to what is prepared and suitable for the shape of the limb.
Concerning the incision of a defect.
Ulcers are veins wrapped, thick, full of melancholic superfluities which occur in many members of the body: and their effect is most often in the legs, especially in the legs of those traveling and working, and carrying burdens.
Therefore, it is necessary to administer a shaking of the body from black bile, sometimes with a forceful shaking.
Then, the patient's veins should be opened.
The treatment of them with iron is according to two methods. One of them is that they should be cut, and the black blood drawn out. And the other method is that the vein should be raised, and the entire one drawn out.
However, its incision is according to this method. First, soak the leg in much hot water until the thick, foul blood is dissolved. Then bind the patient's leg from the upper part of his thigh, down to the lower part of the knee, with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one, or two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then raise, that is, draw the blood in your hand from the lower part of the leg, up to the top, and from the top, it is necessary that the phlebotomist first attend the patient. Then cut the nail until further down: until there issues forth from the blood a quantity which you see sufficient, and which the strength of the patient tolerates.
Afterwards tie it: and order him to abstain from foods generating black bile: and repeat the evacuation and phlebotomy, when the veins are filled, and that harms the patient.
Its elevation, however, is according to this method. Shave the leg of the patient, if there are many hairs on it. Then introduce him into a bath: or embrocate his leg with hot water, until it turns red, and the veins are prominent: or let him exercise with vigorous exertion, if a bath is not available to him, until the limb is warmed.
Then cut the skin in a straight line along the vein's section: either at its last point near the knee, or at its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and scrape the vein from all sides until it appears to the senses: and at its appearance, you will see it sloping from red to black. When it thus emerges from the skin, you will see it white like a string.
Then introduce a probe under it, until when it is lifted and emerges from the skin: suspend it with a blunt, gentle hook.
Then cut another section near that section by three fingers' breadth. Then scrape the skin from above the vein until it appears. Then lift it with the probes as you did, and suspend it with another hook, as you did at first.
Then cut another section, or several sections, if you need them. Then lift, and cut it at the very last section near the heel.
Then pull it out to the section above it.
And do that until you pull it out of the section which is higher, or higher than all the sections, until the whole thing comes out: then cut it.
However, if it does not yield to you for pulling and lifting, then insert an awl with a strong thread doubled, and tie it, and pull it out; or insert a rod beneath it, and revolve your hand in every direction until it comes out. And be careful not to cut it. For if it is cut, it becomes very difficult for you to lift it, and a harmful injury enters upon the weak from it.
When therefore you have pulled it out completely, then put on the wound site wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil; and treat it until it is healed.
If the vine is contracted, having a twisting to which there is an involvement to the parts, and is not as we have said in a straight manner: then it is necessary to cut into it on every part of its twisting, and its places of involvement.
Then suspend it with hooks, until you lift it all: and be careful at your incision on it not to cut a vein, or wound: for it will be difficult for you to lift, or to extract it. Therefore preserve your prudence.
Form of the incident by which the vine is healed.
Form of the blind hook.
It does not have a curvature like the other hooks, nor a sharp end, so as not to wound a vein: and it is of a thick, smooth bend, because if it were slender, it would cut a vein by its thinness: indeed, superfluous thickness is in it, as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is produced in legs in hot lands, such as the Alizegi and the land of the Arabs, and in arid, hot lands, few in tenderness, that is, in which neither grass nor tree grows.
And perhaps it is produced in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And indeed its generation is from the putrefaction that occurs under the skin, as happens within the bodies of snakes and worms, and worms generated between the skin and flesh.
And the sign of the beginning of this vein's occurrence is that a violent inflammation happens in the leg. Then the place becomes blistered. Then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that blistering, as if it were the root of a plant, or an animal.
Therefore, when its extremity appears from it, then it is necessary to wrap a piece of lead, whose weight is from one drachma to two drachmas, around it and tie it: and leave the lead hanging from the leg.
Whenever anything emerges from it to the exterior, wrap that in lead and tie it. If it extends a great deal, then cut off a portion of it, and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it off at the root before the whole of it emerges. For if you cut it, it contracts and enters the flesh; and causes an abscess and putrefaction in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it must be gently treated and pulled out gradually, until the last part emerges, and nothing remains of it in the body; and from this vein, in some men, it emerges to a length of five palms and ten. And it has come to my knowledge that it emerged from a man to a length of twenty palms.
However, if it is cut during your time of treatment, then introduce a rod into its opening, and pierce it with a long hole, along with its body, until all the material within is evacuated; and treat the putrefaction of the place with remedies for several days. Then treat it with the treatment for abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the joint of the foot, or in the foot itself; and many openings occur in it, and a branch emerges from every opening.
Therefore, treat it, as we said in the division, and with what was previously stated.
Concerning the incision on worms generated under the skin, and it is called the Cattle Disease.
This disease is called in a certain land among us the Cattle Disease; because it often happens to cattle, and is a small single worm that is born between the skin and flesh.
And it goes through the whole body, ascending and descending; and its passage from limb to limb is shown to the senses, until it bursts a place in the skin wherever it bursts, and emerges.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humours. Just as worms and snakes, that is, roundworms and threadworms, happen in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice. Because when it travels in the body, and rises to the head, and reaches a place: then perhaps it bursts there, and emerges, and destroys the eye: and that happens many times.
Therefore, when you wish to cure it and extract it, then it is not done except at its wandering and its appearance to the sense.
Therefore it is necessary to bind that which is above it and that which is below it with a strong constricting bandage. Then cut above it, and extract it.
If it penetrates into the flesh, and you do not find it, then apply a cautery with fire to the place, until it dies.
And the greater fear is its corruption, by which it corrupts the eye, as we have said.
If however you see it already having reached the head near the eye, then bind below it the eyebrow with a good constriction. Then cut above it, and extract it.
And it is necessary that the sick person use the cleansing of his body, with medicines that dissolve bad, putrid humors.
And let him beware of foods that generate putrefaction.
Concerning the incision above the ailment Nakir.
This ailment is called Nakir in our land: and it is a pain that occurs in certain limbs, then changes from limb to limb.
And I myself have already seen it, as I relate to you. I was summoned to a woman ill in certain villages. And she uncovered her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm's cord.
And after I stayed an hour, I saw one swelling move with a quiver, as a worm moves, ascending to her shoulder more quickly than is possible: so that it is like quicksilver, when it runs from place to place.
Therefore, the pain receded from that place, and settled in the shoulder.
Then I sat for an hour, and it ran to the rest of the body, until it reached the other arm.
Then she told me that it was revolving throughout her whole body, as I had seen.
Therefore, I was amazed at the speed of its change from limb to limb. And indeed, before that I had not seen this illness with my own eyes in such a manner. However, I saw many who find pain changing from limb to limb, and I did not see it with my eyes, as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think it is anything other than because she was one of those who are from the dry lands, with detected veins:  therefore, that windiness appears changing to the senses.
And it is necessary that they not appear in this way in those who are delicate, and with moist bodies, of hidden veins.
When, therefore, you wish to cure it, and the patient feels that pain: then if it is apparent to you by sight, as we said, then compress above it and below it quickly, and cut above it until that constrained windiness exits, and cauterize the place.
If, however, you do not see it with your eyes, then treat it with shaking the body, and with things that expel winds, and soothe them with foul pills, and pills of serpent, and similar things.
Concerning the extraction of Arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their types and according to the locations in which they strike the body.
Their diversity is indeed such, because among them are those which have large points, and small ones: and concave ones, and blunt ones. And among them are those which have three angles. And among them are those with tongues. And among them are those with stars.
Those which are according to the limbs on which they fall are in two ways. For the members are either from the principal hollow members, such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lung, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and the like of these. Therefore, when it falls into any of these members, and the signs of death appear to you, which I tell you afterwards, then you should avoid extracting the arrow from them, for death follows it in whoever it is in, in many situations. And when these bad signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden deep within the member, then extract it, and treat the wound.
For the signs of the brain, when an arrow falls into it, and penetrates the bone, and emerges through the membrane that is above the brain, are: because violent headaches, and blindness, and dizziness, and redness in the eyes, and swelling, and redness of the tongue, and spasm, and confusion of the intellect, and vomiting of bile occur from it. And perhaps blood emerges from the nostrils, or the ears. And perhaps speech is cut off, and the voice is destroyed. And a white moisture like gruel emerges from the place of the wound. And from it exits something similar to the watery part of flesh.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment, and remove the arrow if it does not emerge.
However, the signs of an arrow when it strikes the heart are: that it is near the left breast; and that one feels as if it is fixed in a hard thing, not in an empty thing. And perhaps the arrow's movement is similar to the movement of a pulse; and black blood runs from the wound. And that is followed by coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting. Know therefore, that death descends without a doubt.
And the sign of an arrow when it wounds the lung is the outflow of frothy blood from the wound; and the vessels that follow the neck become inflamed, and the sick person's colour changes. And he exhales a large, deep breath; and seeks the attraction of cold air.
If the arrow strikes the covering in the chest, then it is near the small ribs; and there is a great gasping with violent pain, with a sigh; and all the members of the shoulders move.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then perhaps undigested food will emerge from the wound.
And if the arrow strikes the abdomen and is fixed in it, something of the excrement, or a piece of the zirbum, or an already ruptured intestine, emerges from the wound. Therefore, there is no hope for its cure, nor in extracting the arrow.
If the arrow strikes the bladder, and urine emerges, and something proceeds from it to the outside, and the pain is stronger than the weakness, then know that it is mortal.
However, the other limbs, such as the face, neck, throat, shoulder blade, arm, the vertebrae of the back, the collarbone, hip, leg, and similar limbs are saved in various ways, when the arrow does not strike an artery or nerve and is not poisoned.
And I tell you what I have seen concerning these arrows, so that you may signify it in your treatment.
Because an arrow struck a man in the tear duct of his eye, at the root of his nose. Therefore, I extracted it from the other side, under the fat of his ear; and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which struck him in the fat of his eye beneath the lower eyelid; and the arrow was already hidden, and I obtained nothing from it but a small extremity, with which it adheres to its shaft; and it was a large arrow from the bows of the Turks, with soft iron, in which there are no ears.
Therefore, the Jew was healed, and no bad accident happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arabic arrow, and it is the one which has ears. Therefore, I cut above it between the two guides; and it was already submerged in his throat. Therefore, I worked on it carefully until I extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from the man which had fallen into his belly; and we thought that he would die from it.
After therefore thirty days had passed, and nothing had changed concerning his condition, I cut above the arrow; and I worked on it, and I extracted it; and he was healed, and no misfortune happened to him.
And I saw a man on whom an arrow had fallen on his back, and the wound had healed over it. After therefore seven years had passed, an arrow emerged at the root of his thigh.
And I saw a woman on whom an arrow had fallen into her belly, and the wound had healed over it, and the arrow remained inside her; and no change occurred in her condition, and the woman experienced no harm in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man, on whose face an arrow had fallen: and the wound healed, and it remained, causing him not much pain; and many similar cases to these.
And I extracted an arrow from a man of the king's leading men, which had fallen into the middle of his nose, and had already inclined slightly to the right side: and the arrow was entirely concealed.
I was therefore summoned for his treatment after the arrow's impact up to three days. I therefore found the arrow wound to be very tight.
I therefore searched for it with a fine probe, and did not feel it: and indeed he was finding a prick and pain beneath his right ear. I therefore hoped that that prick was from the arrow's tip.
I therefore plastered the area with a plaster having attractive and digestive power, trusting in myself that the area would become an abscess, and that a sign of the arrow would appear for me to cut upon it: and no event occurred in the area signifying that the arrow had reached the place.
I therefore applied the plaster to it for many days: and no event occurred, and the wound meanwhile healed: and he remained weak from its extraction for a space of days, until on a certain day he felt the arrow within his nose. And he recounted it to me.
And he recounted it to me. I therefore applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to that wound for many days, until it opened; and when I probed it, I felt a thin arrowhead adhering to the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound with that sharp medicine, until the arrowhead became visibly apparent to us; and about four months passed with him during this time.
Then, after the wound had enlarged, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it and move it, and it did not yield to extraction, I did not cease to refine and devise methods for it, until on a certain day I began to use suitably crafted forceps, in the manner that their form will be described at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And indeed physicians judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. Therefore I restored it, and the wound was consolidated, and the infirm man was healed with complete health; and nothing at all harmed him.
And I narrate to you the quality of the extraction of certain arrows: so that you may place that as a rule and signification upon that which I have not said.
Since the parts of this art and their distinctions are not encompassed with the narrative, nor does the book encompass it, and a skillful craftsman compares little things to much, and the present to the absent, and discovers a new operation and a new instrument in the descent of the foreign matters of this art when they occur.
I say, therefore, that arrows do not emerge from the limbs in which they are fixed, except according to two ways. Or by extraction from the place from which it entered. Or by the opposite of another part.
And that which emerges from where it entered is either an arrow proceeding in a fleshy place, and is drawn out, and emerges.
If it does not yield to exit at the hour in which it falls, then it is necessary that you leave it for several days, until the flesh that is around it rots, and its extraction and emergence are eased.
And similarly, if it is fixed in the bone, and does not yield to you for exit, then leave it also for several days, and repeatedly remove it with extraction and movement every day, until it emerges.
If it does not yield to you for exit after days, then it is necessary that you pierce the surroundings of the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a fine drill, until the arrow's path is widened. Then draw it out and remove it.
If however the arrow is now hidden in a place of the body, and is concealed and hidden from sight, then search for it with probes. If you feel it, then remove it with certain instruments that are suitable for its extraction.
If however you cannot remove it, due to the constriction of the wound, and due to the arrow's length deep within: and there is no bone, nor nerve, nor vein there: then enlarge it above it, until the wound is widened, and it is possible to extract the arrow.
If however it has ears by which it is held, then free the flesh fixed to them from every side, by every means you can, and if means, if the flesh cannot escape from the fracture of the ears, and their twisting, until it escapes.
And when you perform the extraction of the arrow, wherever it is, then use the twisting of your hand, with forceps to all sides, until you cause it to escape: and use the finest precision, so that the arrow is not broken, and its extraction becomes difficult for you.
If you cannot remove it within one hour, then leave it for several days, until those things surrounding it have rotted. Then try again; for it will then be easier.  But if a contrary flow of blood hinders you, then use what we have stated in the healing chapter, and preserve your skill from the incision of a vein, or nerve, or tendon.
And use every means possible to extract the arrow; and let it be with ease and calmness, or with slowness and firmness, as I have told you.
And it is necessary, when you pull the arrow, that the patient be positioned according to the manner in which the arrow was lodged. Because this is more helpful. If, however, this is not possible for you, then use what is possible for you from the figures.
However, if an arrow emerges from the opposite side, or if something from it is already coming out externally; or if the arrowhead causes a swelling on the skin near the upper part, and you see it protruding; cut over it, and let the incision be according to the amount that the forceps can grip.
Then pull it out; for it will then become easy to extract.
If, however, it is retained in the bone, then turn your hand in a circular motion until the arrow is pressed into the bone and widens its way out; then pull it out.
And if not, leave it for a few days; then try again until it comes out.
If the arrow's shaft is in it, then drive it out with it.
If, however, the shaft has fallen off and you wish to administer an impulse, then introduce a concave instrument, so that its concavity enters the arrow's tail; then drive it out with it.
If the arrow is hollow, then drive it out with an instrument entering it. For the arrow is relieved by it.
If the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to cauterize all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you to do so. Then treat it with whatever is suitable for it.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, or belly, or bladder, or side; and is close to the point you are touching with your probe, and it is possible for you to cut above it: then cut, and be cautious of cutting a vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then suture the wound, if you see fit, and treat it until it heals.
And this is the form of the forceps with which arrows are extracted.
Let the ends be similar to the beak of a bird, made of iron: when they grasp the arrow, they do not release it.
And many types are made from them, large, small, and medium, according to the size and smallness of the arrow, and the width and constriction of the wound.
The shape of the driving force is concave.
And the shape of the dull driver.
This is the blunt end, like a ray, so that its entrance into the concave arrow may be eased, and it may be driven with it.
Concerning the phlebotomy of veins.
The veins to be opened, whose use runs through the body, are thirty.
Of these, sixteen are in the head: two pulsating veins, which are behind the ears, called the two Herbs; and two arteries, which appear in the two temples; and two veins that are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, which are called the Lookers; and a prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and the vein that is at the end of the nose. And two Guidegi, which are in the neck. And two veins that are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins that are in the lower lip. And these are the veins called Algecherich. And two veins that are under the tongue.
The veins, however, which are opened in the arms, are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalic, and it is from the wild side: and the common people call it the head vein. And the Fusca. And is the Middle vein, and is composed of a branch of the Basilic, and a branch of the Cephalic: and the common people call it the body vein. And the Basilic, and it is situated on the domestic side: and it is also called the Itching vein. And the common people call it the belly vein. And the arm cord, and it is placed above the fold: and it is that in which bloodletting is done, and it is that which appears above the thumb with clear manifestation.
And the Salvatella: and it is the vein that is between the ring finger and the little finger: and it has two branches.
And in the leg and in the foot there are three veins. One of them is that which is under the curve of the thumb on the wild side. And the second is the Saphena: and its place is at the heel on the domestic side. And the vein Nesse: and its place is at the heel on the wild side.
And in the other leg there are three veins similar to those.
Concerning the phlebotomy of two veins behind the ears.
Phlebotomies of the two veins behind the ears are helpful for chronic colds, and for hemicrania, and for sahafati, and for old, bad head ulcers.
And the quality of the phlebotomy of it is according to what I relate. And it is done that the patient's head is shaved, and his nape is rubbed in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth, a good rubbing. Then the patient should tighten his neck with a bandage until the two veins appear. And their locations are behind the ears in the two depressed places of the head. Therefore, seek them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse beneath your finger, mark that spot with ink.
Then take the phlebotomy knife, and it is called the incider. Then introduce it under the vein in the skin until the phlebotomous reaches the mouth. Then elevate your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and cut the vein with the skin with an incident incision. And let the length of the incision be the amount of two joined fingers, or about that: and make as much blood come out as you wish.
Then bind it with bandages, and leave it until it heals.
And already in the beginning of the book there was incision of them and cauterization of them.
The phlebotomy of the two arteries which are in the two temples is a help for old hemiplegia, and crooked neck, and persistent ophthalmia, and excessive flow of acute tears to the eyes.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is according to what I tell you. Let the infirm person greatly strain his neck, until two veins become clearly apparent to the senses; and let their pulse be demonstrated beneath your finger. Then mark the spots with ink.
Then lift the skin above the vein upwards with your index finger, and insert the lancet cutting underneath, and lift the vein upwards. And cut it, just as you did in the other two veins: and make the blood flow according to your need.
Then loosen the constriction of the infirm person, and place your finger on the vein for an hour. Afterwards place a bandage and pads over it, and bind it tightly above the constriction: and leave it until it heals.
And already the recollection of them, and their incision, and their extraction are in the beginning of the book.
The benefit of phlebotomy of the frontal vein, after phlebotomy of the cephalic vein, is for old facial ailments: and for migraine, and redness: and foul ulcers.
And its quality of phlebotomy is as I describe to you. The weak patient tightens his neck with a bandage until the vein appears. Then take the instrument called a Fossorium.
Place the prominent point that is on the head of the Fossorium above the very vein, and strike it with a comb, or with something else in its manner, and let the blood run according to the quantity that you wish. Then release the wound of the sick person, and bind it, until it heals.
And sometimes it is opened with a wide phlebotome. However, it should not be a phlebotome with a sharp tip, like the other phlebotomes, but rather one with a broad tip. And lance it across, because the bone is near, and perhaps the phlebotome will break in it when it is slender.
The aid of the two veins that are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes is for the eye from scabies and redness; and swelling in the eye, and ailments of the face.
And the quality of lancing them is such that a weak person tightens their neck with a bandage. Then lance them, and you stand over his head.
And the lancing should be made transverse to the length a little, with a small lancet, somewhat broad, for there is no flesh in the place. For if the lancet is of slender form, perhaps it will break.
Then cause the blood to flow as much as you need. And place a poultice on them, and bind them for one night, and then remove them.
The lancing of the veins of the nose is helpful in acute fevers, violent headaches, and facial ailments such as migraine and redness, especially when it is chronic.
And the quality of its bloodletting is such that the weak man should bind his neck. Then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a thin, long lancet, and insert it into the middle of his nostril, between that which separates the nose according to its straightness. Because the vein is not apparent to the senses there, and because blood begins to flow immediately.
And you should hold your hand with the lancet for a little while, and make the blood flow according to your need. Then tie it with a bandage at night. For it will quickly heal.
The phlebotomy of the two veins of Guidegi is helpful for the constriction of breathing, and the beginning of leprosy, and melancholic illnesses, and impetigo and bad ulcers, and those that gnaw, that is, shingles.
And the quality of the phlebotomy of them is that the weak person should be held with a bandage under both sides of their neck: and the skilled person should stand over the head of the sick person, and the sick person should be seated on a seat.
Then the phlebotomist should cut the vein to a length of ample phlebotomy for a short time. Then extract from the blood a moderate quantity, or according to what you see as necessary for that. Then do likewise with the other vein.
Afterwards, loosen the bandage, and bind the two veins with a middle constriction; so that the sick person is not choked. And leave it until morning. For the wound is healed.
Concerning the phlebotomy of veins, Algeberich. Veins, according to Algeberich, are a help in phlebotomy, because they contribute to the removal of swelling in the mouth, and to the corruption of the gums; and to bad ulcers; and to the fissures of the lips, and to bad ulcers, even those that are on the nose and around it.
And the quality of phlebotomy of them is that you should make the sick person sit before you, and bind his neck with a bandage. Then turn back his lip, and look at the two veins: of which one is on the right side of the lip, and the second on its left side; and the indication of both is by the blackness of both. And that is true, because there are small, black veins around them. Therefore, cut each with a single incision.
However, if there is doubt upon you, and you do not know which of them it is, then aim at the incision of the larger of them, and the more evident of them.
And similarly do with the two veins which are in the upper lip: and indeed the custom runs most with it; and cut the two veins which are in the lower lip.
Concerning phlebotomy of the veins under the tongue after phlebotomy of the cephalic veins. However, the benefit of phlebotomy of the two veins that are under the tongue is that phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is for the suffocations that are in the throat, and the illness of the uvula, and the illnesses of the mouth.
And the quality of phlebotomizing those is such that the sick person sits between your hands before the sun; and raise his tongue, and look under his tongue on one side of it a vein, and on the other side of it a vein; and the color of both is sloping to blackness.
Therefore phlebotomize each; and beware that you do not put your hand in the last stages of phlebotomizing them. For under them are arteries, and perhaps a flow of blood will occur from those arteries.
The three veins, however, which are phlebotomized in the elbow, are those in whose phlebotomy custom has run in all men.
And their phlebotomy is done according to two methods. Either by puncturing with a broad myrtle phlebotomize: or olive to the subtlety of the slope: or by cutting with a knife phlebotomize, and it is Alnesil. And this is its form.
And from them are species broad, and subtle according to the wideness of the veins and their constriction. The form of the myrtle phlebotomize be broad, as you see. And it is suitable for opening concave, full, proceeding, apparent, large veins, and those that contain thick, turbid blood.
And this phlebotom is of less width, and more subtle extremities.
And it is suitable for the phlebotomy of thin veins, which contain thin, yellowish blood.
The Basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, is a help in phlebotomy. Because it draws blood from illnesses of the throat and neck, from there following to the chest and abdomen; and it befits the phlebotomist, when phlebotomizing it, to be cautious and to be above fear from it, for beneath it is an artery. Therefore, if he errs, and adds to the phlebotomy puncture, he will cut that artery, and a flood of blood will result. Therefore, it is necessary that one not phlebotomize it with a phlebotomy-piercing instrument; rather, one should cut it with an Alnesil.
If the Basilic vein does not appear clearly, then it is necessary that one seek another, or substitute for it another, or seek a branch of it; or one should phlebotomize the brachial vein instead. For it is manifest, and cut it with the phlebotomy Anesil, as we said.
If, however, you wish to phlebotomize that same one, then it is necessary before the constriction of the arm, that you touch the spot until you know the location of the pulse. Then mark the spot with ink, afterward the arm, and cut the vein with a concave incision with a phlebotomy Alnesil, as we said. And be careful that the blow does not fall within the space from the location of the artery.
And when you see, when you tighten the ligature, an inflation in the place you marked with ink, then that inflation is the inflation of the artery: therefore, avoid it.
If you see blood at the phlebotomy leap, like a child's urine, and it is thin blood, red: then know that it is from the artery's blood. Then quickly place your finger on it for a long space, then remove your finger. If therefore the blood is cut off (for it is often cut off), then tighten the arm, and release it; and let the sick person beware of its movement; and let it be upon his fear.
And do not move it for several days, until it is healed.
If however the blood is not cut off, and overcomes you; and there is no medicine present to you at your moment, then cut the artery, if it appears to you. For its extremities are contracted, and the blood is cut off.
Or take a piece of fig bark, and split it; take one half, and bind it over the place of the vein's constriction in a fitting manner, with a bandage and dressings until another day.
Then if the blood is cut off, it is good. And if not, then treat it with what preceded concerning the place of powders that cut off the flow of blood: and the cutting off of its blood is not difficult in many dispositions, because of the small place of the wound, and the possibility of a bandage from the arm.
The phlebotomy of the Fusca vein is helpful, because it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body, because it is composed of a branch of the Basilica vein and a branch of the Cephalic vein, as we said.
And it befits the phlebotomist that it be above fear from his phlebotomy, for a nerve is beneath it. If therefore the phlebotomist adds in puncturing, and finds a nerve, there occurs numbness in it, and its healing becomes difficult, and perhaps it is not healed completely.
And this nerve often appears to the senses. However, if it is hidden in some people, and is subtle, and is not shown: then it is necessary that you place your phlebotomy where you phlebotomize it, a section with Alnesil, and avoid the nerve with your strength.
If however the vein is between two nerves, then split the vein lengthwise.
Concerning the cephalic vein. Phlebotomies of the cephalic vein are helpful, because they draw blood from the head, and contribute to illnesses of the eyes.
And it is necessary in this vein properly, if you wish, that you phlebotomize it by puncturing with an olive-wood phlebotome, or with a myrtle-wood phlebotome: because it is safer than all the veins, because there is no artery or nerve beneath it.
However, it befits you in phlebotomy to avoid with the phlebotome the head of the biceps only, and to seek a gentler place; and it does not harm it if it is not found with the first stroke, so that it may be repeated on it with phlebotomy several times.
However, perhaps it becomes abscessed in some people when it is not phlebotomized on the first stroke, but that abscess does not harm them in any way.
Concerning the usefulness of phlebotomy, and the precautions to be observed in it. The quality of phlebotomy, and its accidents resulting from it, in whose correction you should consider.
First, therefore, it is something you should know that phlebotomy is not administered except in the preservation of health, and its persistence, and caution against the onset of illnesses.
And so that phlebotomy be done in one of the three veins that are in the elbow, namely, the Cephalic, and the Fusca, and the Basilic. And so that phlebotomy be performed at the beginning of spring on an empty stomach, in spring, and in summer; and in autumn and winter after eating, when signs of fullness appear.
And phlebotomy be performed on the first day, or on the third, after three hours have passed of the day.
However, phlebotomy administered in illnesses does not have a set time; but when the appropriateness and necessity for it arise, at night, or by day, and at every hour, and at every time.
And it is not necessary that children be bled until they reach fourteen years. Nor should a pregnant woman be bled, unless from the fourth to the seventh month. Nor should the elderly be bled who have already passed sixty years.
Therefore, when someone wishes to be bled for any reason, it is necessary that they cleanse their bowels beforehand with a gentle clyster, if there is a great deal of retained feces in them, so that the veins during the bleeding do not draw out putrid impurities harmful to the major limbs from the bowels.
Nor should a patient be bled who has a toctina, that is, a sickness of satiety, nor the drunkard, nor someone recovering from drunkenness, until that is removed from them. Nor the sick with dropsy.
And bleeding should be avoided again after colic, vomiting, diarrhea, and excessive sexual activity, labor, exercise, vigils, fasting, and anything that dissolves strength from the body or animal.
Then consider the subtlety of the humors before that, if the blood is thick with foods and drinks, and medications: if it is possible for him.
Then let him enter a bath, if nothing prevents him: or let him exercise with some exercise, so that the blood is made thinner.
And place his phlebotomy on his chest, around the third day, as we said: and endeavor that his chest be evacuated that day from all evil animal accidents: such as anguish, and anger, and fear: and from all bodily accidents, such as labor, and pain, and excess, and similar things.
And let him have present in his seat things with whose use the custom of men has run from aroma, and odor, and rejoicing, and things similar to them, to the extent possible.
Then let the phlebotomist sit on a cushion which is higher than the cushion of the phlebotomist; afterward let him draw his arm out, and let the phlebotomist rub it with his hand twice, or thrice. Then tie the ligament with a strap, and wrap it twice.
And let the constriction be moderate, because when constriction is unequal, or by excess in the constriction, it then hinders the course of the blood; or in relaxation, it then also hinders the course of the blood.
Then after the constriction, it is necessary that the phlebotomist rub his hands together until the veins inflate and are evident to the senses.
Then the phlebotomist should wipe the phlebotomized area with a little old oil, properly.
Then he should place his left index finger slightly over the vein itself, under the place where he wants to phlebotomize, so that the vein does not move away and the blow is not missed. Because there are veins which, like a fleeing chord in phlebotomy, and there are those which are full of airiness; and when you place the phlebotom knife upon them, they contract beneath the phlebotom knife, and the phlebotomist is deceived, and the phlebotom knife does not open the vein; and if it does open it, it is narrow.  Therefore it is necessary for the phlebotomist to be firm and careful in all these matters.
Then he should lay down the phlebotom knife. If therefore the vein is opened at the first attempt, then it is good. And if not, then repeat it another time under that spot a little, or above it quickly, if the place is not already inflamed.
If it becomes inflamed, or the patient trembles, then leave him alone for a day or two, and do not tighten the bandage. For perhaps it will draw the inflammation. Nor let him enter a bath.
Then let him repeat phlebotomy, if he wishes.
If the opening is small, or the blood flows subtly, and you fear that not the amount of blood you desire will issue forth, then repeat the phlebotomy in the very aperture itself along the correct path, and add a little in the operation.
And do this quickly, before the place becomes inflamed, for an inflamed place is in many men at a small operation.
If, therefore, you see it already inflamed, then do not repeat on it at all, for it will not excuse anything for you. And place upon it something from the dregs of oil, for it alleviates the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than the oil itself, and than other oils.
And similarly administer the dregs of oil in all your phlebotomies, whereby you phlebotomize veins against the prohibition of blood flow: and they do this, the *tyriaca alfarach* and *sagzenea*, when placed from one of them upon the place.
For blood is made thinner and resolved when it is thick.
If a large abscess occurs at the site of the phlebotomy, for it often happens, especially to one who is not a phlebotomist except in that instance, or the vein opening is small, then quickly place on the area a sponge soaked in water and salt, warmed slightly: and bind it for one hour: for it is resolved.
And it is necessary that you do this after the exit of blood from the vein itself, with its completion, or from another vein.
If some blackness or greenness remains at the site after a few days, it does no harm: and if you wish, place something of aloe, or dissolved myrrh, or something from the juice of mint, that is, of balm or similar to it, upon it.
And very often abscesses and swellings occur at phlebotomy of the Basilica vein.
Therefore, place your hand upon it; then, if you find softness upon compression with your hand upon it, that swelling is bad. Therefore, be careful to place something upon it of that which we have said. For perhaps blood of the artery will flow from it.
But make a plaster for it with that in which there is astringency, so that the place may be hardened. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary that you extract from every man's blood according to the quantity of its strength. And that which appears from the color prevailing over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it go out until it turns red; and similarly if you see it thick, then let it go out until it becomes thin; and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for him who is full, strong, and needs bloodletting in one instance, that the phlebotomy of the vein be broadened, and that the phlebotomist be wide; and in him who is weak, let it be the opposite of that.
And it is necessary that he extract it in stages, and that the opening be narrow; and better that which is administered in the phlebotomy of the vein is that it be a transverse section, not a puncture.
And this method of bloodletting is safe from a flow of blood, and from the incision of a nerve; and is more praiseworthy, and safer than bloodletting by width and length.
And to whom it is accustomed to come near to fainting in phlebotomy, it is necessary that he eat before the phlebotomy a little bread soaked in pomegranate water; or secaniabin, if it is warmed; and extract from his blood in three or four stages.
And if he is of a cold complexion, then let him take before the bloodletting bread soaked in mulse syrup, or in a syrup of aromatized honey with spices, or in a fragrant aromatic syrup.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting, and the cause of it is the excessive outflow of blood, then it is necessary that he drink water of meat, and a subtle fragrant syrup; and let him use aromatics with althea, and let his chest be lined with it; and let him use the rest of what we said in the division in the chapter on fainting from evacuation.
Whoever wishes to purge their arm and release their blood a second time, it is necessary for one whose phlebotomy is for great evacuation, and whose strength is weak, to release the blood gradually according to the measure of strength over consecutive days.
Whoever wishes to purge their arm and release their blood a second time, and their body is strong, then let them do so after seven or nine hours from their first bloodletting.
Whoever wishes to draw blood from their body to the opposite part to which it inclines, it is necessary for them to be purged on the second or third day.
In whose body, however, there is much blood already heated and sharpened, and causing fever, it is necessary that blood depart from it in one instance, and that a large quantity be drawn out of it; and the opening be enlarged until fainting occurs; after you have observed all the conditions of bloodletting; and so that you place your hand upon his pulse at the flow of blood, so that death does not occur in place of fainting. For often that happens, when the bloodletter is unaware and negligence occurs.
And it is not necessary, when wishing to purge an arm and release blood a second time, when the vein's opening is already closed and the blood's exit is difficult, to press upon it with force, and quickly bend or extend it. For that is very harmful; indeed, leave it alone until it is bled a second time.
Or remove with sharpness the blood clot from the vein's opening, and place upon it some dissolved salt in water. And place upon it something from the tyriaca alfaroch, or sagzenea, and apply gentle pressure, until blood emerges.
If however the vein is already inflamed, then leave it and do not touch it until the inflammation subsides.
If necessity compels you to release the blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then either bleed it above that spot, or in another vein in the other arm.
However, the arm's vein should be bled at the place of the Cephalic and Basilic veins, if they are not found, and are hidden, since it is composed of them.
And the quality of the bloodletting is such that the weak person should immerse their hand in warm water until the skin reddens and the vein becomes clearly visible.
Then, a ligature should be tightened over it with moderate constriction. Then, bleed along the vein's cross-section gradually, not laterally, nor lengthwise: and let the opening be wide, and your bloodletting should be above the wrist joint for a short while.
If the flow of blood is hindered, then again immerse the hand in warm water, and let the blood run into the water until you achieve your desired result.
However, if you are during the days of summer, then you may excuse yourself from re-immersion of the hand in warm water. And indeed, the flow of blood is greatly facilitated in warm water during the winter.
And bloodletting from this vein is safer than all veins, because there is no pulse or nerve beneath it.
Bloodletting from the Saluatella vein of the right hand is beneficial for liver ailments.
And the quality of its bloodletting is such that you should grasp the vein of the hand with a bandage, or with your hand, after you immerse it in warm water, until the vein swells and is clearly visible. Then, the phlebotomist should cut across it. And if you cut it completely, it will not harm him in any way.
And be careful not to injure your hand with the lancet. For beneath it are the nerves of the fingers laid bare of flesh.
Then, return the hand to warm water, and let the blood flow in it. For if you do not return it to warm water, the blood coagulates in the opening of the vein, and is prevented from flowing.
When therefore you have drawn out the necessary quantity of blood, then place oil and salt on the vein: so that it does not quickly solidify.
And similarly it is necessary to do with every constricted branch.
However, the benefit of phlebotomy from the left hand is: because it is beneficial for diseases of the spleen; and similarly do in its phlebotomy as you did in the second equally.
The benefit of saphenous vein phlebotomy is for illnesses located in the lower part of the body, such as uterine ailments, and retention of menses, and kidney ailments, and old sores of the hips and legs, and similar illnesses.
And the method of phlebotomy for it is that the patient put their foot into warm water: and make friction upon it until the veins protrude: and tie above the joint of the foot with a bandage. And indeed the vein's location is near the heel, appearing towards the big toe: and branches from it into many branches on the front of the foot.
Therefore, phlebotomize it in its larger branch, or near the heel in its aggregation, because it is better and safer.
If you phlebotomize it on the front of the foot, then beware of the nerves that are beneath it on the front of the foot.
And place your phlebotomy instrument so that you phlebotomize it across, as if you wish to cut it: and let the phlebotomist be skilled.
If, however, the flow of blood is obstructed, then repeatedly immerse his foot in warm water, and allow the blood to flow into it until it is evacuated.
If the phlebotomist errs in the first attempt, then repeat the phlebotomy a little higher up, for the location is safe, and you have no fear of harm from it, when you are wary of the nerves, as we said.
And similarly with the Saphena of the other foot equally.
The Sciatic vein's location, as we said, is near the heel on the wild side.
And the relief of phlebotomy is for the pain of the ankle, when it is due to hot blood.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is that the sick person should enter a bath and hurry; and he should bind his leg from the side of the ankle up to above the heel by four fingers, with a long thin strip; for it does not appear except on that account.
