Therefore, when it appears, then the phlebotomist should open it according to whatever method is possible for you, or by transverse incision; and it is better either to cut it by incision, or to divide it by section. For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in many people.
For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in many people. If, however, you do not find it, and it does not appear to the senses at all, then the phlebotomist should open any branch of it, and that one which appears on the back of the foot, and towards the ear: and beware of the nerves, and make the blood run as much as you wish.
Then loosen the binding, and place a skin over the phlebotomy site, and bind the location. For it heals quickly.
Concerning Cupping, and the nature of its use.
Cupping instruments are made of horns, and of air, and of glass.
And cupping indeed is done according to two methods. With scarification, and the extraction of blood: and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification, is according to two methods. Or indeed it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And cuppings which are administered with scarification and blood extraction are fourteen places of the body. Of which one is, the cupping of the Alnocrati, and it is behind the head; and Alkehel, and it is the middle of the neck; and the cuppings of the Alhagdahim, and they are two blades of the neck at two sides together; and the cuppings of the chin, and it is under the jaw at the lower mouth; and the cuppings of the two shoulder blades; and the cuppings of the Alachosos above the end of the tail; and the cuppings of the two armpits, and they are in the middle of the two arms; and the cuppings of the two thighs; and the cuppings of the two calves.
And cupping indeed does not extract blood from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of that no strength is lost from them, as happens with phlebotomy. Nor should cupping, whether with or without fire, be administered in any illness resulting from fullness until the whole body is emptied.
If necessity prompts cupping from illness or custom, we administer it at any hour at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at whatever time it may be.
This is because there are men who, when blood multiplies within them to the point of needing extraction by cupping, find heaviness and throbbing in their head.
And among them are those who find fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. And among them are those who find itching in their face and forehead, and darkness and dullness in their eyes.
And among them is one who scratches the site of their cupping. And among them is one whose laughter multiplies. And among them is one who finds the taste of blood in their mouth: or the tongue becomes suppurated, and expels blood. And among them is one whose sleep multiplies. And among them is one who sees in their sleep blood and redness, and slain ones, and wounds, and things similar to those.
Therefore, when we see something like this, and especially if it is in the third middle part of the month, we then order that a wind-drawing procedure be carried out after two or three hours have passed during the day.
The benefit of neck cupping is that it contributes to the heaviness in the head and that which flows to the eyes. But it is necessary that it be after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the place of a head-vein bloodletting.
And it is not permissible for one of cold temperament, or one with a cold, to use it. For it brings him great harm.
And therefore, the elderly and those with cold ailments of the head should not use it.
But if one is frequently exposed to it, forgetfulness is produced in him.
And therefore, you should instruct the wind-sucker to lower his hand somewhat with the wind-sucker, fearing the production of forgetfulness.
Wind-suction, or Alkehel, is in place of the dark vein-opening and the basilica vein-opening, and therefore it is beneficial for asthma, and constriction of breathing, and fracture of the breathing instrument, and cough, and fullness.
And it is necessary that the Alkehel wind-sucker be moved somewhat, because if it is placed lower, it creates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Wind-suction, however, of the alhagdahim, is beneficial for accidental pains in the head, and for ophthalmia, and for hemicrania, and for perforation, and for pain in the tooth roots: and it is in place of the basilica vein-opening.
And it is necessary that you instruct the wind-sucker not to press his hand with the scarification, so that he does not strike an artery and blood flow occurs.
Wind-suction under the chin, however, is beneficial for sores in the mouth, and for gum corruption, and for similar ailments which are in the mouth: and it stands in place of the other vein-openings, which are in the two lips.
Wind-suction of the spatulas, however, is beneficial for alchafacham, which comes about from reflex and heat.
Wind-suction, however, of the interior parts of the two foci, is beneficial to the vein-opening of the three veins, Basilica, Fusca, and Cephalica: because it draws blood from all those small veins which are in the flesh, and those small veins draw from other larger veins until the drawing reaches the three veins.
And it is necessary that you instruct the wind-sucker not to proceed too much in the scarification, because the place is denuded of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
A cupping vessel, however, that is cupped over the alchausos, is beneficial for hemorrhoids of the anus, and for lower ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the wind-sucker to use a large cupping vessel, and that it be made of air, because the place needs strong suction, and perhaps the delicate cupping vessels are broken: and a large scarification should be made.
Cupping of the legs, however, diminishes markedly, because they draw blood from the whole body, and benefit old pains in the kidneys, and wombs, and bladder; they induce menstruation, and are beneficial for botor and ademenul; and they stand in the place of bloodletting of the two saphenous veins and alhorcobeni.
However, they weaken the body greatly, and cause fainting in many people.
And the benefit of the alhorcobeni cupping is close to the benefit of cupping the two legs.
The quality of placing the cupping vessels is that you should first place a vacuum cupping, and draw with moderate suction; and do not prolong the position of the cupping vessels, but place them quickly and remove them quickly, so that the humors come to the place with an equal arrival; and do not cease to repeat this and continue, until you see the place already red and swollen, and the redness of blood appears; then therefore scarify, and repeat the suction gradually. Then consider in the disposition of the bodies.
For whoever is of tender flesh, of rare pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once, no more, so that the place is not ulcerated.
And command the cupper to enlarge the scarification a little, and to deepen it a little; and to temper the suction in ease, speed, and smooth movement.
If there is thickness in the blood, then it is necessary that you scarify him twice. In the first instance, the path is opened to the thinning of the blood and its becoming watery; and in the second, to the meticulous scrutiny of extracting the thick blood.
If truly the blood is very foul, then let the scarification be done a third time, so that the final result is achieved.
And finally, when we wish to extract blood gradually, or a little bit, one scarification suffices for us. If we wish to extract much blood, we scarify with many scarifications.
And if we measure that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that it be scarified with a deep scarification, and the equal measure in scarification is the depth of the skin.
What is that which ought to be administered from oils at the position of Cupping, and what the cupper should beware of.
In that one whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are narrow, it is necessary to anoint the places to be cupped with opening, softening, and dissolving oils: if it is in the summer time, such as the oil of alkeiri, or bruised oil, or oil from sweet almonds, or oil from pumpkin seed.
And if it is in the wintertime, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or camomile oil, or sarbantine oil, and similar ones.
If however the superfluity is thick, cold: then the oil should be amaranth oil, or balsam oil, or white oil, or dill oil, and similar ones.
If however the cupper has large pores, tender flesh: then it is necessary that he refrain from oil, and it is necessary that these wash the places of the cupping after cupping with rosewater, or with cold water, or with soothing water, or with pumpkin water, or with purslane water, and similar ones to them.
However, he whose blood is of much moisture, should wash the cuppings with vinegar, or with myrtle water, and sumac, and similar ones to them.
However, whose superfluities are thick, let him wash his cuppings with old wine, or with water of sorrel, or with a decoction of dill, or camomile, and similar to them.
And it is necessary that cupping be avoided in a bath, or in the vicinity of a bath, indeed it is necessary that it be administered after leaving the bath by an hour, or by two hours.
And it is not necessary that anyone sleep after cupping.
What is it that cupping or bloodletting ought to be governed by before cupping?
And after it, it is necessary to consider first: then if the cupped or blood-let individual be choleric, and sharpness and inflammation overcome his blood: then it is necessary that he take things that cool, such as pomegranate, and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and secaniabin, and iuleb, and things similar to these.
And place his food poultry, and mutton sekebiget, and foods seasoned with wild fruit, and things similar to these.
And whose complexion is cold, it is necessary that he take in drink a syrup of honey, or of almiua, or alsecaniabin made from seeds, and that he take medium fragrant wine, which is between old and new, and it is prescribed that he use little food. And place his food poultry, and hempseed, and sparrows, that is, small birds, and young pigeons issiadebet.
And it is necessary that the drink on the day of cupping and bloodletting be more than the food, and it is necessary that in some men be given in the drink at the hours a tyriaca alfaroch, or a medicine of musk, that is, musk, or alesselithe before cupping, and before bloodletting, or after: in order that the principal members may be strengthened and the blood may be refined. And it is not necessary that they take it in heated drink.
Concerning cupping without scarification. However, cupping without scarification are cupping which are placed upon the liver, and the spleen, and gout, and upon the abdomen, the navel, and the regions of the kidneys, and the chest of the hip: because these members do not tolerate scarification upon them.
And they are not sought out unless either the attachment of blood from a member to a member: as we place a cupping upon the breasts in the sickness of a flux of blood from the nostrils. And we use them to resolve cold, viscous windiness in a member: such as we place a cupping on the belly, and the navel. For it indeed rarefies the member, and heats it, and removes pain on account of this, because it resolved that windiness.
It is also placed upon the kidneys, when obstruction occurs in them, or a stone. For by its virtue of attraction perhaps it opens the obstruction, or eradicates the stone from its place.
And similarly it does when placed upon the liver and spleen, concerning troublesome windiness in both of them.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty with only incision. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of lukewarm water in cases of pleurisy; and that is, that the cupping glass be filled, and be large, with warm water alone, or water in which herbs suitable for it have been boiled.
Form of cupping glass administered with fire.
Let the width of its opening be that of two open fingers, according to what we have formed: and let its depth be half a palm: and let there be a small hole on its side, approximately in its midpoint, for the needle to enter: let it be made of bronze or brass, with thick, smooth, equal, and polished edges, so that it does not harm the limb where it is placed upon it: and let there be a cross-shaped rod of bronze or iron in its center where the candle with fire is placed.
And now this cupping glass is made large, larger than we have described, and slightly smaller than that, according to the illness and the age of the one who uses it.
For the cupping glasses of children and those who are thin are different from the cupping glasses of men and those with large bodies.
The method of placing this cupping glass with fire on the limb is as follows: light a wick with fire made of flax or a small candle of wax, and place it over the middle of the cross-shaped rod in the center of the cupping glass, so that the fire is positioned approximately at the lower part of the cupping glass, so that the patient's body is not burned.
Then it is placed on the limb; and a finger should be on the hole that I mentioned, until the time when we hold the cupping glass: when necessary, remove the finger, and vapor will exit through that hole, and the cupping glass will immediately be released.
Then, ignite the wick according to its manner, and repeat it if you need it.
However, in a cupping glass administered for pleurisy with water, there is no cross-shaped rod nor hole; and it is administered only by filling it with water and placing it on the limb.
And this is its form.
And this cupping glass, the larger it is, to hold much water, the better it is.
Concerning the suspension of leeches.
Leeches are not applied in multiple places, unless on those limbs where cupping is not possible, due to their small size, like the lip, and the gums, and similar parts. Or on a limb bare of flesh, like a finger, and the nose, and similar parts.
And the quality of their use is: that you should choose leeches that are from fresh, clean, and putrid-free waters.
Then they should be left in fresh water, day and night, until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their bellies. Then the body should be first evacuated with bloodletting and with cupping. Then wash the sick limb until it turns red. Then place them upon it.
When, therefore, they are filled and fall, and suction of the place is possible with the cupping glass, then it is more useful: and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then with much water, and it should be rubbed, and squeezed out.
If the flow of blood, after the falling of the Sanguisug, continues and is oozing: then dip a linen cloth in cold water, and place it over it, until the oozing stops.
If the blood multiplies, then powder crushed zegi, or gallnuts, and similar things from astringents, over it, until the blood stops.
Or place the halves of shelled beans on the area: and let them be left until the beans adhere to the area, for the blood stops.
And if a repetition of the Sanguisuga is necessary, when possible, change them.
If, however, the Sanguisugae are prevented from suspension, then the place is to be wiped with fresh blood. Or pierce the area with a needle until some blood comes out. Then place them, for when they feel some blood, they immediately adhere.
When therefore you wish them to fall off, sprinkle some crushed aloe, or salt, or ashes over them: and they will fall off immediately.
This chapter is also very necessary in the art of medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of accidents in bones.
Know, O Sons, that the foolish among physicians and the common people boast about this chapter, and those who have never examined the book of the ancients, nor read a letter from it.
And because of this reason, this method is private among the sciences in our land. And I have never encountered any good in it at all, and I have not obtained that which I have obtained from it except for the length of reading the ancient books, and the study over their understanding, until I extracted the knowledge of it from them.
Then I adhered to experiment and study for the length of my life.
And now I have described to you from it in this chapter all that contains my knowledge; and upon that which passed my experiment, having shortened it for you, and delivered it from the occupation of elongation, and shortened it by the end of shortening, and shown it by the end of declination; and I have formed in it many forms of instruments that are administered in it, as it is from the addition of declination, as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary, as we say in the prologue of this chapter, to summarize the discourse, and the chapters, the understanding of which compels the artisan in the first place to stand upon their truth: namely, so that you, and those who are ready, may know, or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when a fracture, or dislocation, or twisting, or fall occurs to someone, then it is necessary to hasten first to bleed him, and to release him, or both at once: if nothing prevents it, such as weakness of strength; or is he to whom something of these has happened a child, or a decrepit old man; or is it a time of great heat, or great cold intensely.
Then let him be content in his food, with cold vegetables, and the flesh of birds, and kids: and forbid wine, and thick meats, and filling from foods and every food that fills the veins with blood. Until when you are sure of the hot abscess, and do not fear the effusion of matter to the limb, then let the sick man return to his first regimen, with which his custom runs.
When, therefore, the fractured bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the sick person be fed with nourishing foods, with much, thick, heavy nourishment, in which there is viscosity: such as foods made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish: and thick wine, and similar things to them. For with this regimen, the coagulation of the fracture is made quicker and better.
And know that fractured bones, when they are in men who are in early old age and decrepit, are not possible to be joined and consolidated according to their nature always, due to the drying out of their limbs; and those that are from them at the end of their softness are consolidated and joined together, like the bones of small children.
However, nature connects upon the fractured bone from every side something similar to glue, in which there is thickness, by which it adheres and binds it, until they are joined together, and bound together, until it comes to the end of its strength and firmness, as it was in the beginning, until nothing hinders it from its operations; and for this reason it is necessary that you place the sick person's nourishment, foods in which there are heaviness, and viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of the limbs; for the fracture of the leg is different from the fracture of the skull; and the fracture of the breastbone is different from the fracture of the spine; and similarly, all the other limbs are different from one another; and I have come with a recollection of every type of fracture, explained in its own chapter, distinct from another. And the types of bone fracture are diversified even within themselves.
Because its fracture occurs in an arched way, besides which splinters happen in it. And sometimes its fracture occurs along the length of the bone; and its fractures are jagged, that is, splinters and projecting additions, as are in a reed when a knife is sharpened for writing. And not projecting; and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption at the head; and there is a fracture with a small fissure.
And each type has a specific instrument or method for its restoration, according to which its reunion will come about, distinct in its locations.
And from that by which the fracture of the bone is recognized, there is its twisting, and its own prominence; and its appearance to the senses, and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
Therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in the location, nor sound upon touch when you touch the bone with agitation, nor does the patient experience great pain, then there is no fracture there, but it is possible that there is twisting, or a small fissure: therefore, it is not necessary to move it with the hand and compression at all, but rather place upon it remedies whose reunion comes about, after the time that suits the location: then a thin bandage is tightened.
And know that when a place is bowed and split in two, besides the struggles which occur in it, nevertheless, every part is removed from its counterpart: then it is necessary for you to begin your work of straightening it, making it equal before a hot abscess happens to it.
If however a hot abscess happens to it, then leave it for a few days, until the abscess subsides. Then make it equal according to whatever method is possible through gentleness and skill.
And know that its restoration and its equalization is easier than in a bone where struggles occur.
Then tie it, according to what its remembrance will bring to you.
If however there are struggles in the bone, then an extension of this fractured limb is necessary, from two parts, if it is a hand or a foot; or with your hand, if the limb is small; or with two ropes, or with a rope and a hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb on an equal spot, according to its natural shape, until, when the fractured limb parts are extended, then endeavor to restore those additions to their places with all your skill, and in whatever way you can: and beware, according to your strength, that pain or injury does not result from your operation to the weak; and strive according to your strength to apply one of the two bones with its partner, according to the best forms.
And it is necessary at that hour that you try and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see something different there, rectify it, and equalize it by the extent of your ability.
And beware of violent extension and strong compression, as many foolish ones do. For many times they cause a hot abscess or contraction in the limb through their operation, as we have seen it many times from their operations.
Then, after the equalization, and decent operation, and constriction of that limb, and rest, and tranquility, and advise the sick person regarding it. And have him beware lest he move it during his waking hours, and his sleep, during his turning over, and his tossing, and at his bowel movements, and all his movements with his utmost power. And that he beware that the limb's position not be one that will result in pain. That is, when he feels pain or injury in the limb's position, then it is necessary that he change it to another position in which he does not feel pain.
And be careful with him that his position be even, straight, so that no twisting occurs in the limb when it is restored.
And the quality of the fracture of the limb is, according to what I relate.
Know that fractured limbs are diversified in their smallness, and their size, and their shape.
Therefore, those of them which are small, such as the arm, and finger, and foot, and similar to them, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft, fine cloth. However, those of them that are large, such as the thigh and leg, it is necessary that there be a wide, hard wrapping on them.
Because a wide ligament connects the large limb, and binds it from every side with an equal constriction, so that a spot does not enter it.
Then, after your evacuation, begin to apply a suitable liniment to it, in soft linen on the very site of the fracture itself. Then begin to wrap the bandage over the site of the fracture itself with three or four wraps, according to what the limb deserves; and tighten your hand slightly with the bandage.
Then proceed with it towards the upper part from the fracture site, and tighten less than your initial tightening with which you tightened the fractured place. Then move away from the fracture site with the wrap slightly, and gradually loosen the tightening, until you grasp a large part from the healthy area.
Then take another strip, and wrap it again over the fractured site with wraps. Then proceed with the wrap towards the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be in tightening the wrap, and loosening it, according to what we have said in the first upper wrap.
Then place between the wraps soft stuffing, or cloth, with which the twisting of the fracture is equalized, if there is twisting in it: and if not, do not place anything in it.
Then wrap another strip over it. Then place on top of these wraps appropriately formed stars immediately, if there is no swelling in the place, nor a hot abscess.
If however there is swelling in it, or an abscess: then place on it that which will settle that abscess, and remove the swelling: and leave it for some days. Then tighten the stars on top of it.
And let the stars be from the halves of broad canes, shaved, skillfully shaped; or let them be from the wood from which sieves are made, which are made from pine or geraid, that is, palm branches, or calingi; and let the breadth of each star be, according to this shape and this same figure.
However, the star placed upon the wound itself should be somewhat thicker and wider than the other stars; and the length of the star should be in accordance with what is permitted by the limb's size and narrowness.
Then tighten the stars with another bandage, according to the same tightness as the first. Then tie it from above with threads neatly, according to what we said about the tightness; and it is that your tightness should be over the fracture site more; and the further it is from the fracture, the less the tightness.
And it is necessary that the middle threads be in thickness and thinness, and be of soft flax.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the operation of foolish men placing their threads from twisted flax cords, it is a great error; because the tightness falls away from them, outside the quality.
And again, thin threads are not suitable, because you will not achieve with them, through the tightness, that which you want.
And it is not necessary that there be less than a finger's breadth between the suture and the suture.
If the patient is injured by the ends of the sutures after the tightness in healthy places, then place under them soft linen or dyed wool, until nothing is injured from it.
When, however, there is a fracture with a wound, and a rupture occurs in the skin, I will come with a recollection of it in the second chapter according to its particularity.
And know that not every fractured limb needs to be bound with sutures from the first day. Because, when the limb is large, you should not place sutures upon it unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your own safety in regards to a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters, by which the ancients restored those that are placed upon a fracture and dislocation.
Method of an abbreviated common plaster, restoring a fracture, suitable for many complexions, especially women and children; because it is from that which neither heat nor cold conquers.
And it is to take from the mill dust, and it is the flour bran that hangs in the mill walls at the grinding of the millstone: and to mix it as it is, except that you sieve it with the white of an egg: and place its mixture neither thick nor thin. Then use it.
Another method of plaster, restoring fracture, dislocation, and twisting. Take of aloes, and laudanum, and acacia, and enula, and mugath, and juice of all, each 10 drams; of myrrh and aloes, each 5 drams; and alethil 20 drams.  Grind all, and sift, and mix with alethil water; or with the white of an egg, if the patient's complexion is heated. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, restores broken bones quickly, and benefits many people.
Another method of plaster, which also benefits fracture and twisting. Take of mugath, and mess, and white marshmallow, of all 10 drams; of myrrh and aloes, each 5 drams; of acacia 6 drams; of Armenian bole 20 drams.  Grind all thoroughly, and sift, and make into a poultice with water, or the white of an egg, and apply.
A method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places; and it relieves the pain that accompanies them, and the cracking and fracturing of the bone. Take wool, and soak it in vinegar and boiled oil, and place it on the affected area.
And in this plaster there is no power of restoration, but it is good for calming a hot abscess, and it specifically drives away pains.
Method of plaster which restores a fractured bone. Take leaves of the common fig and leaves of wild poppy; and grind them all, and make a plaster from both.
Method of a shortened plaster, administered in the restoration of a fractured bone, and when you desire the resolution of the remaining abscess. Take of marshmallow root, camomile, violet flowers, and herb flour, of each one part: grind them all, and mix them with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
If, however, it is inflamed, then make it with water of moist coriander, or with water, and use it.
Method of another stronger plaster for resolution, than this first one, when a hard abscess occurs during the restoration of a limb. Take of marshmallow root, linseed, pomegranate bark, melilot, marsh mallow, violet flowers, and camomile, of each one part; grind them all, and mix them with willow water, or with fresh water, or with wine. All of that according to the warmth of the limb and the quiet of its heat.
Hippocrates, however, did not say in his book that anything should be placed on a fractured limb during its restoration except a cerotum made of wax and oil: nothing else; and he recounted that it should be a medium between thickness and thinness.
However, Galen thought that things should be placed upon a fractured limb during its restoration that have a drying effect on heat, such as myrrh, and aloes and olibanum, and similar to those we have narrated.
Method of the plaster that helps for injury and pain. Take mugwort, and chickpeas, and the hairs of a man's head, cut, or bird feathers, and marsh mallows, and salt, in equal parts: grind them, and sieve them, and mix them together: and make a plaster from it.
The quantity of time, after which the bandage should remain and then be released, is as follows: if no pain or itching occurs in the sick person, and if the fractured bone does not move from its place, then do not remove it for many days. But if intense itching, or troublesome pain, or swelling occurs in that place: then quickly release it immediately and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, moistened: and immerse it in lukewarm water, and cleanse the place with it, until the itching ceases, and the pain subsides.
But if intense itching, or troublesome pain, or swelling occurs in that place: then quickly release it immediately and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, moistened: and immerse it in lukewarm water, and cleanse the place with it, until the itching ceases, and the pain subsides. Then let the limb rest for one hour. Then place upon it wool cut finely, soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil: and bind it upon it for one night, until you are certain of the absence of a hot abscess, and the swelling of the limb subsides, and its pain disappears.
Then apply a thin bandage and a small plaster: and do not tighten it with your first binding, and bind it thinly thereafter until it is healed.
If you see that the abscess, redness, pain, swelling, and all the associated problems have completely disappeared, and you no longer need a plaster and bandage, then treat it again as you did at first, equally.
If nothing from what we have said occurs to the limb, then do not loosen it, except after three, or four, or five, or seven days. And when twenty days have passed, judging from the condition of the limb as we have described, until the fracture adheres, and the flesh around it is fully consolidated, then add more bandage than your initial one, and increase the strength of the dressing, according to its previous description.
If you see the fracture site becoming dry and thinning more than necessary, then know that nourishment is being prevented from reaching it. Therefore, apply warm water to it every time you loosen it, and let it remain for all three days, and slightly ease the bandage.
Through this operation, nourishment reaches the limb, and it heals quickly.
However, what foolish bone setters do in a limb's fracture a second time, when it was not initially set correctly, and is set crookedly, is an error in their practice, and a great fear.  And if it were correct, the ancients would have written about it in their books, and performed it.
And I have seen no trace of it at all in that, and it is better that it not be done.
Concerning the accidental fracture of the head.
The types of fracture are diverse, and its shapes are many: and its causes are distinct.
For there is a fracture that occurs from striking with a sword: and the blow removes the bone completely as far as it reaches the siphac, which is under the bone, as an axe does in wood: and because of this, this type of fracture is called Axialis.
Or the sword cuts some part of the bone, and cuts its surface, and the incision does not penetrate to its innermost part: and this type of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, a Lifting absolutely.
And there is a wound of two fractures, either great or small.
And there is a fracture which is a crushing, or grinding: and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall upon a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which already approaches the tissue, which is beneath the bone: or it is on the surface of the bone: and the wound of these two fractures is also either small or large.
And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone in the subtlety of a hair: and it is a small fissure, and because of that this type of fracture is called Capillaris.
And from these is a fracture, which is made from a fall, or a blow with a stone, and similar things, which cause the lamina of the bone to enter into the interior, and a hollow is made: just as happens to copper pots, when a blow strikes them, and part of them enters into the interior.
And this happens most often in the heads of moist bones, such as the heads of children.
And all these types of fractures are produced by separate efforts, and I will return with a reminder of it all in its place.
And we know all these types of fractures by probing over them, and by investigating them with probes, and by removing the corrupted flesh that is over them.
The Capillary species, however, is recognized by this, that it is detected upon the bone, and is scraped away, and is painted over it with ink: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, consider first the accidents of the sick person: then if you see from the accidents that it signifies evident indications of fear, such as vomiting, cholera, extension, loss of understanding, loss of voice, syncope, acute fever, protrusion of the eyes, redness of them, and similar accidents in many dispositions, undoubtedly it is not cured: and if you see accidents not terrifying you, and you hope for health, then begin its treatment.
If a wounded person comes to you immediately after being wounded, and it is in the days of winter, then it is necessary that you strive for the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to every disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then it is necessary that you hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before that which is under the bone from the pannicle is corrupted, and those accidents which we have narrated occur.
If the fracture of the bone has now reached the pannicle touching the brain, and is with small pieces and drawing, then it is necessary to remove the broken and ground parts, as I tell you.
And you should shave the head of the wounded person, and uncover the bone in whatever way is possible for you, and according to the shape of the wound, and what is easy for the sick person.
If during the uncovering of the bone, a flow of blood or a hot abscess occurs, then apply to it with what is necessary. And you should fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil, and keep the wound at rest until the abscess subsides and you are secure from the flow of blood. Then begin the treatment of the bone, and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
One method is to cut the bone with a scalpel of sharp edge, and this is its form.
Then apply another scalpel somewhat wider than the first, and this is its form.
Then apply yet another scalpel wider still than the second.
And the second method is to have various scalpels available, some wider than others, and some shorter than others; and let their cutting edges be sharp, and let them be of tempered iron, or steel; and use gentleness in striking the scalpel, so that it does not strike the head and injure it.
However, if the bone is strong, hard, then it is necessary to pierce around it, before you apply the scalpels, with drills called shallow drills; and they are not so called unless because they do not pass through the limit of the bony tissue to that which is behind it; because a drill has a rounded end above that which is under its pointed head, similar to an edge, and a small circle prevents it from sinking and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it befits you to take many of these drills, each one appropriate to the thickness of the bone, until you have a drill for every skull; whose pointed end in length and shortness should be according to the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three types of drills, or trepans.
The method of piercing around a fractured bone is this: place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been pierced. Then change the drill to another place, and put that which is between each hole the thickness of a radius, or about that.
Then cut with the chisels that which is between each hole from the bone, and make it finer than you can on it from the end of lightness, as we said, until you eradicate the bone, either with your hand or with some other of those instruments that you have prepared for it: such as pliers and fine forceps.
And it is necessary that you beware with every precaution, lest the drill or chisel touch anything of the siphac.
When therefore you have encircled the bone by piercing, and it has been separated from the siphac, if it has been continuous with it, and you have extracted it, it is necessary to smooth and even the extremity of that which remains in the bone with another similar instrument, like a chisel. However, it is necessary that it be thinner and more subtle than other chisels.
If any small pieces of bone remain, and splinters: then take them with ease, with that which is at hand of your instruments. Then treat the wound after that with the dressings and ointments which we have said.
And another method of operation is easy, entirely remote from fear: which Galen said, and praised it with great praise: and this is his statement. You must first begin to uncover the portion of bone in the place where the fracture is more violent and more evident; until when you have uncovered that part, and have placed under it the extremity of this lenticular chisel, of which this is the form.
And the lenticular part cuts nothing smoothly; and its sharp part, going lengthwise on its two sides, as you see; so that the lenticular part rests against the bone, its sharp face striking the bone.
Then strike the chisel from one side with a small mallet, until you cut the whole bone with ease, as if it were turning over; and you be sure by the cloth that no accident happens to it that you would entirely fear.
And if the physician were not more foolish than men, and more ignorant of it, nor were asleep, then if anything remained attached to the bone from the cloth in certain places, he would cut it from it with the extremity of his lenticular chisel, and would free it from it with ease. It is freed from it without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the cloth, and that which is cut off from the bone is its surface, and there remain in it roughnesses and subtle irregularities, then it is necessary that you smooth those roughnesses and eradicate those irregularities with the fine razors from which you have prepared a variety of sizes, so that it may be possible for you to administer from them the most suitable and the straightest ones according to the place, according to the work that leads you, and the broken bone, and its figure. And it is necessary that you administer in your judgment, with which at first you scrape the bone, more broadly than those razors. Then use after it a finer one.
And it is necessary that you administer in your judgment, with which at first you scrape the bone, more broadly than those razors. Then use after it a finer one. And do not cease to do this according to continuity, until you arrive at administering the thinnest and finest of them all.
In every remaining small fissure, and fine fracture, it is necessary that you give care, according to that which leads to its straightening.
And it is a matter not hidden from everyone who has any study in this art, and has stood upon what we have written and shown in large fractures.
If, however, the flap is retained when you remove the bone, then it is necessary to take a linen cloth, according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the wound opening. Then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth: and do it as easily as you can over that, so that it is not weighted by this.
Then apply a wide bandage over it: and do not bind it tightly, unless to the extent that holds the wound only: and leave it with this care for one day, or two days, until you are sure of being free from a hot abscess. Then unbind it, and apply after that remedies with which superfluous dryness is overcome; such as lily roots, and herb flour, and frankincense powder, and birthwort, and similar to them: and it is that you make a powder from these remedies, which is powdered over the wound as they are dry.
And universally apply to it any medicine whose property is to cleanse, and not to bite. And beware, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment is clear; neither be there any dirt of ointment, nor any filth.
And beware, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment is clear; neither be there any dirt of ointment, nor any filth. Nor allow the virus to gather on it at all: because when the virus gathers on the membranes of the brain, it corrupts it and makes it putrid, and great harm comes upon the sick person after that; and it occurs in the head, when the bone is cleansed from it, especially when its care is neglected, blackness on its surface.
Therefore, consider then, if the accidents which we have stated befall the sick person, know that it is undoubtedly harmful.
And if blackness does not occur except from the medicine that is placed upon it, and is in the power of that medicine to cause that blackness; then it is necessary that you take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and lines with them a cloth. Then place it upon the bone: afterwards, treat it with the kinds of treatment that are suitable, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the Nose when it is broken.
Know that the upper two parts of the nose are not broken except simultaneously, or one of them: because they are two bones; since its lower part is cartilaginous, it is not broken, and only attrition, twisting, and crookedness occur to it.
If one of the two parts is broken, then it is necessary that you insert a small finger into the nostril, and straighten that fracture with your index finger and thumb from the outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape; and do this skillfully and easily. And beware that your operation does not cause pain to the sick person.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it is necessary to straighten it with the end of a radius bone, where there is a small thickness.
If, however, the fracture is in two parts, then do the same; and hasten its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, at rest from a hot abscess.
Then insert into the end of the nose a bandage of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part; or insert two bandages if the fracture is in two parts; and let the bandage have a thickness of such quantity as to fill the nasal opening.
And some of the ancient restorers said that the bandages should be soaked in butter and changed every day. However, that does not seem to me; rather, it is necessary that the bandage be soaked in egg white, mixed with mill dust. Then leave the bandage until the bone is strengthened and the cartilage hardened.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose in place of the bandage, after a soft cloth has been wrapped around it; and it does so that it holds the nose fracture more firmly, and so that the weak person is not prevented from breathing.
And this is not necessary. If you wish, do it; and if you wish, do the bandages.
If, while treating a hot boil on the nose, the nose should again be injured, plaster the nose with cerote, or with cot infused in rose oil, or with a small amount of diaquilon.
However, if a hot boil does not occur, then it is necessary to plaster it with sesame flour and powdered frankincense mixed with egg white. Then place soft lint over it, and do not bandage the nose at all.
If, however, the bones of the nose are fractured or broken into small pieces, then it is necessary to split them open and remove them with instruments suitable for them. Then sew the fissure, and treat it with that which causes it to heal and to consolidate with appropriate plasters or ointments.
If a wound occurs between the nostrils, then it is necessary to treat it with strips of cloth and apply a lead tube until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of the lower jaw, when it is fractured.
When the lower jaw is fractured, and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, then see if the fracture is only on the outside, and it does not break into two pieces, and is bent inwards; then its recognition is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, you should insert the index finger of your left hand into the patient's mouth; likewise, if the fracture is on the left side of the jaw, then insert the index finger of your right hand; and with it, gently push the curvature of the fracture outward from the inside with your other hand from the inner side of the bone, and with it straighten it.
If the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two pieces, then it is necessary to apply external pressure from both sides, in a straight line, until its alignment is possible.
If, however, movement or breakage has already occurred in the teeth, then bind those which you hope remain with gold, silver, or silk thread. Then place a poultice on the fractured jaw. Then place a doubled cloth over it, and place a large, properly made compress over the cloth; or an equal piece of sole leather, the length of the jaw.
Then bind it from above, according to the preparation for its ligature and the suitability of its application, until it is not broken; and instruct the patient to use tranquility and quiet, and give him soft drinks as food.
If, however, you estimate that something of its shape has already been altered in any way, then hasten and loosen it on the third day. Then rectify what has been altered in it, and plaster it with mill dust, with egg white, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the poultice; and place soft wool over the plaster.
While that plaster remains attached to it, and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it, do not loosen it, until it is healed; or the fracture is tightened. For this fracture is often tightened within three weeks.
If, meanwhile, a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have often said in its sedation, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider. If a fragment is already separated from the bone, or from the surrounding tissue, then be meticulous in this, so that you remove those fragments of the surrounding tissue with whatever is suitable to you in removing them from the instruments.
If truly the wound opening is constricted, then widen it with a phlebotom as much as your need requires.
Then, when you have removed that surrounding tissue, and nothing remains of it, then suture the wound opening, if it is wide; and if not, place over it plasters that are suitable for it, and cause it to heal, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fork, when it breaks.
Indeed, a fork is most often broken from the anterior part on the humerus side, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
Either it breaks and separates into two, apart from the tissues that occur on it; and it is easier to restore it.
Or, such that tissues occur in the bone; and it is more difficult to restore it.
Or, such that it is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation on it, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two attendants present, so that one of them holds the aid that follows the fractured fork, and the other extends the neck towards the side.
Then adjust the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be; and there should be neither a prominence nor a hollow in it.
If you need more extension, then it is necessary that you place under the patient's armpit a sphere of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to your necessary quantity; and extend and lift the fork, and contract the sphere with your hand, until you equalize the fracture as it should be.
If you cannot pull the extremity of the fork outward, because it has reached a depth, then it is necessary that the patient be placed on their neck; and you should place a medium-sized bolster under their shoulder, and the attendant should press on their shoulder downward until the bone of the fork that is deep is brought upward; and then straighten the fracture and equalize it with your fingers.
If, however, you feel a splinter from the fork and it moves, then it is necessary that you cut above it, and extract the splinter with ease.
If, however, it is retained in the bone, then make an incision on it with one of the incissors you have prepared for it, after you have placed under the fork the instrument that keeps the sifach; and it is an instrument of wood or iron. And this is its form.
It resembles a spoon without a concavity.
And let its width be according to that which you need from the size of the bone, and its smallness. Its length, however, also according to what is possible for you for the operation.
And it shall have two extremities, as you see: and one extremity shall be wide, and the other narrow.
If however the rupture of the wound that you made when you extracted the splintered bone is wide, and you guard against a hot abscess: then bring together the two lips of the wound with sutures.
And if the rupture is small, or you fear a hot abscess: then fill the wound with cloths and bandages according to the amount of the wound's fissure.
If a hot abscess should occur, then soak a cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and place it upon it.
A constriction of the bone, however, when it is without a wound and without incision, is such that you should place upon the bone a plaster made from mill flour with egg white. And place soft lint upon it. Then place a sphere beneath its tip, if you need it. Then take a *himematem*, that is, a wheel held by the Saracens, which is very long, and let its width be one palm, or approximately that. Then place a doubled pad over the lint and plaster. Then take a thin plank, whose width is three fingers, and similarly in length. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the plank in the place of the alhimemati that falls over the fracture and the place. Then place the alhimemati over the fracture as it revolves, and wrap it over its neck, and under its healthy joint, and under its diseased joint: and reduce it over every part, and as you see that the constriction holds the fracture in a fitting constriction: and that is, what is not concealed from you, and all of it, so that the plank is not removed from over the fractured bone. And for this it is necessary that you look after the sick person every day. Therefore, whenever the ligament softens, and you see the plank already removed, then straighten it and tighten the ligament.
Then place the sick person's sleep on his back, and place a small pillow under his joint at his sleep at night, so that with it his help may be raised from his side. Therefore, the fracture of the fork is raised by the elevation of the humerus.
Or bind his arm to his neck, and do not loosen the bandage if it does not happen in a place where an accident comes from itching or from an abscess, until twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if you see its face at that. And add to the constriction, and leave him until it is restored and coagulated.
And indeed the fracture of the fork is tightened and strengthened mostly in twenty-eight days; and sometimes it happens in some men in a shorter time than that.
Concerning the restoration of the fracture of the shoulder blade.
The shoulder blade is rarely broken, and in its broad area; and only its margins are broken from it.
Therefore, when a part of it is broken, and it is broken in its middle, and this is not recognized except by touch, then, according to the nature of the fracture, attempt its preparation. And reduce it to its natural shape by every means of skill possible to you.
Then place mill dust with egg white, and soft linen, and place a cushion of doubled cloth on top. Then place a wide splint of thin wood, according to the size of the entire shoulder blade, or a little larger.
If, however, there is a concavity under the splint in any part of the shoulder blade, then equalize that concavity with soft linen until the splint descends evenly.
Then bind it from above with a long bandage, with a fitting constriction, and firmness of the splint, until it cannot be removed from its place.
And consider the bandage every day. Therefore, whenever the bandage softens, tighten it, and adjust the splint if it is displaced from its place: and let the patient lie on his healthy side.
And the shoulder blade indeed is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When therefore this number is complete, then loosen the bandage: and you will be safe. For it is from bones whose connection is not feared, nor their separation.
If however a piece of bone projects, and pierces beneath the skin: then cut above it, and remove it. And do what we said in the fracture of the furcula from the suppuration of a hot abscess, if anything happens from it.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured breast.
The breast sometimes breaks in its middle, and that rarely happens. Its extremities, indeed, are broken and shattered by its accidents most often.
When its middle breaks, which inclines downwards, and a severe pain and difficulty in breathing and coughing occur to it, and perhaps it vomits blood, and a cavity is revealed in the fractured bone, and is not hidden from the senses, its restoration is that the infirm person be laid supine on their back, and a cushion be placed between their shoulder blades.
Then press his shoulders, and gather the ribs from both sides with your hands, and adjust them to their proper alignment, as much as possible and appropriate, until the shape of the bone returns to its proper form.
Then place a plaster and a bandage on it, and place on top of that a thin board of willow or chalingi, or similar light materials, after you have wrapped it in cloth. Then adjust it in binding it over the fractured bone, so it does not move; and proceed with the bandage around the back several times, and tighten it with a proper tightening.
Then check the bandage every hour, and whenever it loosens, tighten it; and if its necessity calls for its removal because of itching or corrosion occurring in the area, or pain, or abscess, then quickly untie it and remove the plaster, and correct whatever happened with its method of treatment.
Then replace the plaster, if you see it to be in place; and apply a bandage to it, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of Ribs, when they are fractured.
Know that a rib fracture does not occur except in the thick parts that follow the back, and from their extremities on the front side. And there is no friction of them unless because they are cartilaginous. And its knowledge is not hidden from sense in investigation with the fingers.
And its restoration is done so that the fracture is made equal with the fingers according to the possible manner, until the shape is made equal, according to what is necessary. Then plasters, and bind the broken bone with splints, if it needs it.
And if the rib fracture is sloping inwards, then a violent pain and piercing occurs to the sick person, like the piercing that occurs to one who has pleurisy; because the bone pierces the membrane, and also a shortness of breath, and coughing, and the vomiting of much blood occurs; and this is a difficult cure.
And already the ancients have devised many methods for it: and among them are those who say to place the sick person's foods that produce inflammation and windiness, so that the belly is inflamed and extended and the fracture is driven outwards.
We however abhor, lest a quick cause of a hot abscess be made, if it does not happen. If however it already happened, then add to it.
And some of them said: let a windy substance be placed upon the area, then it be sucked with strength, and this is similar reasoning. However, it is feared that the windy substance will draw superfluities to the area, due to the disposition of the weakness.
And some of them said: the area should be covered with wool already submerged in hot oil; and cushions should be placed between the ribs until it is full; so that there is an even bandage when you wrap it according to the roundness.
Then the sick person should be treated with the treatment for pleurisy, through food and medicine.
If a violent thing constricts the sick person, which they cannot tolerate, and the mouth stings or the covering wounds with a piercing injury, and we fear for the sick person, then it is necessary to cut over the area and uncover the fractured rib. Then we shall place upon it the instrument that preserves the fistula, the description of which preceded: and we shall cut the mouth with ease, and cut it.
Then we shall bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with sutures; and we shall treat it with plasters until it is healed.
If meanwhile a hot abscess occurs, then hurry, and pour powders in rose oil, and place it upon the area; and let the sick person be treated with what is suitable for the abscess.
Meanwhile, let the person lie on the side on which they sleep, until they are healed.
Concerning the restoration of the fractured spine of the back and neck.
A fracture of the neck bones sometimes occurs, and attrition rarely happens to them; and similarly to the vertebrae of the back as well. Therefore, when this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not, then consider. For if you see his hands already softened, and numb, and dead; and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor contract them; and when you contract them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then know that it will not heal, and in most cases is mortal.
If he moves them, and feels the contraction with the fingers and the prick, then know that the neck bone is sound, and that the patient will be healed with treatment.
If a similar thing happens to the vertebrae of the back, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not, then consider his feet. If you see that they are softened, and that what happened to the hands happens to them, then when he lies on his back, wind and excrement come out involuntarily; and when he lies on his stomach, excrement comes out involuntarily; and when he lies on his back, and wants to urinate, and cannot: then know that it is mortal. Therefore, do not labor in his treatment.
If however, nothing of that happens to him, the matter is less serious.
And the treatment for what happens from that is to attempt the calming of a hot abscess; thus, that you place on the vertebra, ground rose oil alone; or with the yolks of a roasted egg.
Place that on it several times a day, until when the hot abscess is calmed; then place on the spot one of the strengthening, drying plasters, and bind over it with a bandage; and order the sick person to use quiet and rest. And let him not sleep on the part where he finds pain until he is healed.
If it happens at a rubbed-away bone, or something that has already separated from it: then it is necessary to cut above the skin, and remove that. Then bring together the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with stitches. Then treat it with some healing ointment, from unguents, until it is healed.
If, however, the last bone of the alhausos breaks, and is the end of the tail: then it is necessary that you insert the thumb of your left hand into the anus, and adjust the broken bone with the other hand, as far as possible, and with a second adjustment.
Then place it on top of it, and remove it: and treat the wound with the previous treatment, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the Ancha.
Chapter XI.
The bones of the Ancharum rarely break. If they do break, then their fracture is not, unless it is a shattering at their extremities: and they are split lengthwise, and deflected inwards: and pain and a puncture occur in the affected place, and the leg that breaks is stunned.
And its restoration is indeed to extend your hand over it, until you stand upon the fracture as its form is.
If the fracture is only at its extremities, then equalize that fracture, according to what is prepared for you by the equalization, until it is made similar to its natural form.
If however the fracture is lengthwise, or already bends inwards, then make the sick person lie on their stomach, until its fracture's restoration is prepared for you. When therefore you have equalized it, place a plaster upon it; then place a splint of wood or leather upon it; and bind a bandage over it such that you do not fear the fracture's shifting, nor the splint's removal.
And equalize the concavity of those things with that which fills it, until the bandage takes it according to equality; and command the sick person to sleep on their back, or on their healthy side.
If however a hot abscess occurs to him, then cease from the extension of it, and its restoration, until the hot abscess subsides; and place upon it that which settles it, according to what preceded, then return to its restoration; and bind it as is necessary.
If however it occurs in the bone, splinters, or is crushed, extract it.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is that which is between the elbow and the shoulder blade.
If therefore it is fractured, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: to take a piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form, which follows.
And two bandages are tied at both ends.
Then it should be hung from a high place: and the sick person should sit on a seat. Then he should place his fractured arm over the wood, until it reaches the tickling of it, in the middle of which is attached the curve of the wood.
Then suspend something heavy above it, so that the attendant extends it downwards. Then the physician should align the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns, as it should.
And another method is that the sick person should lie on their back: and suspend their arm from their neck with a bandage. Then command two attendants, that one of them should hold that which is above the fracture with his hands, and that the other should hold below it, and that each of them should pull towards their side.
And if you wish the extension to be stronger, then tie under the fracture with one bandage, and above it with another bandage, and let each of the two attendants pull them towards their own sides.
If therefore the fracture is near the end of the humerus, then it is necessary that one bandage be placed under the elbow, and another under the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that a bandage be placed over that place and over the elbow itself.
Then straighten the fracture gently without effort, until when the fracture is straightened as it should be, and a good application is applied, then tie it, if a hot abscess does not occur.
If however a hot abscess occurs, then leave the binding until the seventh day. And place upon it cut wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until when the abscess subsides; then tie it: and the manner of tying it is, that you place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster. Then bind the arm to the support itself, and place his open hand on his shoulder.
And place a cloth, and tie the arm to the support, so that the arm stands in the position of stars: if nothing prevents you from that, nor is anything disturbed on you from the broken bone.
But if you fear that something from the broken bone will be disturbed on you, then use splints: and it is that you place a splint over the fracture itself which is broader and stronger than the other splints. And place between each splint the width of a finger: and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
Then bind the splints with the bandage I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandage should be tighter over the place of the fracture. And the more the fracture is extended, the less tight the bandage should be.
If therefore you see the position of the splints and the bandage, as we said, at the time when you restore the limb immediately, then do so; and if you fear a hot abscess, then leave the bandage and the splints until the seventh day, as we said.
Then consider the ligament for all three days, lest itching or swelling occur in the place, or lest food be prevented from reaching the limb, owing to the disposition of excess from the bandage. Therefore, rectify all of it, according to what we said.
If however you are confident that nothing will happen from it: then do not loosen the bandage, unless after three days, or more.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and his hands on his stomach: and place under his assistance a mattress full of wool evenly.
And consider at every hour of the night or day that the shape of the fractured limb is not loosened or the bandage softened. Therefore, rectify all of that with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we have previously stated, so that there should be delicate food at first, until his mouth begins to tighten: then it is necessary to increase the thickness of his food.
For it is the custom of a swollen leg and foot to tighten in forty days: then, therefore, it is necessary to loosen it and give a bath and to treat it with ointments that rectify it.
If the fracture is foul, bruised: then do not loosen the bandage and splints from it until fifty days, and even until two months.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called two forearm bones.
One of which is small; and it is the one that follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is the one that is situated below the small one on the lower part.
Perhaps therefore only the larger forearm bone is broken, or the smaller one, and perhaps both are broken at the same time.
When therefore the smaller forearm bone is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the forearm bone is broken below, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken simultaneously.
If therefore the bone that is broken is the smaller forearm bone above, then it is fitting for the physician, in restoring it, to apply a small amount of extension with ease, until it is straightened.
If however the larger forearm bone is that which is broken, then it is necessary that he apply a much stronger bandage.
And it is necessary that he apply a hand-shaped bandage in restoring it, and extend its extension over a pillow. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers; and let the lower joint be lower than all the other fingers; and let the patient, sitting upright, be positioned squarely over himself, and let the pillow be before him, or in a direct line with him, in height, so that the patient does not incur fatigue.
Then let the attendant extend the arm from the lower part, either with his hand or with a bandage. And another attendant extend it from above similarly.
Then the physician should adjust the bone until he restores it to the best possible shape.
If there are fragments in the fractured bone, then strive to restore each fragment to its proper place with your skill.
If, however, a detached fragment appears in it, and it pierces the skin, and there is no hope for its restoration, then dissect over it and remove it according to the method we have mentioned in the previous ones.
If, however, the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a special chapter on it, therefore receive the treatment for it from there.
If, however, a hot abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then apply a bandage with a cerate made from rose oil, white wax, and of a consistency between thick and thin; and bind the bandage tightly over it until the abscess subsides; then remove the cerate, and apply a plaster prepared from mill-dust with egg white. Then place splints, and let the splint placed over the fracture itself be a little wider and stronger.
And know that the number of arm splints is six in most cases, whether the fracture is in one or two forearm bones simultaneously.
Then apply your binding over the fracture site more strongly and vigorously, and somewhat more tightly towards the upper or lower portion of the fracture; or apply a looser binding, slightly less tight than the first, as previously described at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths wrapped around the fracture be soft, damp; and not hard, rough. And let the thread with which it is tied be of linen, medium between fine and coarse, as we have described.
And consider the limb and the ligament after days. If anything should happen requiring correction, such as itching occurring to the limb, then it is necessary to bathe the limb with warm water until that itching subsides, and to leave the limb unbound until it subsides, then reapply the binding.
If the constriction has now softened, and the bone has now been removed, and similar things to these: then correct all of it with your skill, and consider it again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, because of the excess of the constriction, then it is necessary to soften it a little, and to leave it for some days, until nourishment flows to it, then bind it.
However, if nothing of what we have said happens to the sick person, then it is not necessary to loosen it, unless after twenty days, or about this number.
Then the sick person's hand should be suspended from his neck. And let his neck be even, and let it be guarded by his skill from violent movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All that is according to the dispositions of the complexions, and the dispositions of the virtue.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the Hand.
Know that fracture of the hand's ridges and the knots of the fingers rarely occurs. And it does not occur to it unless by very frequent rubbing.
Therefore, when a fracture or abrasion of the sole occurs, then it is necessary that the infirm person sit square, and there be a seat before him according to equality. Then place his hand upon it outstretched.
Then let the attendant extend the broken bones, and let the physician align them until when they are joined with a good juncture.
Then it is necessary that he place a plaster and tow, if a hot abscess does not occur. Then place a bandage over it according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
If however the fracture is towards the lower part, roughly the sole of the foot, then make a sphere out of cloth and order the sick person to press upon it with their fractured foot. Then tie it with a long cloth.
And let the bandage be made of leather, in which there is softness, so that the leather is applied to the sole of the foot; and bind it, as is necessary.
If however the fracture is to the outside, then it is necessary that you place a bandage over it, and another bandage from below on the sole; and let the hand be open and straight. Then apply a bandage, as the hand is turned; and stretch between the fingers with a ligament.
If a fracture should occur in any of the finger joints, then if it is the thumb, it should be made equal according to what is necessary. Then bind it to the sole; and if you wish, place a straight, small bandage on it, so that the fracture is raised and does not move.
If however the fracture is in the remaining fingers, such as the middle, and index, or ring, and little finger, then it should be made equal and bound to the healthy finger that follows it; or all should be bound according to their continuation; and it is better. Or place a small bandage on it, as we said for the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the disposition of the hot abscess itself: and apply with it what is necessary, when anything arises from it, according to the repetition of its account.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured hip.
The hip bone is often broken, and it is clear to the senses, since it is turned to the inner and posterior part.
And its restoration is done so that the ligament above the fracture, and the ligament below the fracture, are tightened. And the sick person lies prostrate on their face.
Then the attendant should extend each ligament on their side, equally; and this, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
If however the fracture is near the root of the hip, then let one ligament be on the side of the crest, so that the extension falls upward; and the other ligament below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, then let the ligament be near the knee, so that the extension is downwards.
Then the physician should align the bone with both hands, until he restores it to its natural shape, and the bone is joined with a good union.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and a bandage, if a wound does not occur in the limb. If however a wound occurs in it: then leave it for a few days, until the hot wound subsides, then return to your treatment.
However, its bandage should be like this. It is necessary to wrap the hard bandage, that is, the binding, over the fracture: and double it twice, or three times: and let some excess remain. Then wrap the leg, until the heel reaches the root of the buttocks.
And insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee, towards the lower part, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of the two sections. Then wrap the excess from the bandage over the leg and thigh.
Then place splints on the thigh, at the site of the fracture itself, and place one splint over the leg bone.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the tightness is equalized.
Then begin with a strong bandage from the middle, over the fracture site, in three or four wraps. And the further you extend the bandage from the fracture site, the less tight and softer and gentler your bandage should be.
Then pull on the two ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and leg. Tie then with both ends the three splints that are above. Then proceed with the two ends of the thread to the lower part, until you reach the ankle. And with both ends, also tighten the ends of the splints on the other side, so that the bandage does not move from its position.
Then release the constriction upon it, while it remains, so that no ulceration, nor abscess, nor swelling, and similar things, occur to the limb. If anything of that sort happens. Therefore, hasten with its solution, and correct whatever happened from it entirely, according to what we have often said.
If, however, there is a piece of bone in the bone that pierces: then it is necessary to smooth it, if it is possible for you. And if not, split upon it, and remove it, and treat it with what was previously mentioned, until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is bound, besides which splints are added to it, as we said in the help and the arm.
However, in its restoration thus fractured, lameness does not happen to the sick person. And if it is restored alone, besides which the other leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that he whose it is should limp always.
And know that the hip is bound for fifty days, or is somewhat added to, or is somewhat lessened: all that is according to the diversity of complexions, and of the other dispositions.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured kneecap.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap rarely occurs, but it often experiences attrition.
If, however, a fracture does occur, then it is only either a fissure or a hole in its parts. And it is both with a wound and without a wound. And it stands upon all of that with sensation.
And its restoration is such that the parts of it that are broken are aligned with the fingers, until they are brought together and joined as much as possible by means of alignment, ease, and making.
Then apply a plaster of sesame flour, or mill flour, with egg white: and put a round pad on top of it, if you need one: and bind tightly above it with an appropriate bandage.
Then consider all the treatments we have described in other fractures: like a hot abscess, and similar things: so that you oppose each accident with what is appropriate to it, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick and is called the Cruris bone, and the other thin, and is called the Focile bone.
And the same types of fracture occur in both, as occur in the two bones of the arm; and because of that, its restoration is done in the same way as the arm's restoration, and the operation is the same.
