If both bones are broken at the same time, the leg is twisted in all directions.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is twisted towards the front.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it lower; and it is from among those which are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and alignment, and the tying of splints according to that equally.
However, if the fracture of the leg is a foul fracture, having much bleeding, so that the extension should be less and your easing gentler.
And in the leg indeed there is something in the operation above the arm; and it is, that when you have aligned the splints and completed all your operation, then take two strips of pinewood, that is used in the making of rafters that are placed beneath planks; and are made of palm leaves; and choose from those which have a small thickness, and neither are from the smallest; and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.
Then wrap each one with two wraps according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side, and the other on the other side; and let them be from the knee down to the foot.
Then tie the two strips in three places, from the two ends and the middle. For when constriction is prohibited, the leg declines to the right and left, and stands in a good position.
And now indeed let a cradle be administered made of wood according to the length of the leg, and let the leg be placed in it, so that it may keep it from moving.
And very much indeed it is necessary that you do this in a fracture when there is a wound with it properly.
Then consider the leg every day, and strive with utmost care to treat it for an abscess, or swelling, or any other ailments. If therefore anything of those things happens, then apply to it what is necessary until it is healed, if God wills.
Of the fracture of the bones of the Foot and Toes.
A fracture of the heel bone indeed does not happen at all.
However, a fracture of the bones of the Foot sometimes happens, and a fracture also rarely happens to the Toes; and it does not happen to them except for attrition in many dispositions.
If a fracture of the bones of the Foot occurs, and you see those bones elevated above one another, then let the patient place his foot on prepared ground, as if walking.
Then stand you, and place your foot upon that which is elevated from those bones; afterward press, or tread it down; and make it even, until it returns to its place.
Then apply a plaster, and put linen over it; and place a small board beneath the sole of the foot, having two flat ends.
Then bind it with a proper bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths and whatever else it needs.
When therefore three or four days have passed, then loosen the bandage. For you will find the bones aligned, whether broken or dislocated. Therefore the operation on them is as you see.
If however some fingers are broken, then restore them and align them according to what I have told you about restoring the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint along the length of the finger, and let it be slightly wider than it; then place beneath the foot this board that I have told you about, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
If, however, what is broken of the bones is two, or three, or more, then place a splint of its appropriate size over each finger, wrapped in linen cloth; and bind the board to the lower part of the foot; and let it stand on one edge of the board outside the sole of the foot, so that it holds with good retention.
And it befits you, lest you forget, to consider all that I have told you in the other fractures from accidents that we have discussed; and counter every accident with what is appropriate.
Concerning the fracture of a woman's vulva, and the bone of the breastbone, and the penis of a man.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, then make her sit upright. Then incline her towards her own back a little, and let her be held back.
Then let the midwife fill her vulva with wool, until it fills it: and let it become in the vulva like a sphere.
Then let the woman be shaken, and raise her back gradually. For that wool will emerge, until it becomes like a sphere before the vulva's opening, and the fractured opening returns.
Then place a cushion on her back. Therefore, when she wishes to urinate, remove the wool with ease, then let her urinate: and return it according to the manner in which you first inserted it: then return to her original cushion.
Let him do this for seven days, or thereabouts, for the opening itself is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder, and tie a reed tube over its opening, and insert the whole bladder into her vulva. Then blow into the tube with force, until the bladder inflates within the vulva, for the rupture returns.
Then let it be filled with wool, and let it stand for several days, according to what we have narrated, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the comb of a man or woman is broken, then administer in its restoration and its equalization that which we have narrated concerning the bone of the hip.
And the rectum is not concealed in these nearby fractures, which occur rarely, especially in those who have some study or practice of it.
And understand this book of mine thoroughly, for the fracture itself signifies to you in many dispositions the right way in its restoration and its constriction: understand therefore.
But when a man's penis is broken, take the throat of a goose, and insert the penis into it. Then place upon it a bandage of cloth, and let it be wrapped and left for three days, or thereabouts, and it will heal, if God wills.
Concerning the restoration of fractured bones, when there is a wound.
It is appropriate for one who has suffered a fracture with a wound, especially if it is large, like the hip bone, or the auxiliary bone, or similar to it, that you hasten and immediately bleed him: if the conditions of bloodletting are witnessed to you, as we have previously mentioned.
If the wound flows blood, then it is necessary that you hasten to its excision, so that you pulverize ground zegi upon it: if another is not present to you.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day: and do not postpone it, if an abscess does not occur.
If, however, a hot abscess occurs, then postpone its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess is settled. And do not approach him on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause him to experience bad accidents.
If, however, the fractured bone is protruding above the skin, then it is necessary that you try to reduce it, and to make it level with your hands with ease and little extension.
If, however, its extension and its levelling with your hands are not prepared, then reduce it with this instrument. And it is an instrument made of iron, whose length is the amount of seven or eight fingers, and its width is according to the amount of the wound. And because of that, it is necessary for the physician to take from them three or four, according to the quantity he needs for the treatment in every kind of fracture.
And let it be round, and let there be a small thickness in it, so that it is not doubled under compression upon it at the time of the operation; and let it be sharp at the ends, with a margin at its ends. And let its upper part be at the thickness from its middle to the lower part.
And this is its form.
It is necessary in this operation that its sharp curved end be placed upon the projecting end of the bone, and to drive it with it in one turn, until when the bone returns and is made equal by some equalization.
Therefore, try to make the fractured ends even with each other.
If its fractured ends are thin, and it does not receive the instrument with good acceptance, then it is necessary that you cut the end of that bone, until the instrument has power over it.
If however you cannot reduce them with what we have narrated entirely, then cut it with what is suitable for it from the incisions we have mentioned, or saw it with one of the saws in any way possible for you.
Then scrape away what remains in the bone of roughness and small splinters.
When therefore the bone is reduced, and the sick person finds intense pain harming him after its reduction, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Do therefore that. For you confer great aid to the sick person when your restoration of the bone is completed.
Then immerse a cloth in astringent, black wine. And properly if it is in summer. And do not place a roasted piece of meat on the wound, nor anything containing oil, so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption within it.
Then administer the splints at the hour of completion of your restoration of the bone. And leave the wound uncovered; thus, cut with scissors an opening in the dressings according to the size of the wound.
And beware with all caution, lest you bind the wound with the fracture. For many times foolish physicians have done this, and have caused their patients either death, or decay, or putrid matter, that is, pus. And let your bandage be soft, gentle, different from the bandage of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or is a large wound, and you fear some bad accident, which we have described, and find pain in the suspended area: then you should not place splints upon it. And make them bandages of hard cloths in place of splints, and bind it with them.
When however it is after one day, or two days, and you see the wound beginning to generate pus: then remove the cloth that you placed over it with wine. Then apply bandages and ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds, such as tetrapharmacon ointment and similar ones.
And it is suitable for you to loosen the bandage. And consider the wound every day morning and evening, until it is consolidated and healed.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that pus runs from it downwards with ease.
If many days pass over the wound, and it is not consolidated, nor is pus separated from it: then know that there are small bone splinters there. Therefore it is necessary to examine the wound with probes. Remove any of those splinters that are separated, and extract them. And those of them that are not separated, and pierce the limb, and cause pain to occur, try to cut them and remove them by any means possible to you.
If a wound suffers from a zuchen, or ekileten, that is, festering, or some other form of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary to apply to each of them the appropriate remedy from the treatment, of which the account precedes in its chapter.
And in that which my discourse must dwell upon, and to which you must direct your mind when a bone is fractured and protrudes from the limb, such as the hip bone, and the arm bone, and similar large bones, do not presume to pull it back, for often death results from that; rather, leave it until it becomes rotten.
Perhaps it will fall out by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then, if it seems good to you, treat the wound; and if not, leave it.
Concerning the treatment of Swelling at the site of a fracture.
This swelling often occurs at the site of healing from a fracture. Especially if it is near the joints, and it disfigures the shape of the limb. And perhaps it hinders the limb from its natural function.
Therefore, if the swelling is recent, apply remedies that have astringent properties, such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, sarcocolla, acacia, and similar ones.
So that you take some, or all, of these, and mix them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar, and place them over the swelling on a bandage, and bind them firmly over it.
And leave the bandage on, and do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it and apply another, until the swelling is removed.
Or bind a lead sheet over it properly. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the limbs.
However, if the swelling has already turned to stone, and is hard, and urgent need compels its removal: then cut upon it from above, and cut away the protruding excess; or scrape it with certain scrapers until it is removed; and treat it until it heals.
Concerning the treatment when it is restored, and the limb remains after that slender, not according to its nature.
When a broken bone is restored, and the limb remains after that slender, weak, then that is not so except for many reasons. One of which is, the multitude of loosening of the ligament and its tying not according to what is proper; or because of the excess of tightening of the ligaments, until the nourishment is prevented from flowing to the limb; or because of the multitude of excessive poulticing at an unsuitable time; or because of the scarcity of blood in the body of the weak person, and their weakness.
And the treatment of that is, to nourish the sick person, and to make their body soft, until the blood is multiplied in it. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and gladness upon him.
Then place pitch upon the limb: that the pitch may attract much nourishment to it. And diligently apply embrocation to it with lukewarm water, until the nourishment flows, and it returns to its natural form.
Concerning the treatment of fractured bones when they are restored crookedly.
When, after healing, a limb already restored shows crookedness, and a prominence of the fractured bone, or a knot: and its limb's shape is disfigured thereby, yet the limb is not hindered from its natural function: then it is not necessary to heed the opinion of one who thinks that the limb should be fractured again.
And many foolish physicians and bone-setters have already done so in our land.
And this operation is indeed very reprehensible, leading to death, or great fear, so that it is potentially lethal.
But if the lameness and knotting are recent, then it is necessary to bathe it with lukewarm water in which softening herbs have been boiled, such as marshmallow leaves and its root, and melilot, and similar ones. And a poultice should be made with a softening plaster, such as a well-made diachylon.
Or take the mucilage of marshmallow root and mix it with hen's fat or sesame oil, and plaster it with that.
Or take fat figs and grind them with pigeon dung, and similar medicinal substances which are said to lessen consolidation.
And sometimes the knotting is loosened with constant, ancient, and gentle rubbing, which is done with damp hands. And movement of the limb should be administered to every part at all hours.
If, however, twisting has already occurred, and it has become strong, and has turned into stone, and the necessity for its treatment with iron has arisen: then it is necessary that it be cut above it, and the continuity of the bone be loosened, and that what is superfluous from the knotting or bone be cut with fine incisions: and ease should be administered to it with care and diligence.
Concerning the treatment of dislocations.
Dislocation is the departure of any joint from its place. Therefore, it is prevented from its movement, and the shape of the limb is disfigured. And it causes severe pain and grievous injuries to the afflicted.
Therefore, when any dislocation occurs, it is necessary to hasten its restoration; nor should one delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed, and perhaps the reduction of the dislocation with it.
Therefore, it is not appropriate for it to be moved or stretched during the time of its inflammation. Because it often causes spasms and harmful pains.
But when that happens, then it is necessary to hasten to the phlebotomy of the sick person. Then let it be left alone until the inflammation subsides.
Then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced gently.
And every limb should be treated with that whose memory comes to its proper place.
I have indeed already arranged the chapters on dislocation, even as preceded in fractures, from the upper part of the body to its lower part.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws are seldom dislocated except rarely.
And dislocation of both occurs according to one of two ways. For either they are moved from their places by a small movement, and are softened a little. Or they are dislocated by a complete dislocation, until they are softened to the part of the chest, until the sick person's saliva runs, and cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is wrapped up with his speech.
When however its dislocation is small, then it returns in many ways by itself with little effort.
However, if the dislocation is complete, entire: then it is necessary that its reduction be administered quickly, and not be delayed at all; and it is so that the attendant holds the sick person's head; and the physician inserts the thumb of one of his hands into the root of the dislocated jawbone between his mouth, if the dislocation is from one side. Or let him insert two of his thumbs together, if the dislocation is from two sides. And the remaining fingers of his hands should be outside, with which he will balance.
And command the sick person to clean their jaws and to free them to eat in every direction. And the physician should balance the jaw, and push the jaw until it returns to its place.
If its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated at once, then administer vaporization with hot water and oil until the reduction of both becomes easy; nor delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
When therefore they return and are balanced, and the sick person's mouth is closed, and does not soften: then place cushions of cloths upon them, with cerot which is made from wax: and rose oil.
Then it should be bound with ease with soft bandages.
And let the sick person's sleep be on his back, and let his head be supported between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right and left; nor attempt, or be forced, to chew anything; rather, let his food be soft, to be drunk, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is bound; then let him eat what he wants, and let it be administered with ease.
Let him not exert himself over the operation of his mouth at eating and drinking, and yawning, until the jaw is bound, and healed.
If the reduction of the two jaws becomes difficult, when they are dislocated at any hour, and do not return to their places: then often fevers and constant sweating ensue; and perhaps the sick person's bowels are loosened, and perhaps he vomits pure bile.
Therefore, when you see this, know that it is destruction, and often the one who suffers this dies within ten days.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone, and the extremity of the humerus.
The collarbone indeed does not dislocate from the inner side, because of its continuity with the breastbone. And it sometimes dislocates outwards: and that is apparent to the senses.
And its restoration is that the sick person lie on their back, and extend their arms. Then grasp the place with a strong hand-squeeze, for it will return.
Then place a plaster and pads on it, and bind it tightly.
However, its extremities that follow the elbow, and continue with it, do not dislocate, except rarely. If, however, it dislocates some day, then it is necessary that it be reduced and straightened according to what we have said, and as is prepared for you.
Then place a plaster, and pads, and a bandage on it. And instruct the sick person to adhere to peace and quiet until healed.
And with this same treatment the humerus extremity is reduced when it is also separated from its place.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the Humerus.
Know that the Humerus is not dislocated except in three ways.
One of which is that it is dislocated to the lower side of the armpit. The second is that it is dislocated to the side of the chest. And perhaps it is dislocated to the upper part of the Humerus: and that happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated posteriorly, because of the scapula. And it is not dislocated anteriorly, because of the nerves.
And very often it is dislocated and exits downwards towards the armpit: especially in those whose flesh is scant. Because it emerges quickly in them, and enters quickly.
However, in those whose flesh is plentiful, it is the opposite of that, namely because it emerges with difficulty, and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some men through a blow or fall, and the humerus becomes inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is thought because of that, that it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, it is necessary that you test it until you stand on the certainty of it, and then proceed in its cure.
And dislocation is recognized when it is directed downwards towards the armpit: and distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a healthy humerus. For you will find a clear difference among them, and you will find a concavity in the head of the humerus, and under the armpit, at the touch, the head of the humerus is like an egg itself: and the sick person cannot bring their hand to their ear, nor move it in all motions.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest, or upwards, then you will find a clear touch, nor is it hidden.
And indeed, a simple reduction of this dislocation is accomplished when it is recent, or the patient is a child.
And its reduction is that the attendant raises his hand upwards. Then place your thumbs under his armpit, and lift the joint with strength upwards to its place: and the attendant should lift his hand and extend it upwards. Then he should place it below, for it quickly returns.
If however it does not return by what we have said, and there have been many days since the dislocation occurred, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water, and an embrocation administered which softens and soothes, such as if the roots of marshmallow, and fenugreek, and melilot are boiled in water, and administered.
Then the patient should be placed on his back: and a sphere and wool should be placed under his armpit, which should be equal between softness and tightness.
Then the physician should place his heel upon the sphere, and lift the shoulder head with strength, and pull the patient's hand downwards.
And if you wish, reduce him according to this method: and it is that you should have a man present who is taller than the patient, and he should stand on the side, and insert his shoulder under the patient's armpit: and lift his armpit upwards, until the patient is suspended in the air. And another attendant should pull the patient's hand downwards towards his abdomen. If however the patient is light, then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him, so that it weighs him down; for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced according to another method: and it is that a long piece of wood should be fixed in the ground, whose head should be round in shape, like the sleeve of a mortar; which should not be thick, nor thin. Then, after soft cloths are placed on the head of the wood, the patient should be placed underneath; and the patient should be standing along the length of the wood.
Then, let his hand be extended downwards on one side, and let his body be extended on the other side with strength; for the joint returns to its place with speed.
If its reduction is difficult through all that we have said, then use this treatment: and it is that you should take a piece of wood whose length is the amount of two arms, and its width the amount of four fingers, and its thickness the amount of two fingers; to which there should be a round head, so that its entry into the depth of the hip is eased, according to this shape.
Then soft cloths are bound over the round head, so that the wood does not injure the patient.
Then place the wood under the patient's hip, so that the hand or arm is extended downward onto the wood; and tie the wood to the support, arm, and hand.
Then place the arm over a step of a ladder across its width, and extend the hand downward, and let the rest of the body hang suspended from the other side. The joint immediately enters [its proper position].
When, therefore, its entry is completed in whatever way possible, then it is necessary that you place under the hip a sphere of equal size made of wool. Then place the prepared plaster, made from mill dust with frankincense and egg white, over the entire shoulder, as it revolves from above.
Then bind a decent bandage under the hip, and let the bandage revolve over the plaster from above, and bind his hand to his neck, and let it hang, and let his hand not be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that little food be given to the sick person, until the limb is strengthened; because this is quicker for its healing.
Then a larger one is to be applied after seven days, or five, and it is to be exercised with movement. If therefore it is strengthened and does not soften, then it is already healed.
If, however, the joint is dislocated many times, because of the moisture that happens to it, or because of another cause, then it is necessary that a cautery with three holes be administered to it, according to what has been said in the chapter on cautery.
If all this is done, and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the joint is not strengthened, and the plaster and constriction are applied to it repeatedly, and it is not strengthened, and it falls, and cannot be raised upward: then know that its nerves that are to the head of the shoulder, are already cut, or stretched, or softened: and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
The reduction of a dislocation that occurs towards the breast and nipple, or to the back, is done with pushing and pulling with the hands, until it returns, and the remaining constriction and treatment are administered to it until it is healed.
If, after healing, heaviness occurs in the limb, and slowness in its movement, then let the sick person use the bath repeatedly, until that heaviness is eased, and it returns to its original nature.
On the treatment of elbow dislocation.
Know that the elbow joint dislocates with difficulty, and similarly returns with difficulty; and it dislocates indeed to all sides, and especially to the front or to the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you, for it lies under the sight and under the touch, whatever form it may dislocate into. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the sound one, it is clearly shown to you. For the joint hollows out, and the arm cannot double itself nor touch its shoulder with it.
And it is necessary that you hasten and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot abscess occurs to it.
For if a hot abscess occurs to it, its reduction becomes difficult, and perhaps it is not completely healed, especially when it is towards the back. For it is worse than all other types of dislocation, and causes more intense pain to them, and often death descends with it.
And its restoration, when it is of those that can be reduced, is that his hand should be extended with two hands, and his arm be stretched out, and the hands of the physician be above and below the elbow, and he should push the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the base of his palms, until it returns to its place.
Or truly the dislocation is to the front: then it returns so that he doubles his hand with his hand, until he strikes with the base of his palm the elbow which is dislocated.
If the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer a vigorous, very strong extension.  It is so that two assistants should extend the arm, and two assistants also hold it so that it is not moved during the extension.
Then the arm should be revolved in every direction after a long doubled cloth, or a broad bandage, is wrapped around his hand.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, he should anoint it with oil, so that it be of those things which aid in the lubrication of the joint with ease. Then he should drive the joint with a forceful impulse until it returns.
And after its reduction, it is necessary to place a plaster on it, containing astringency and desiccation with egg white; and a suitable bandage should be tied. And let the arm be suspended from the patient's neck, and leave it for a few days.
Then it should be released. If therefore the joint is made firm in its place, then loosen the bandage from it, and leave it.
And if you see the joint not being properly bandaged, then reapply the plaster and bandage, and leave it for a few days, until it is properly bandaged. Then loosen it.
However, if after the reduction of the joint, heaviness occurs and its condition is in motion, then apply moistening in bathing, and gentle friction, and compression, until it is lined, or place on the joint the fat of a ram's rump. Then it should be bandaged, and leave it over it day and night. Then remove it, and immerse him in a bath. When, therefore, he sweats, then rub the joint with moderate friction. Then apply it again in one, and in a second, and in a third place using the ointment, upon entering the bath, until it is relieved.
And if you wish, place upon it a poultice of vaccine, moist, heated with butter, and bind it over it.
Do that several times. For it is thus soothed, and returns to its proper state.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of Almahassem.
The joint between the arm and the hand, the Mahassem, is often dislocated.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. Unlike other joints. However, speed is necessary in reducing its dislocation at the time of its happening, before the area becomes infected; or a hot abscess arises there.
And the reduction of the dislocation is to place the weak hand's Mahassem on a table, and for the attendant to extend his hand; and for the physician to place his palm on the prominence of the joint, and to push it, until it returns.
However, it is necessary to consider, if the palm has already receded to the back of the hand; then the invalid should place the back of his hand on the table at the extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding to the back of the hand, then let his hand's position be inside on the table, that is, the inside of his hand be placed on the table, so that the physician's hand falls upon the very prominence of the joint.
If, therefore, it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then bind it with a soothing plaster for the abscess: and leave it, and do not repeat it.
For if it does not tolerate, nor can you effect its reduction, after the days have passed, the joint nonetheless remains according to its twist: and it does not harm the invalid anything, except that the hand is softened and cannot grasp anything; then know that the nerves are severed, or bruised.
Therefore, there is no remedy in it, except that it be cauterized near its location. And perhaps that will not help anything.
When therefore the Almahassem does not return, then place upon it the plaster which we have described. Then let it be bound, and let it be left for five days; afterward, let it be unbound and left, and let the hand be exercised.
If however its movement is deprived, and something serious occurs in it, then gently apply warm water and friction several times, until it is soothed.
Concerning the treatment of dislocated fingers.
When a finger is dislocated in any direction, or from its position towards the back of the palm, or the front of it, then extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns to its place.
Then support the finger's head and suspend it towards the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until it is made straight on its own day.
Therefore, when it is night, bind it according to the same narration: and do not cease to loosen it by day: and exercise it with movement: and bind it by night.
Do that for a few days, until it is strengthened.
And similarly do this with it if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand: and bind it to the part itself, and perform your first operation upon it until it is healed.
And similarly do this with it when it is dislocated to the other parts.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the spinal column.
When it happens to someone that the spinal column, or the neck, has a complete dislocation, or many vertebrae are removed from their places: then there is no cure for it, because death hastens upon the sick person.
And a sign of it is that the excretion of the sick person comes out without will, and he cannot hold it; and many times some of his limbs are softened, or his two legs, or his two arms, or one of them; or one vertebra is removed from its place.
For often it is removed, which happens often in their removal to four parts.
Therefore that which is removed to the back is called Gibbosity.
However, the treatment for it is that you should consider then, if the gibbosity occurs from childhood: then there is no treatment for it, nor is there any healing from it at all.
However, in that which occurs from a fall, or a blow, or similar things to them, the ancients have already spoken of methods of treatment in lengthy language. Of which much does not return with profit.
However, I have already abbreviated from that, of which little excuses itself from much of it from my proximity to the intention: and by my exposition, in which I have exposed it, and I have formed its instrument in a different manner from that in which they have shown it and exposed it.
I say therefore, that in gibbosity which occurs on the anterior part, there is no remedy, nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which is made to both sides also.
And it is not cured from them, unless that which occurs in the back properly, with that which I narrate: and it is, that the infirm person be extended upon their face upon an even raised platform near the wall: and that damp straw be spread beneath them, so that their chest is not harmed.
Then let a straight piece of wood, fixed in a hole in the ground, be placed at the end of the platform, approximately at the level of his head; and another piece of wood, approximately at the level of his feet, at the other end of the platform; and let an attendant hold the wood, and it not be firmly fixed in the hole. And another attendant hold the other.
Then wrap around the head of the infirm person, and under his armpits, a soft, firm bandage: and extend the end of the bandage to the wood which is at his head: and tie it to it.
Then tighten another bandage over his armpits, and over his knees, and at his heels. Then gather all the bandages together, and tie them to the other piece of wood which is at his feet.
Then let each attendant extend the bandage to a part of the wood, and do not remove the two pieces of wood from their places where they are fixed; however, let them not be firmly secured, as we have said.
And the physician shall place his palms upon the vertebra with force, until it returns; or he shall place a board upon it. Then he ties his feet to the board until it returns.
If, however, it does not return by this treatment, then take a board whose length is approximately three arms' length. And hollow out a space in the wall of which we spoke, which is near the sick person, into which the end of the board may enter. Then place the middle of the board upon the vertebra. And let the physician place his feet upon the other end. And let him pull tightly, until the vertebra is narrowed, and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, work with a turning tool held in your hand: and it is as if you were to fix two pieces of wood, each the length of one arm, in the ground near the head of the sick person at the rear of the platform; and two pieces of wood near his head at the rear of the platform.
And let the distance between each piece of wood be one palm; and now a hole has been made in each place for the turning tool to pass through; and let each piece of wood be firmly fixed in the ground so that it does not move at all.
And let a round piece of wood be inserted; and the turning tool rotates the ligament through the two holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its end is a hole in which a piece of wood is fixed, whose length is a palm together with what is turning; and in the other two pieces of wood there is similarly one of these.
Then tighten the ligatures that you have tied around the chest of the sick person with the turning tool that is at his head; and the ligatures that you have tied around his legs with the turning tool that is at his feet.
Then let a servant stand by each turning tool, who shall turn his hand with the cord by which the turning tool is rotated; and let the physician adjust the Spondylus, according to what we have said.
This is the form of the turning tool, which follows.
Then, after the Spondylus returns, and the place is leveled, then it is necessary to apply a drying plaster with egg white. Then, a piece of linen. Afterwards, place on top of the plaster a small plank from a board, whose width is three fingers, or approximately so. And its length the quantity that covers the hump, and over the healthy part of the Spondylus.
Then bind it with the bandage we have described. And let the sick person use light nourishment until he is healed.
If something of prominence remains in the place at the end of healing, then it is necessary that medicines which soften and soothe be administered, with the use of the board which we have described for a long time. And now a lead plate is administered on it.
And sometimes a prominence occurs in the final part of the spine of the back, and a dislocation is suspected because of it, and that is the bone that is now prominent. Therefore it is not appropriate to use this medicine with such a presumption, for perhaps it will cause death.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated Ancha.
Know that dislocation of the Ancha joint, and the shoulder joint, does not occur except in a complete dislocation; nor does it happen to them what happens to other joints from a slight displacement and concavity.
And indeed the Ancha joint dislocates according to four parts. That is, because it is dislocated forward and backward.
And most often it is dislocated inward; and little is it dislocated forward and backward.
And the treatment for its dislocation inward is this: because when you join the healthy leg of the weak person with the unhealthy one, it is longer; and the knee of the weak person is more prominent than the healthy one, and the weak person cannot bend his foot at the groin; and there is a place that follows the groin suppurated with a hot abscess, because the head of the thigh has reached there.
And the sign of someone who has experienced dislocation outward is that the symptoms are the opposite of these symptoms.
And the sign of someone who has experienced dislocation forward is that the healthy leg extends to its full extent; however, he does not bend it without pain in the knee; and if he attempts to walk, he cannot step forward; and his urine is retained, and his groin becomes suppurated, and during walking, his heel is on the ground.
And the sign of someone who has experienced dislocation backward is that they do not extend the knee, nor can they bend it before bending the groin; and their leg is also shorter than the other; and the groin is softened, and the head of the thigh is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction, however, of this type of dislocation is to consider, if the dislocation is old, that it has become established in the affected person. Then, if you administer its reduction, and it remains in its position: then there is no cure for it at all. Therefore, it is not necessary to make a presumption of it entirely.
However, in the case of someone whose dislocation is recent, and is of one of the four types of dislocation: then hasten to attempt to move the joint, and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it right and left; for perhaps it returns and does not need further treatment.
If, however, it does not return in this way, then it is necessary to prepare a strong assistant, and to extend the leg from the lower part: either with his hands, or with a bandage, which is tied above the leg, above the knee; and another assistant extends his hand from above, so that he introduces his hands under the shin. Then he should tie with a gentle bandage over the root of the thigh, and hold the other end of the bandage.
And let its extension be either from the anterior part of the groin to the part of the crotch, or from the posterior to the back part.
And let the extension of all of them be simultaneous, until the sick person is lifted from the ground with his body, and remains hanging.
And this type of extension is a common type among the four types.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for every type to do what I will say about its own cure.
In the proper reduction of it when the dislocation is inward, it is necessary that the sick person be laid on his healthy side. Then he should place a bandage over the root of the thigh, in that which is between the head of the thigh and the place which is under the groin. Then extend the bandage upwards from the groin to the higher part of the body, to the part of the pubic bone.
Then another strong attendant should take his arms, and lessen the bulky place of the sick person's thigh, and extend it outward with forceful extension. For now it returns to its place.
And this type is easier than the other types of treatment, by which this limb is reduced.
However, if it resists you and does not obey your instructions with this treatment at all: then it is necessary that the two feet of the sick person be bound together with a strong, soft bandage, above the two heels and above the two knees; and let the length of each be from its counterpart a quantity of four fingers; and let the sick leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then let the sick person be suspended above a wooden beam, which is in the house; and let its length from the ground be the quantity of two arms.
Then command a strong young man to support the head of the hip; and let another young man be suspended with the sick person; and let another young man, supporting the hip with strength, push, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
However, in its own return, when the dislocation is outward, it is necessary that the sick person lie on a raised platform, according to what we have described in the case of one with a hump. And let a bandage be tied around his sick leg, and around his chest.
Then let two pieces of wood be placed, one at his feet, and the other at his head. Then let a piece of wood be placed in the middle of the platform, securely fixed; over which let damp cloths be wrapped, so that it does not harm the sick person; and let the wood be between his hips, so that it is not pulled downwards on extension.
Then let each attendant pull in his direction, and the physician adjust the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it yields to the return, it will be good.
And if not, place it on the board itself and press it, just as we said in the case of the hump, evenly. However, it is necessary that the sick person lie on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that the sick person's leg be pulled quickly: and it is according to this same account on the platform: and let the physician place the palm of his right hand on the sick person's groin. Then grasp it with the other hand, and with that grasp place it extended downwards towards the knee.
And if the dislocation is to the rear, then it is not necessary that the sick person be extended downwards, and be raised above the ground; rather it is necessary that he be placed on a hard object, as it is necessary, as is also done if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we said about his lying on his two hips: and it is on his face, and ligaments extended, as we said recently.
And it is necessary that compression be applied with a board also to the place to which the joint emerges, when the reduction is completed as is necessary.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you: and it is that you extend the two legs of the sick person; and when you see them equal, and the sick person tightens his leg, and extends it without excuse, then know that the limb has now returned as it should; then join the two hips, and place a plaster, and bind with a bandage, that is, a roller; so that the hip is not moved to any part: and the sick person should adhere to rest for three, or four days.
Then loosen the bandage and the plaster, and compare the leg with the other; then if you see them equal in condition, then know that the dislocation is now firm; therefore dismiss the sick person to walk.
And if you see any softening, then return and plaster it, and bind it according to your first binding; and leave it also for three days.
Then loosen it and make him slow in walking on both [legs] for some days, until he is well strengthened.
Concerning the dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outward, inward, and downward, that is to say backward.
And it is not dislocated forward.
And a sign of its dislocation is that you should instruct the sick person to apply their leg to their thigh. If however it does not adhere to the thigh: then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all the ways of its dislocation is that you should make the sick person sit upright, so that he/she may extend his/her leg if possible upon it, and adhere your back to his/her face, and place his/her foot between your feet.
Then make his/her knee adhere with your palms, and straighten them between your fingers over his/her knee. Then apply with your palm the two sides of his/her knee with force. And another attendant should extend his/her foot, until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg will adhere to the thigh with ease, not with effort.
Then plaster it, and make the leg adhere to the thigh. Then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days. Then untie them.
And let the infirm person not use walking for several days, until he is healed.
If, however, its reduction is refused to you, then administer a strong extension with bandages, whose description preceded in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the heel.
The heel sometimes is removed by a small displacement, and sometimes is dislocated completely. And its dislocation occurs either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen, extending towards the side to which it is dislocated.
The treatment of its removal is easy to restore: and it is to be extended with ease with the hands, and aligned until it returns.
In its treatment, however, when it is dislocated completely, it is necessary that you make the infirm person sit upright: and let a strong minister hold him firmly from the back of his back, in the middle of it.
Then hold with your right hand his foot from its upper part, and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot at the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with the left two times.
Then extend with the left, and propel the front of the foot with the right towards the leg without effort.
Do that twice, as we have narrated.
Then propel the front of the foot to the leg in the third turn, and you pull the heel.
If therefore it returns in one turn, or in two according to this method, and you see the foot straightened, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation upon it, because it returns.
If however, the reduction of it with what we have narrated is prevented, then make the sick person lie on his back on the ground, and firmly fix a peg in the ground, so that it falls between his thighs; and you have now wrapped a cloth around him, so that it does not harm the sick person.
Then let the attendant take his thigh; afterward let another attendant extend the foot, either with his hands, or with a bandage that is tied around the ankle of the foot. Then let every attendant pull against the pulling of his counterpart; and let the peg stand between the two thighs of the sick person, holding him, so that his body is not pulled downwards during the extension.
Then let the physician straighten the dislocation with his hands, and let another attendant hold the healthy leg downwards. For the dislocation returns with speed.
Therefore, when the dislocation returns, and its return is declared to you, then place a plaster and lint, and bind it firmly with bandages, and tie the foot with a bandage to the leg.
And it is necessary that you beware or fear of the nerves that are above the posterior heel, so that the bandage is not too tight upon them and injures them.
Then leave it for two days, or three. If the bandage softens, tighten it; then loosen it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the sick person from walking for forty days. If however he attempts to walk before that time, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not dissolve upon him, and corrupt, and not receive a cure after that.
If however a hot abscess occurs to him, then it is necessary that you administer to its abatement that which our previous account in another place has stated in relation to the treatment, and an embrocation, until it is removed.
Concerning the dislocation of the toes of the foot, and its treatment.
It is necessary that what is dislocated from them be straightened with slight extension without effort; and indeed this is not difficult, but rather easy.
If however the dislocation is in some of the dorsal knots of the foot, then it is necessary that the afflicted person place his foot on a level ground, or on a board; and he is standing as if walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot on that which projects from those joints. Then press it with your foot with force, until it returns and you see it now straightened, and no protrusion appears in the place.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot, which takes the whole foot, having two ends. Then bind it with a suitable bandage for three days. Then unbind it, and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is bound and secured from reduction.
Concerning types of dislocation with wound or fracture.
When something of this sort occurs, and you attempt its treatment and restoration, often death ensues.  Therefore, it is not proper for anyone to undertake such treatment unless he is prudent in the long practice of the art, merciful, pious, slow, not rash, nor presumptuous; and that he employ, in the beginning, medicines which calm only hot abscesses; and that he leave the sick person to divine judgment. Unless you see, or hope for, salvation from destruction with a lessening of the illness, and in which something of hope appears. Therefore, attempt its reduction immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot abscess occurs.
If, therefore, the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regimen that calms the abscess, and treat the wound with whatever suitable drying ointments.
If, however, the dislocation is with a fracture, and separate bone protrusions occur, then attempt their removal, and do in that case what we have said in simple illnesses, from what has preceded concerning their places.
And beware according to your ability, and guard yourself from entering the path of fear, according to what my previous teaching was to you.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and more safe for your body among those in your time.
